Red jowl’d and creeping close at Summer’s heels,

r rom out the stricken earth and quivering air
but overstrains the metaphor.

enas
A crystal bowl she holds within her hands,
killed with the juice that banishes regret,
The ichor of the month made eloqu. nt.
And Syd C., by lack of compactness in his
sestette, spoils this pleasant beginning:—
The creeks are up, and rising rivers pour,
Brown and tumultuous, through the fragrant earth,
kast filming green, to meet the virile mirth
Of waves that shout full-throated on the shore.
Ihe lissom saplings, quickened to the core,
Llisten bronze-crested with the new leaves’ birth
And bees, gainst coming time of transient dearth!
krom knots of bloom fast overfin their store.
Five sonnets survive the test of poetic
quality. Marie E. J. Pitt uses a wrong rime-
scheme for an octette that is followed by
these fine lines:—
Why cease the dancers? Trails the slackened bow
On strings that fired their flying feet of old,
And all their laughter dies ; with laggard tread
On mourning uplands flushed with windy red
The young year moves, among her braided gold
A silver strand, the first faU of the snow.
And then there were four: Nina Murdoch,
Helen Power, Mary A. K. Robb, A. Gore-!
Jones all women. Nina Murdoch, as usual,
supplies the most characteristically Austra-
lian pageant; but her sestette looks too much
like patch-work.
On the lush flats and reedy swamps again
1 lie slender ibis goes upon its way.
The freshened creelcs that swell the rivers
sway ’
With hyacinth set moving by the rain
That snlashed the faded scrubland with a
stain
Of vivid green. And, through the humid
day,
The steam arises of the leaves’ decay
To tell of Summer’s glory on the wane.
For she has served her purvose, and the year
Puts her aside. So statelily she goes
That none would guess what passionate tears
she shed
To find her charm grown colorless and sere.
November led her from a sapling close ,
Avril will find the splendid Summer dead!
Nina Murdoch.
And then there were three. A. Gore-Jones
admirably develops an interesting idea, lack-
ing, however, a fit clothing of fine imagery.
“Windy steeds” unfortunately suggests
“winded steeds.”
The haggard witch is here with wild green
eyes,
And wan face swept by toosing, tawny hair,
A ragged mist through which her limbs show
bare;
As mounted on her windy steeds she flies
Across the hills; but though around her rise
A rich fecundity beyond compare,
Who knows what warrant yet her moods de-
clare !
The-air is troublous, stirred with stormy
sighs.
Turbulent March! Mach year young Autumn
lays
Upon your altar all her early store,
And eager eyed awaits her due reward,
The purpled grape, the slowly ripening
maize.
Will you fruition bring, or on them pour
The hail’s white whips, the lightning’s flam-
ing sword?
A. Goke-Jones.
And then there were two. Each of these son-
nets is of high poetic quality, and each-gains
its effects by the use of suggestion rather
than by the more prosaic method of direct
statement. In each March is implied with-
out being named. Each has faults. Helen
Power’s “The beneficent rising” needs an
effort to scan, and the quickening of the
soeed of the line, that would allow this lib-
erty, is not called for by the sense. “The
solemn rising of the harvest moon,” perhaps.
Mary A. K. Robb’s “gold grain’s store” is
not obnoxious, since it is meant to retard a
line expressive of slowness; but her
“Autumn’s heart a-throb upon the strings”
is too strained an image.
The prize goes to Mary A. K. Robb, 21
Prospect Hill-road, Camberwell, Victoria,
because her sonnet suggests an Australian
landscape, whereas Helen Power’s, through
no fault of her own, suggests a Tasmanian
and an English landscape. The latter son-
neteer writes:—
I am sending a sonnet for the March competition,
but without much anticipation of its being favorably
considered since I know of your rigid rule as to
local coloring.”
Nevertheless I must, in justification of my little
sonnet, remind you that I live in Tasmania, not in
Australia, and that the features of the landscape
therein mentioned are all visible from the window
where the poem was written. Nothing could be
much less like an Australian autumn than the one
we know here, especially in the south of the island,
and m a place like this, thickly planted with Eng-
lish trees.
Tarnished, the first rich emerald of the year,
ISut gaudier blossoms than the Muring can
show
Make bright the borders, while the orchards
gloio
With wealth of hue in many a ruddy sphere,
lhe world keeps silence, lest it fail to hear
lhe long-drawn sigh that stirs the sentinel
row
Of poplar trees, when, wistfully and slow,
Their first leaf falling tells the end is near.
Dreams sweep the heart, like shadowy clouds
at noon
*lhat float across a field of ripened grain —
Much dreams as come to him who, lonely,
sees
The beneficent rising of the harvest moon,
And hears the undercurrent of earth’s pain
In the hushed whispers of the waiting trees.
Helen Powee.

BY MARY A. K. ROBB.
Soon Summer’s symphony will be no more:
1 was heralded, by bursts of melody—
A sweet-voiced orchestra, unbatoned, free:
And, softer notes than these, that skyward
soar —
The glad, slow whisper through the gold
gram’s store:
The fretting of the stillness by a bee:
A twitter from a cradle in a tree:
And creeping waves caressing close the shore.
Methinks I catch a mellow, pulsing tone
Of swift, dark waters slipping, far away —
A :natter of slow drops from rain-drenched
things:
iJ l J ear a m ntter from wide spaces lone
Whence ghostly winds are sped, that vagrant
stray—
’Tis Autumn’s heart athrob upon the strings
Entries for the sonnet on our April close,
addressed to this page, on May 4.
Purple Mists, and How to Write
Them
The chief market for fiction in this coun-
mu I s ~ the subscription libraries.
-Lne bulk or the subscribers are women, and
women rarely read anything but fiction or
chronicles of scandal that masquerade sol-
emnly under the guise of historical me-
moires. \\ ith such a demand comes
the opportunity for the writers of feminine
of which Gertrude Page and F. E
Milis Young are the chief wholesalers.
Whenever a novel by either of these writers
is announced the sagacious managers of the
principal subscription libraries in this city
order hundreds of copies. Now, are there
any 1 /'! lst i raliari girls Wl ' it; ing fiction who
wouldn t like to have their novels ordered
on sight by the hundred copies for every
subscription library throughout the Empire?
AH you have to do is to write novels of
the Mills Young style. The wonder is that
you haven’t done it. There is no secret
about the Mills Young Method: the trick is
easy to any average writer who has no high,
foolish ideals of literature. Mills Young has
worked it briUiantiy and successfully for
bouth Africa. Why don’t our local Mills
Youngs apply her technique to Australia?
l\ot tor local consumption, for our women
library subscribers don’t want to read about
their own country; but there are all the
other fiction readers yearning to read about
any country but their own. One doubts
whether Mills Young has any considerable
sale m South Africa. Her troubles!
j*
Let us analyse the latest Mills Young—
I urple Mists (Lane, per Dymock’s
Library), and deduce the rules. Introduce
the Gn-l and the Man in the first line.
John Shaw first saw Euretta against the
flame-colored background of the sunset.”
John Shaw... .Euretta: the man and the
girl. Now you know what it’s all going to
be about. Add three lines about the sunset,
carefully keeping it in its place as a back-
cloth for Euretta. Curious name....easy
to remember... .suggests something exotic.
You’re right. “She was elemental.” All
women, even you, gentle reader, like to think
you’re elemental. Well, you can be Euretta.
“He saw the girl coming towards him over
the veld” (local color —in your Australian
version, it will be “boundless plain”; but
don’t linger over a detailed description: get
oii to the man and the girl) *“with fire be-
hind her and the wind in her streaming
hair....In her vivid youth she suggested to
him Life.” And when the man speaks, “his
voice, though harsh in tone, was cultured
and not ill-pleasing.” “ r l 1 'hen his glance
travelled rapidly to her left hand, which,
bare and unringed, hung looselv at her side.”
It’s all right: it’s not going to be a problem
novel. Room for more Euretta: “the small
head poised defiantly on the buoyant young
shoulders. Euretta was 21 and she pos-
sessed a figure as perfect as Aphrodite’s own
....He looked her over carefully... .This
man, for some unknown reason, influenced
her as no other man had done. In a sense
his mind dominated hers.” There, we' knew
it, he’s going to fall in love with her!
Next morning (next chapter) she had
been bathing, and he saw her, himself un-
seen, drying her long, black hair in the sun-
light.. Space' here for a slab of luscious
description: “She lay at her superb length
on the veld in the simplest attire, just a
white Turkish wrapper.... Her pretty feet
were naked, and the lovely, rounded arms
were bare, almost to the shoulders.... Shaw
stood for a while without moving a muscle,
and surveyed the picture. He had never
seen a more perfectly beautiful woman’s
form, even in a painting.”
How discreetly the thing is done! Had
he come earlier he would have seen her
naked —and that, for Mills Young’s clien-
tele, would have spoiled it all. And when
(chanter three) he says good-bye, “he held
her hand and looked straight into her eyes
with a steady, searching look.” He will
come hack, you may be sure. And when he
does “this woman with the figure of a god-
dess, and the dreamv, beautiful eyes, made
him tremble by her very nearness.... She
was delicious, this shy. wild, primitive young
thing, who was so nrtlesslv seductive, and so
unconsciously tantalising.” Ha« your young
man ever called you a shv. wild, primitive
young thing? And how delicious if he did !
Well, you can fancy that you're Euretta.
That’s what you nay your subscription to
the library for, anyway.
But it’s not all love-making, else there
wouldn’t be a novel. Action, please! He
marries her, and she in a blouse and skirt,
clean, of course, but hardly smart. But she
doesn’t love him: the woman in her has not
yet been awakened. For many chapters you
think it is a marriage in name only, so
discreetly does Mills Young keep you out-
side the bedroom door. Then, with a deli-
cious gasp, you hear of the coming baby.
You would never have suspected it. The
Purple Mists can be read by the nicest
people. And all the time “within her slum-
bered the possibility of a great' passion”
(There, you knew it!), “but the restriction
of her life had made her a prude.” Why,
you, yourself, have the slumbering possibility
of a great passion !
Then he takes her to Cape Town, where
he is a surgeon. And this primitive girl
from the veld at once becomes the best-
dressed woman in Cape Town. You see, she
has all the. talents. And when she looks
slightly fatigued, “the weariness enhanced
instead of lessening her charm.” Fortunate
one of her sex! Herein comes a passing
mention of the ‘‘purely physical” in their
relationship, but it is done with so delicate
a touch that no woman could take offence.
Add a paragraph of what the husband
thought the first time he saw her in evening
dress—this is essential. Then enter the
lover. “His type was Bvronic.” It always
is ; and it always has a “beautifully chiselled
mouth.”' But when lie stared at her. “she
inclined her heed ever so si : ah fly. and passed
hv.” But Mills Young bos deceived us.
This is merely a trifler. The real lover
enters.
Complications. Euretta leaves her home.
The lover pursues. Shaw had omitted to tell
his wife that he loves her. and she had over-
looked mentioning the fact that in three
months’ time they were going to have a
baby. But if either had spoken, what would
have happened to the novel? Mills Young
knows better. And Euretta is not a bad
woman. Circumstances compel her to go
with the lover, and he promises that she shall
be merely his housekeeper. Then for a
dramatic “curtain.” The baby is born —
with a crookpd some! And what more
natural than that Euretta should write to
her husband, who is the best surgeon in
South Africa, to come and cure their child.
And then what more satisfactory than that
Shaw should forgive his wife, and that “She
crept closer within his embrace and hid her
weeping face upon his breast”? For she
was “just a weak, heart-broken woman,”
and this was the first time that he had under-
stood her!
That’s all. But the title? Must have a
vague, tremulous title. “The Purple Mists?”
That’s it. But what has all this to do with
purple mists? Candidly, nothing. _ Let’s
write a paragraph at the end, in which the
lover is seen walking swiftly away “into
the purple mists,” and the thing’s done.
Now, all you women-writers of Australia,
what’s to prevent you from writing an Aus-
tralian Purple Mist? The chief caution
to beginners is the necessity to shut out real
life. Life is all right enough in life, but for
fiction suitable to woman readers life is
too shocking. What is wanted is a senti-
mental view of people. • Learn to look at
everything through the fiction reader’s eyes.
She doesn’t want facts: she has enough
washing-up to do in her own life: she goes
to her Purple Mists to get purple mists,
the more misty and the more purple the
better. Make your heroine the most beauti-
ful woman in Australia ; make her also a
shy, wild, primitive thing. Make your hero
the sort of being that you would like your
Bill to be, strong, stern, powerful, virile.
Give them the sort of love-affair that you
hope to have yourself some day. Marry
them, but not with orange-blossoms or the
Wedding March; and then bring about The
Misunderstanding that will wreck their
happiness for 17 chapters. The only way to
do this and retain your reader’s approbation
is to introduce the verbal misunderstanding.
This is the sort that happens only in novels
or on the stage. It consists of husband and
wife saying things that the other partner
always takes in the wrong way. It can be
cleverly « kept up for many chapters. In
real life, of course, if people love each other,
it matters not in the least what they say.
Love is a thing independent of verbal
quibbles. There is no divorce on record
which has been brought about by a verbal
misunderstanding between partners who
love each other. There must be a deeper
disagreement before a marriage is a failure.
But if you gave your hero and heroine that
fundamental incompatibility of tempera-
ments there would be no way in which you
could bring them together for your neces-
sary happy ending. So stick to the things
they say to each other, word them so that
they carry a double meaning to the hearer,
and make the hearer take the wrong mean-
ing.
Then introduce the Other Man. But it
is necessary, to. keep your reader’s sym-
pathy for the wife, to allow her to become
jealous of her husband. Of course, to
keep your reader’s sympathy for the hus-
band, he must be innocent too. The only
way for you to arrange things is for the
wife to be “too proud” to ask her husband
about the Other Woman, and thus to allow
him to explain the innocent facts, Other-
wise there would be no novel.
Then, in the last chapter, bring the inno-
cent husband and the more-sinned-against-
than-sinning wife together; and the inevit-
able happens. The husband doesn’t explain
about the fluffy-haired widow; the wife
doesn’t explain about the other man; he
just catches her in his strong arms and she
just lays her head on his stalwart shoulder.
and_ all is forgiven and everything is exactly
as if this terrible smash-up had never hap-
pened. If you like, you can mention that
they are going to begin again. And if you
produce a baby at this moment, or discreetly
hint at the possibility of a baby, the reader
closes the book with a happy sigh, and rushes
straight off to the librarian to ask for
another Purple Mist. And the librarian
promptly produces half a dozen just as sat-
isfying, just as misty, just as purply.
•
It is all the late Edward’s fault: if he
hadn’t died E. Coqngeau wouldn’t have
“essayed to mark the date of his decease by
writing a few lines in commemoration of
the event,” and “from that time forward ”
she wouldn’t have felt a desire to express
her thoughts in verse, “with the hope that
Queenslanders, no less than others, may see
beauty in everything God has made.” Well.
God made her, and she made this stanza in
the poem entitled “Le Roi Est Mort”:—
O Angels , waft him home!
0 Lord of Life and Death,
Thy will he done!
The King is dead!
And yet , he lives again! his son doth
Him succeed!
God hless his reign!
and we hope that Queenslanders, no less
than others, may see beauty in that. But
Stella Australis, by E. Coungeau (Gordon
and Gotch, Brisbane) isn’t all so horrible as
this early sample. True, she can actually
write acrostics upon the loss of the Titanic
and other subjects, and she had not refrained
from an “In Memoriam” on Captain Scott:
but, here and there, she writes pleasantlv of
pleasant things, without, of course, any need
to write at all. Her “Prose Fragments”
should be put through the crushing machine.
They are much too large and lumpy.

School Teachers Who Suffer from
Insomnia and Nervous Breakdown.
The following is a testimony to the nerve-restoring powers of
Clements Tonic. One of the most brain-racking of duties is that of
public-school teaching—correctly training “the young idea how to
shoot,” making pupils perfect by examination time —these great de-
partmental worries make nervous breakdown and insomnia common
ailments amongst the rank and file of public-school teachers. This
letter is from a lady who has been a brilliant example of her profes-
sion, having held the following high appointments in the Educational
Department of New South Wales
First Assistant Fort Street Model School for Girls.
Mistress, East St. Leonards School.
Assistant Mistress, Cleveland Street Public School.
Mistress, West Redfern Public School.
This testimony must appeal with commanding force to each mem-
ber of the Educational Department, showing what a nerve-soothing,
insomnia-killing, and health-restoring medicine they have in Clements
Tonic. All they need do is to try and persevere with it. The rest
and health_are certain. Miss CARDWELL writes from her residence,
“Coree.” 45 Booth Street, Annandale, 27/8/T3, thus: —
CLEMENTS TONIC LTD.
“I have great pleasure in giving my testimony to the benefits derived
from Clements Tonic as a builder up when in a run-down condition.
“When teaching in the State Schools of New South Wales, I many
times resorted to its use, taking one to three bottles in course.
“The ever-constant strain on the nerves of a Public School Teacher
soon brings on a low state of the whole system. In my case it was over-
strain in teaching, and mental worry through family bereavements. I had
been taking prescribed medicines, but seeing Clements Tonic advertised, I
tried one small bottle, and feeling so much benefit from its use, got the
larger size. It was such a restorative that, when I felt the least run-down,
I at once sent for Clements Tonic, and it never failed me. No household
should be without it, or fail to use it in any case of over-strain, for I feel
sure it will have the desired effect.”
I am, Sirs, gratefully yours,
A. M. CARDWELL,
CLEMENTS TONIC MAKES INSOMNIA A THING OF THE PAST.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Wooing.
"He’s been giving me black eyes for the past three
weeks.” “What has he been doing- that for?” “Be-
cause I don’t love him any more.”
—Sydney police-court report.
He gave me gifts ; my heart was cold,
He thought that he might fight his way
Into my heart with wooing bold ;
He gave me black eyes ev’ry day.
I loved him not for all the proof
He showed me in his own sweet way
Of how he loved ; I held aloof
In spite of black eyes ev’ry day.
Now in his cell my lover lies,
And, in my feeble woman’s way,
I think of him —and wipe red eves.
(He gave me black ones ev’ry day.)
N.s.w.
EVELYN NOCTABOUT.
The Forbidden Fruit.
Wanted a first-class orchard manager, married,
without children, or one child preferred. Must be
thoroughly competent in all departments.
Wanted a first-class orchard hand, married, with
children preferred. Must be thoroughly capable.
—Tasmanian advertisemen ts.
This is the law of supply and demand to
which all parents bow,
The married counle who take a job must
without encumbrance be ;
I*tit he who farms on shares such land as
grazes the butter cow
May have a full quiver of twenty-one, and
is scorned with two or three. •
For children on stations loaf and eat, >and
are black on the squatter’s list.
They are wealth to the orchard worker, but
a drug to the orchardist.
The orchard manager bloke may not, for
appearance sake, his son
Let work in the rows of leafless trees or
pick in the apple time,
But the orchard hand who, by love or luck
has a baker’s dozen won
May see them gathering scarlet fruit in the
autumn dew and rime—-
May see them welcomed while yet for them
there is not an award,
Welcome to earth and capital—while there is
no Wages Board.
The anxious unborn who press for life at the
painful gates of birth,
Who clamor for flesh, to the man and wife,
for the world in its joy and pain,
Are hereby advised to select a pa who’ll give
them a chance on earth,
Whose vocation will find them a help, and
whose labor will prove them a gain.
An orchard hand or a dairyman should be
first on the Fathers’ list,
And last shall be station managers—and the
One-Child orchardist.
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEOFORD.
A Strike of Loyalists.
n the lung of England signed the Bill and force
was used to coerce the Ulster loyalists, he for one
would never again sing the National Anthem. How
could he sing God Save the King” if his Majesty
gave his sanction to such an iniquitous measure?
(.iriear, near.)
Speaker at a Melbourne Orange meeting.
Never again ! Though he starve and die
hor the lack of praise we gave him.
Never again! Though he yearn and sigh
hor the strains of the old “Gor’ Save
Im.”
If a king will thus transgress our rules
We loyalists shall down our tools.
Wliat is the use of a king to us
VVho is dead against our notions—
Mocking our whims and our foibles thus
■.Tr'r nd - OUr “ lo y al ” Orange motions?
iV hat is the use of our loyalty
It a king won’t think the same as we?
Never again shall we doff the hat
To the strains of anthems royal;
n hen they lift their voices—(mostly flat)
And declare they still are loyal.
And the lack of howls in a minor key
Shall rack his soul with misery.
Not a word of ours shall praise his name,
I or he counts no more our cobber.
And a king who fails to play our game
Ain’t worth a single slobber.
Then let him reign in silence gi’im ;
This lack of praise will madden him.
Never again shall the rafters ring
With a midnight song uproarious,
Asking Heaven to save our king
And to send him great and glorious.
And Heaven, marking well the lapse,
Will cease its patronage, perhaps.
Then let him mope in his castle dim,
A king unsung, ungreeted ;
And yearn in vain for the loyal hymn
To which he once was treated.
And let him weep and cry in vain:
“Let’s hear ‘Gor’ Save’ just once again!”
Never again ! Though he plead with sobs
And on his knees doth sue us !
For kings who want to hold their jobs
Must be respectful to us.
If they ignore our little ways
Henceforth shall we withhold all praise.
Upon his throne in London toion
A king sat up and wondered.
The loyal howls he longed to drown
No more around him thundered.
“Nay, can it he we are so hlest?
Peace! Peace at last! Noiv may we rest A”
VICTORIA.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, Thu Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
betters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.

THURSDAY, APRIL 23, 1914.
THE COMPLETE STRANGER AND
THE COW.
Many years ago—and yet. perhaps, not so
very many years ago, the date being half-
way between the previous and the subsequent
—a man had a very great and rich estate
left to him. There wasn’t even a road on it,
but that sort of thing may happen to any
place. Scotland didn’t own a genuine road
150 years ago. The whole world didn’t
possess a road in the days when Exodus
was cutting its first tooth. All Australia
wasn’t gifted with a road till the other day.
There were no railways on the- new estate,
but that was nothing unusual. No railways
were heard or dreamt of anywhere until
yesterday or thereabouts. The man who
inherited the great, rich estate, in the time
that was half-way between the previous and
the subsequent, was no worse off than any-
body else, and a thousand times better off
than most people, inasmuch as he didn’t need
to pay for the rich area, and so far he hasn’t
required to defend his ownership of it. The
only reason why the place didn’t prosper
immediately was that he went about the
work of development in a curious fashion.
He took round by sea —and it was a very
long and circuitous sea-passage—a Complete
Stranger and a Cow. He marooned them on
the end of the property which was farthest
away from anywhere and anybody. Incident-
ally it was the worst section of the property.
The Cow was very sick on the voyage, and
the Complete Stranger, when he saw the
lonesomeness of the place he was going to,
said that even a fertile province might not
amount to much if it was like Robinson
Crusoe’s island in the matter of isolation.
After a number of years the owner of the
property started to investigate. He wanted
to hear something about development. And
finding that the Complete Stranger and the
Cow, left solitary and deserted on the shore
at the far end of nowhere, hadn’t blazed forth
into wild prosperity, he said that the estate
was a burden, a dead loss and a white
elephant.
* * * *
•That, put into the fewest possible words,
is the story of Australia’s Northern Terri-
tory. The Territory contains the biggest
tableland in the Commonwealth, and the
biggest area of land specially adapted, by cli-
mate, for fresh, raw, sturdy British immi-
grants. But even the freshest British immi-
grant on the tallest tableland doesn’t care
to grow wheat and such-like produce if he
has to cart it 1000 miles to a market, and in
a community which grows more wheat than
it can use the markets for new or extra
wheat are at the deep-sea ports. If the
Territory is left as it is—and the Common-
wealth Government hasn’t built even a yard
of railway in the cool highlands or anywhere
else, so the place is certainly being left as it
is—it will fill up with people when every
decently fertile section of Australia which
is blessed with railway communication has
been occupied, but probably not before. In
view of possible foreign complications it is
impossible to wait so long, and in order to
hurry up events the place requires to get the
same chances that were found necessary in
the case of Bankstown or Cobar or Longreach
or Mildura or Gawler or Kalgoorlie. The
Northern Territory Problem, about which so
much is said and written and blithered, is
in no way different from the Sydney Tele-
graph Problem or the Sydney Evening
News Problem. If there was no road, or
tram-line or railway to their offices, and the
papers couldn’t even be delivered at Strath-
field except by a long sea voyage, they would
probably remain as undeveloped as the pleas-
ant miles of grass in the vicinity of Alice
Springs. Even their literary excellence—
such as it is—wouldn’t prevail against the
isolation.
* * * *
The story of the Northern Territory is
the general story of this great country. Be-
fore railway building started, and the
feeling of lonesomeness was worn off
through one very small city being
connected with another that was per-
haps still smaller, advancement was
quite gradual. It took New South Wales
22 years from the beginning of things to
acquire 11,566 people, yet it had a compul-
sory population which was chained to the
spot and was flogged if it disapproved of the
climate. It took Westralia, also with a com-
pulsory population which was flogged if it
called the country a Problem, 42 years to
accumulate 1172 people. It took S.A. 52
years to become possessed of 14,630 inhabi-
tants. Queensland made its first separate
appearance 52 years after the British Em-
pire started in Australia, and then it had
only 28,056 people. Settlement, while roads
were few and railways were none, was
mostly on the coast and mostly on that part
of the coast which had direct communication
with the outside world. Sydney happened
to be the first official convict (or flogged)
settlement, so it became the end of an offi-
cial marine service from England via the
Cape of Good Hope. This included, in pass-
ing, the ports all the way round from Fre-
mantle. Also Sydney was handy for the
Chilian wheat and flour trade, and was the
natural terminus of the old mail line via
Panama. The coastline from Fremantle
round northerly to Cairns or thereabouts
was neglected because it was dreadfully diffi-
cult to navigate in the days of windjammers.
The loss of Pelsart’s Dutch armada in these
waters was a lamentable advertisement, and
scared navigators badly. The North lagged
behind in consequence. Still it didn’t lag
very far behind. So far as can be guessed
from geological records, it took Sydney and
Melbourne and Adelaide six billion years or
so to get as far advanced as Darwin or
Geraldton or Broome or Normanton are
now. The worst that can be said even of
Darwin, which is about the hottest and most
Chow-infested place in the Territory, is that,
in the procession of six million years or
thereabouts, it is a century later than Syd-
ney and half a century more dilatory than
Melbourne. It seems like undue haste to
call the Territory a problem and a white
elephant on such slender evidence.
* * * *
There has, of late, been a considerable de-
parture of officials from Darwin, the far, lone
end of the estate which the owner attempted
to settle by marooning a Complete Stranger
—generally a Chinaman —and a Cow at the
most remote tail of geography. It is no
great wonder that there is a departure. See-
ing that Australia, with less than two
people to the square mile, isn’t yet driven,
by lack of space, to be an Israelite in the
wilderness, it is impossible to settle an
empty patch by merely sending out officials.
Nobody even thought of settling Bankstown,
which is only a few miles from Sydney, by
despatching there a horse-doctor as Deputy-
Governor, an Inspector of Schools, a Lands
Board, a Protector of Aborigines, an Inspec-
tor of Mines, a Geologist, a High Panjan-
drum, a Pooh Bah, a Chief Medical Officer,
an Inspector of Lighthouses, a Government
Botanist, an Experimental Farm, a dredge,
an interpreter, a Judge, a Marine Board,
and a Very Great Chief Entomologist or
Beetle Investigator or Battler with White
Ants. All the Government did was to build
a railway and let Bankstown, which had
mouldered for 100 years right at the back-
door of Sydney, find itself. The Territory,
which has mouldered no longer than
that at the back-door of Adelaide and
Townsville, wants the same treatment
—no more and no less. Even if two
railways were finished —the one north-
ward from Port Augusta to Darwin, which
is nearly half constructed, and the one west-
ward from Townsville to the Westralian
coast, which is already constructed as far as
Cloncurry—the bill would be nothing remark-
able. The whole thing could be done for
less money than the amount of the loans that
the present N.S.W. Government fools away
in two years on works that seem to yield no
return whatever. The country is easy for
railway building. The increased land values
created by the railways would be almost en-
tirely Government profit. The opening up
of the Territory by a very ordinary line, or
series of lines, is in the same category with
the bridge between Sydney and North Syd-
ney, which is just an ordinary bridge. The
matter has been used as an election cry or
advertisement by politicians till it has come
bo look something enormous, incredible,
boundless, shoreless, sublime, and un-
precedented, and an idea has arisen
that the man who tackles it must
be a giant who has girded up his
loins, and buckled on his armor, and
put on his seven-league boots, and thrown'his
hat into the ring, and drawn his sword, and
cast away the scabbard, and generally re-
solved to perish on the spot. But nothing
of the sort is involved. A grocer
could tackle the work if he was an unimag-
inative grocer and not impressed by bogies.
Some years ago a very similar bogy existed
in France —the famous Humbert swindle.
It was the nearest thing in recent times to
the deep consideration of the Sydney Harbor
bridge and the tremendous responsibility of
the N. Territory. There was a safe which
must not be opened save under extraordinary
and complicated conditions, and these con-
ditions filled the law courts with clamor for
years. Inside the safe was allegedly a gigan-
tic mass of gilt-edged securities, on the
strength of which vast sums were advanced
by credulous capitalists. An impression pre-
vailed that the matter was so tangled that
it would involve centuries of litigation. Then
a cheap, ordinary French magistrate—the
kind of man who would simply build a North
Shore bridge or a Northern Territory rail-
way, instead of spending years in writing
about the subject—ordered the safe to be
opened, just as if he was fining a drunk,
and, as the iron box only contained 2d. and
a hairpin, or something of the sort, the
whole trouble ended.
* * * *
The moral which it is intended to convey
is that the Northern Territory is just a bit
of land. It is better than much of the land
of Australia, because it is cool and elevated
at the high end. which happens to be the
biggest bit of high country in Australia. It
is low and hot on the small coastal section,
but this may happen to any other place where
the coast is flat and tropical. The Territory
is dark at night and light in the day-time,
and it is wet when it rains and dry when it
doesn’t, these also being common and fre-
quent characteristics. There is no problem,
save that the Territory can no more be de-
veloped than any other place by marooning a
Complete. Stranger and a Cow at the far end
of it. All it requires is the some treatment
that the Sydney Government found necessary
in the case of Bankstown and other suburbs.
It has, however, this advantage, that the
railway will run almost entirely through
Government land, so that there will be no
costly resumptions, and the Government will
get practically all the unearned increment.
These facts make the problem much easier
than that of building the first two miles of
the North Shore (Sydney) line from Mil-
son’s Point.
THE VOTER WHO AROUSED
SUSPICION.
It is difficult to take much interest in the
charges of personation and double voting at
the recent N.S.W. State elections. The whole
thing seems shadowy and mixed. To begin
with, there is the fact that, in the early days
of the llolman-McGowen Ministry, a
foolish sunnorter was found to have been
straining the electoral law very considerably
in the llolman-McGowen interest. If the
law had been enforced he might have had a
bad .time, but the matter was brushed aside
as a case of “misguided enthusiasm,” or
something to that effect. An impression arose
then in badly-informed and illiterate quarters
that casual or even sloppy enthusiasm
might be overlooked if it tended to the suc-
cess of the Holman-McGowen combination.
Many politicians in office have also been will-
ing to overlook the enthusiasm of their sup-
porters, and many have done it, but very
few have talked about it. At the recent
State election the first ballot left things
fairly even between the Government and the
Opposition. But there were a dozen elec-
torates in which a second ballot was neces-
sary because, amid a multiplicity of candi-
dates, no one had obtained an absolute
majority. The two leading candiates had
to go to the poll again and fight the thing
out. In certain of these electorates the
various straggling Opposition representatives
had a good majority if their votes were com-
bined, and it was presumed that at the
second ballot these votes would be combined
to a great extent and would go to the lead-
ing candidate. Therefore Liberalism exalted
its tail exceedingly, and said that the battle
was won. It even opened a bottle of cham-
pagne and set fire to a big cigar. Nothing,
it reckoned, save a very remarkable reversal
of public opinion in a remarkably short time
could save the Great Loan Ministry.
* * * *
The reversal, or something, came with
sufficient energy and at a sufficient number
of places to save the Borrowing Govern-
ment and give it a good margin. At
Bingara the total vote increased from
6947 to 7220, an addition of 273 votes.
But the supporters of the selected Govern-
ment candidate increased by 394", so he ad-
vanced easily from second place to first. At
Enmore, the first ballot showed 8955 votes,
of which the leading Opposition candidate got
4458 ; the Government,. or Hard-Borrowing
candidate (who, by the way, is Attorney-
GeneraD, only 4365; and an outsider 129.
At the second ballot an extra 761 votes were
dug out from somewhere, and as practically
all of them went to the Government candi-
date, he became 754 in front instead of 90
behind. At Hurstville, the discovery _of
1226 new voters in the short space of time
between the first and second ballots shifted
the Government or lOU candidate from a
minority of 306 to a majority of 368. At
St. George the total vote grew in the few
days between the first and second ballots
from 9672 to 10.256, and the vote for the
Government candidate from 4366 to 5231, so
that, where he had been in a hopeless-looking
minority of 431, he rose all at once to the
giddy affluence of a majority of 206. At
Willoughby the total vote was 5942 at the
first ballot, and 9509 at the second. The
Liberal vote had been split between Flem-
ing and McMillan on the first occasion,
and, apparently, on the figures, went solidly
for Fleming on the second, for the Flem-
ing vote went up from 2777 to 4601. That
almost exactly accounted for McMillan’s
transferred supporters. But though there
was no superfluous Labor candidate at the
first ballot whose support would pass auto-
matically to the other Labor candidate at the
second, the vote for the representative of the
Deficit Ministry increased by 1103 (from
3805 to 4908) and a blighted Opposition
went home with its tail down and a wild
expression in its eye.
The party which opposes the Borrowing
Government of N.S.W. is a rather clumsy
and inarticulate creature, but what it seems
to be trying to say is this: (1) That, accord-
ing to the experience of other countries, the
vote at the second ballot is generally smaller
than at the first, and some of these increased
votes are so remarkable that they ought to
be dried and stuffed, and put in a museum.
Also Nielsen, the Labor Government’s sup-
porter who gets £4 a day for expenses, might
take them as a show item to the Panama
Exhibition. (2) That an extensive change
of nublic opinion in such a short time is rare,
and its happening shows the Finger of
Providence. (3) That the fact of there
being enough reversal to save the great
Indebted Government (which was in
charge of the election business) from hav-
ing again to depend on a precarious majority
of one—the majority which fell over the
stairway—clearly shows the Entire Hand of
Providence, also Both Feet. (4) That
(supposing it wasn’t a reversal of form) it
is remarkable that the Big Shortage Govern-
ment organisation—generally supposed to be
a pretty good organisation—missed so many
supporters at the first ballot, and the fact
suggests that it isn’t such a very good or-
ganisation after all. At the same time, the
fact that it rounded up so many of its neg-
lectful supporters between the dates of the
first and second ballots shows that it isn’t
such a hopeless organisation as some people
imagine. (5) That it is all a Staggering
Coincidence. That appears to be what the
Opposition is trying to say, or at least it is
understood to gesticulate and flap both hands
to that effect.
* * * *
Certain small, futile complaints have been
made about alleged impersonation and such-
like things, but they appear very useless and
purposeless. Certainly there are possibili-
ties of one sort and another. The elector’s
right, which served as a receipt or passport,
and could only be used once at one election,
by reason of the stamp that was put on it,
has been abolished. Now any man can go
into any polling booth, or anv number of
polling booths, and vote as anybody, or as a
lot of people, provided he isn’t recognised as
a personator and assuming that he gets in
ahead of the individual he is personating.
And if that individual is a chronic abstainer
from voting he may never know that his
vote has been used by some unauthorised
party. In fact, hundreds of him may never
know. The first ballot shows the chronic
absentees whose votes may be borrowed with
impunity, and .1 a few hundreds of these
votes are us. d ai the second ballot there
is very little in .he law to prevent such a
thing. All t iis 'a unfortunate, especially
in the case of :m election at which so many
unusual things happened. A careful inquest
or autopsy is n nut-red impossible. It is
understood that (he Borrowing Government
has put the police, controlled by itself, on
the job to find out whether it —the Borrow-
ing Government —happened to get its ma-
jority, or part thereof, by the devices of
misguided supporters who can’t stand inspec-
tion. But the police force is notoriously
overworked, underpaid and undermanned. It
is notoriously anxious for a rise. There are
probably two constables, or perhaps a bobby
and a half, at Willoughby. If there are two,
and they called on the 9509 electors who pur-
ported to vote at the second ballot —the one
which caused so much surprise—that would
make 4754 J calls apiece, even if everybody
happened to be in when called upon. To
each inhabitant the question would have to
be put whether the vote registered was genu-
ine or bogus. All this investigation being
obviously impossible, it is naturally inferred
that there wasn’t much research, on the
part of the Borrowing Government’s police
force, into the hints that the Borrowing Gov-
ernment’s success might have been due to
“misplaced enthusiasm” on the part of some-
body or another. It is feasible to suggest
the restoration of the old electoral right, but
apart from that the matter seems hardly
worth further investigation. Why struggle
with the intangible, and scuffle with ghosts
in a fog?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Cornstalk Stamp Duties.
Sydney and other places had a day of joy
when the Holman Government’s new stamp
duties came into force. The measure was
ruled out twice on objections raised by
Cohen, M.L.A., as being a botched sort of
affair and beyond the “order of leave.” The
man who ruled it out on Cohen’s applica-
tion was Speaker Richard Denis Meagher,
and some of the people who had helped to
put Richard Denis in the chair murmured,
and said it wasn’t the sort of thing they
had expected. The measure got through at
the third time of asking, but the capable and
level-headed Cann was in charge instead ot
the misfortunate Hoyle. There was some
trace of a hitch at the last moment through
the neglect of the Attorney-General to write
out the formal certificate, but the usual use-
ful Cann noticed the omission and had
things put right. So the Act started on the
pleasant Wednesday of last week, when the
sun had risen after a beautiful night or
stars, and the skies were blue, and life seemed


almost worth living despite the high price
of food and the low cost of burial. Mercan-
tile people, who are really the most ignorant
white folk going, fled to the Federal post
offices to buy the new receipt stamp, or the
new stamp that is gummed on to the cheque
—the latter being a bluish contraption with
a pink King looking eastward in the middle.
The Act was in force for a whole day before
a printed copy was available, so people who
had no sense fled in all manner of direc-
tions. The Federal post office considered
the applicants as if they were dead, and
said this was a purely State matter. Then
the public besieged the stationers’ shops, and
howled for stamps or blood. One big
stationery was only saved by the
police from being trodden under the faet
of the stamp-buying multitude. Country
towns telegraphed that their stocks had
given out, and held up a banner with the
strange device, “Stamps or Death.” Things
straightened out.as the day went on; still
on Thursday morning a Bulletin man
tried to buy ss. worth of the new goods at
a big city emporium, and only got Is. worth.
Meanwhile eminent lawyers put ice on their
bulging foreheads and proceeded to advise
their clients as to how they stood. Even the
leading botanists and zoologists of the coun-
try admitted that on some points the Act
was a trifle doubtful, and the curator of
the museum wasn’t absolutely sure. For
instance, the Act says that “receipt given for
or upon the payment of any sum of money
amounting to £2 or upwards” shall carry a
twopenny stamp. That seems to include
every kind of payment. Yet, according to
the S. M. Herald, “the Stamp Commis-
sioner regarded wages as different altogether
from salary, and while receipts for wages
were exempt those for salary must be
stamped.” If the Commissioner has a right
to make this differentiation on his own re-
sponsibility the question arises, what are
“wages” and what is “salary.” Also where
does “screw” come in; likewise “honora-
rium,” “fee,” “refresher,” “stipend,” “dirt
money,” “emolument” or “allowance”?
According to Webster’s dictionary “sal-
ary” is a recompense paid by the
year, half-year, quarter or month, while
“wages” are paid at shorter intervals.
With this view the Encyclopaedia Britan-
nica concurs. Another dictionary definition
is that “salary” stands for a higher grade of
work than “wages.” The editor of The
Bulletin collects his money weekly, the
simple reason being that he couldn’t
hang out for a whole month, and as
his employment consists largely in complete
disapprobation of the Holman Government,
it is, presumably, in the view of the Holman
Government, low-grade. Therefore his
weekly receipt will pass unstamped. A wild,
whirling circumstance in the Act is the
charge of 6d. on scrip “entitling any person
to become the proprietor of any share or part
of a share in any company or proposed
company.” It would be difficult for the
law to do otherwise than decide that this is
a tax of 6d. on each share, and when a new
company starts with the shares paid up to
Is. it looks like a rather considerable tax —
in fact, a 50 per cent. tax. A “letter or
power of attorney” for the receipt of any
sum not exceeding £2O is to carry a ss.
stamp, and if the sum to be received is only
10s. or £l, the tax looks considerable. There
are certain exemptions, but they don’t in-
clude wages, so plainly if Bill, laid up with
a broken leg, gives a written order to .Tim
to collect the 15s. which Thomas owes him
for tree-felling, the ss. stamp must be affixed
—or somebody is liable to fall in. There
are other visible points of interest, but these
are the principal ones. Quite a. lot of points
that are not yet visible will probably emerge
when the Act begins to struggle with legal
artificers in the law courts.

The Dark Coast.
Dear Bulletin, —The Australian Mercan-
tile Marine is demanding in a voice whose
thunderous tone is torn between rage and
nervousness when the recommendations of
Commander Brewis in regard to the light-
ing of the coast are to be carried out. It
was intended originally that the control of
the lighthouses should pass from the States
to the Commonwealth in July last. How-
ever, the States were asked by the Cook
crowd to attend to the job till the end of
1913, by which time the CooK-ites, ever
optimistic, hoped to be in a position to know
something about the matter. The first half
of 1914 is well-nigh sped, and nothing worth
talking about has been done. And mean-
while, chiefly owing to the criminal delay
that has occurred in this matter, three first-
class ships have landed on Australian soil.
The first, a Dutch East Indian liner,
stranded on Proudfoot Shoal, at the western
approach to Torres Strait —one of the first
death-traps that should have been publicly
advertised for the benefit of mariners.
Another Dutchman struck, and narrowly
missed becoming a total wreck, on Bramble
Cay, at the eastern approach to Torres
Strait. And next the St. Paul smote
Smith’s Rock, off Cape Moreton, and sank
in five minutes. By the present look of
things these tragedies are but the precursors
of a cloud of others. The States, in antici-
pation of the Commonwealth taking over the
lighthouses, have for some years spent as
little as possible on them. Badly-needed
repairs have been- left undone. Each week
and month the position gets a little worse
from the point of view of the passengers and
crews of the Commonwealth’s Mercantile
Marine. Those responsible ought, by rights,
to be indictable for murder, or at the least
manslaughter, on the transpiration of the
next sea-horror, due to the ill-lighted condi-
tion of the coast. The spectacle of Prime
Minister Cook being hanged at the yard-
arm of a ruined liner, in the presence of a
few hundred sodden corpses, a quantity of
jettisoned cargo, five or six thousand sharks,
and the rest of the appurtenances of a local
wreck would have a most inspiring effect on
dilatory politicians.—Yours., etc., Embit-
tered Mariner.

Proposed Suspension of Law.
“Solomon Malaprop” writes as follows: —
The Melbourne Political Labor Council at its
recent sittings came to some decisions on the Defence
question. Two of them were as follows: —-
“The Defence Act should be so amended as to set
forth clearly that the object of creating a citizen
Defence Force, based on universal military training
and service, is for the purpose of defending the Com-
monwealth against possible foreign aggression, and
therefore under no circumstances should any per-
sons so enrolled be compelled to interfere with
workers engaged in an industrial dispute.
“No State Government should be allowed to raise,
arm or use any force against the workers in the time
of an industrial dispute.”
It may not be a matter of much importance, still
it seems a pity that the proposition wasn’t put into
more definite shape. For if neither the Fed-
eral military force nor any State force (the
police are a State force, so they are in-
cluded) is to be used against the workers during
an industrial dispute, it is plain that the workers
are to be allowed to do as they please, save for
such unorganised and tumultuous defence as any
single house-father may put up with a chair-leg or a
garden rake, or any unlawful measures which may be
taken by a self-appointed Provisional Government.
Of course there would be a Provisional Govern-
ment of some sort, or a pretence at one. According
to the propositions quoted above, the Federal Legisla-
ture, elected by adult suffrage; the State Govern-
ment, which is built on a fairly liberal —sometimes a
very liberal—suffrage; the citizen army, which is
really the male population ; and the police, who are
mostly drawn from the manual labor section of the
community, are not \o interfere against the workers,
though apparently they may interfere on their side. There
is no suggestion (1) of any guarantee that the
strikers would establish an efficient police of their
own; (2) of any assurance that every worker would
be a gentleman who could be trusted on the loose
when law was suspended; or (3) that thieves,
drunks, loafers, sex maniacs and miscellaneous unde-
sirables wouldn’t mix themselves up in the crowd,
and pass as “workers,” and take advantage of the
suspension of law.
So Fat in general, backed up by some of the non-
striking classes who weren’t really Fat, would estab-
lish some kind of a purely class and self-appointed
Administration —if they could find a leader —-while
the People's Government looked on. Being practi-
cally an outlawed dog in any case, Fat would have
to throw his belltopper in the ring, for the reason
that nothing could make matters much worse,
for himself and his women-folk. He would
doubtless import any kind of foreign troops
supposing he could get them. He would buy up
any kind of local force that could be obtained for
money. If he lost there would be an unholy indus-
trial smash, for quite a heap of machinery and ap-
pliances would be broken in the struggle, and it
would be some time before industry got fully going
again. If he won he would probably bum the
Trades Hall alive in his excitement, and make an
unholy attempt to establish a despotism, or oligarchy,
on the ruins of the old order.
It would be a pity if any obscure fuss of this sort
should happen in a country which has acquired the
most liberal Constitution and the most promising
industrial arbitration system extant. . Perhaps the
proposed Melbourne P.L.C. scheme might be recast
so as to embody some sort of guarantee that, if the
ordinary law is suspended during a strike, the strikers
will supplv a good semblance of temporary govern-
ment to take its place. This is important, for other-
wise some very weird and obscene folk will get
loose under the pretence of being “workers.”
The Chameleon or Quick-change
Administration.
During the last few days the Cook Govern-
ment, by the mouth of Senator Millen, has
repeatedly notified its passionate devotion to
the Defence policy—both naval and military
—which it cursed like a legion of devils or
gnomes or what not a few brief years ago.
Ever since Winston Churchill told the
House of Commons that Australia could
best be defended in the North Sea; that
Japan was the Commonwealth’s trustiest
friend and most reliable protector; and that
the idea of an Australian fleet of any size
worth mentioning was, in consequence, pre-
posterous—ever since, in brief, Churchill
revived the old Dreadnought arguments of
half a dozen years ago Prime Minister
Cook, Defence Minister Millen and their
subordinates have been simmering with in-
dignation. Millen published a minute on
the subject which must have caused
Churchill to reel with astonishment,
assuming he knew anything of the history
of the birth of the Australian navy. So
uncompromisingly Australian was Millen’s
attitude in this respect that it evoked a con-
gratulatory cheer from his natural foe,
Fisher. Needless to say, the attitude of
the Ministry does not represent the senti-
ments of the rank and file of Liberalism;
of those persons, in other words, who have
nothing to gain by turning their coats. The
imperishable Conroy, of N. S. Wales, for
example, had the following protest to lodge
the day after the appearance of Millen’s
reply to the British First Lord: —
He considered that, though the Defence Minister’s
attitude might meet with political support, it would
not meet with the support of men who had
thought the matter 0ut.... If war broke out with
Germany the fighting would be in the North Sea.
Long before the Australian fleet could get there the
war would be over. If the unit was there when the
battle was on it might turn the scale in favor of
the Empire... .What was wanted was concentration.
The sentiment of the Empire should be encouraged,
not local sentiment, as was being done at the present
time He trusted that the Minister’s statement
would not go forth as the policy of the Liberal party,
as long as he was a member of it.
There spoke a genuine Australian “Lib-
eral.” The lot of such unfortunates is a
tragic one. They must swallow, every year
of their lives, doctrines which they have de-
nounced over and over again as pernicious,
or be reconciled to the prospect of remain-
ing obscurely in the political background for
all time. Parliamentary Liberalism has no
use for either consistency or honesty in poli-
tics. The party which stood for Freetrade
for so long, and which deluded simple forth-
right characters such as Conroy and Bruce
Smith into believing that it really believed
in Freetrade, has jettisoned that unfortu-
nate old creed as though it bore a curse. It
fought as an anti-Socialist party for six
weary years, and it has now adopted all the
Socialism of its predecessors—the Land-tax,
the Federal Arbitration Court and the like
—and is preparing in addition to introduce
an Insurance Bill that will be the Socialistic
limit if it becomes law. Liberalism has been
particularly strong on the sacredness of the
Constitution ever since the Laborites put
forward their proposals for amending that
somewhat unsatisfactory instrument. All
that is changed now. Only last week the
Federal Attorney-General, dealing with the
Privy Council’s decision in the Sugar
Case, intimated that the Cook Gov-
ernment intended to submit a set of consti-
tutional amendments which would have for
their object the widening of the Australian
Parliament’s powers. The effect of this tire-
less consumption of principles on the part of
the leaders of Liberalism is beneficial, as re-
gards Australia. Owing to it the Nationalist
policy is carried out whichever party hap-
pens to be in office. But the effect on the
fortunes of the narty itself is bound to be
bad in the end. For one thing the inevitable
outcome of such a system must be to drive
the frauds and the wasters to the top. There
is no room for an honest and consistent Lib-
eral in the Liberal Parliamentary party of
to-day. And furthermore, a party such as
this, which has no settled convictions, which
is anti-Australian to-day and pro-defence to-
morrow, anti-Socialistic on Monday and in
favor of State assistance to everybody on the
following Tuesday, has no hope of being
taken seriously for long by the electors.
Australian Liberalism is the world’s greatest
exposition of the Fiji uncle business in poli-
tics ; and the Fiji uncle enterprise was never
a permanently reproductive one.
An Extraordinary Happening.
A Newcastle (N.S.W.) representative
advocated decentralisation at last week’s
Sydney meeting of the Chamber of Com-
merce delegates, and the gathering well-nigh
fell dead with astonishment. The distaste
which Newcastle has for any scheme designed
to connect—say—Port Stephens with the
Northern railway system is as lively as the
hatred which is evinced by an ageing beauty
for her fair young rival. The matter has
nothing to do with party politics. For the
first time in the history of N. S. Wales
every seat in and immediately around New-
castle is held by a Laborite. Decentralisa-
tion is supposed to be a State Labor plank.
Yet the prospects of justice being done to
the N. S. Wales electors who inhabit the
Port Stephens hinterland are as remote as
they were when Newcastle and environs
were entirely in Tory hands. For a New-
castle man, dependent for place or power
on the goodwill- of his fellow townsmen, to
advocate the opening-up to trade of. Port
Stephens, were equivalent to social and poli-
tical felo-de-se. Newcastle wants all the
Northern, and other, trade that is graspable,
and looks to its public men to ensure that
the iniquity is accomplished. The weird-
est of insanities are perpetrated to this end.
The strange case of the s.s. Erse is worth
noting in the above connection. The Erse,
when loaded, is too big for Newcastle’s arti-
ficial harbor. But its owners decline to have
it trapesing about the ocean in a semi-loaded
state. And so, the other day, this vessel
took in half its cargo at Newcastle, left
that perilous haven of refuge treading
lightly, as it were, like Agag, and dropped
anchor in Port Stephens. There it was met
by another semi-filled ship and loaded up
with the second cargo. Salamander Bay,
which is a mile or so inside the Port Stephen
heads, is large enough and deep enough
to carry the navies of the world,
and the waters leading to it are capable
of floating the largest ship extant at low
tide. So the Erse had no difficulty in start-
ing for its British Columbian home with a
full cargo aboard. To an entirely sane
community this wicked business of a bogus
port, such as Newcastle, being encouraged
at vast public expense, while nothing what-
ever is done for a mighty asset like Port
Stephens, would be a matter for scandal.
But the anti-decentralisationists are not
altogether sane: in fact, they are provincial-
ism-mad and parish-loony. And the bulk of
the remainder of the electors don’t fully
grasp what is happening. The building of
a railway line between Port Stephens and
Stroud-road would cost little. Most of the
material could he got on the spot at bed-
rock prices; and the expenditure on land-
resumption would be extraordinarily low.
On the other hand, a huge quantity of good
country would be opened up to settlers in
areas where the rainfall is plentiful. About
100 miles of needless haulage to Sydney
would be saved to Nor’-Westerners, and the
cougestion at Darling Harbor would be les-
sened proportionately, with beneficial results
to those whose perishable products find their
natural outlet in Port Jackson. N. S. Wales
would achieve a new, live, deep-water port,
where at present it merely has an old dead
one —a port in which vessels of all sizes
would be able to barbor in weather of all
sorts, and not required, as is often the case
with Newcastle, to stand off till the blast
moderated or the tide rose or what not.
Also the State would acquire, presently, a
new city—a State-owned city if the Labor
party remained in power and lived
up to its creed—with all the Treasury
gains accruing therefrom. There is,
however, the usual menagerie in the path,
and the majority of its denizens are the
selfish animals who speak for commerce in
Newcastle and Sydney. For the most part,
these calamities think nothing of Australia,
little of N. S. Wales, and less of the inter-
ests of anv particular section of it. by com-
parison with the immediate material advan-
tages to be gained by them if their native
city retains the monopolv of all the trade
that is going. That is why the spectacle of
a prominent Newcastle citizen advocating
genuine decentralisation stirs something
the same wonderment in the. bosoms
of discerning observers as would inspire a
crowd if it saw a shark rescuing a bather
from the undertow.

The silliest and saddest thing going is the
time-wasting motion of censure. Andrew
Fisher moved one on Joseph Cook, chiefly
on the allegation that he had given Tees-
dale Smitß a railway contract without
competition. Joseph Cook hooted back
that Henry Ciiinn had been appointed to
the transcontinental railway construction
job on certain recommendations, one of
which set forth that he ought to have it
because he had worked for the Labor party
at election time. Exactly what Andrew
Fisher hoped to gain by his motion of cen-
sure it is hard to say. He might defeat
Cook in a technical fashion through some
of Cook’s lazy and sluggish supporters be-
ing away, but that wouldn’t give him a
steady majority in the House of Represen-
tatives. He might postpone the double dis-
solution for a while, but it is difficult to
guess why he should want, to do so. Fisher
can hardly lose his majority in the Senate;
it is so big that he might as well expect to
lose a whale in an egg cup. And he may
very easily recover his majority in the Re-
presentatives. A Federal election looks like
a very good gamble for the Fisher party
and a very poor sort of prospect for the
Cook party. If ex-Premier Fisher was a
hardened gambler —a man who was accus-
tomed to putting his sovereign on the bob-
tailed nag at long odds—he wouldn’t try
to delay Cook’s double dissolution. In-
stead he would tell that sorrowful and hair-
scanty person to hurry up and do his worst.
From “F. Croson” :
The weak point in the Vic. Wages Board system—
its want of compulsion—has found it out again. If
the employees don’t choose to accept a determination
they strike; if the employers are dissatisfied with
things they close down their works, or lock out their
men; and there is no power to say either party Nay.
Lately Minister of Labor Peacock put into force
the Stonecutters’ Board’s determination—and so
brought to a head a dispute that has threatened for
some time. The employers’ representatives on this
Board had resigned, and the trade had declined to
nominate others—allegedly because it mistrusted the
chairman —so the Board went ahead without them.
Naturally the determination didn’t suit them,
so they countered by desiring their employees
to take an “indefinite holiday.” This is bad-
dish for the employers, and absolutely rotten
for the employees, who aren’t to blame in any way.
The only comfort is that the public won’t be worried
much. The trade concerns itself with those slabs of
granite or marble whereon are set forth the virtues
of the dear departed, the place of birth, death, age,
etc., Of the late lamented, the achievements, titles and
so on of the remains, and similar inconsequential
matters. It wouldn’t be an unadulterated tragedy if
the whole race of stonecutters was abolished and the
making and erection of tombstones prohibited,
whereby much useless labor could be turned to account
and many slabs of smooth granite utilised for hearth-
stones, paper weights and so forth. Also, the healthy,
sane cause of cremation would thus be advanced
somewhat. The trouble is that the same factor which
is dislocating this trade is at any moment liable to
hold up one of the enterprises that really matter.
The Australasian Cow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wellington, Thursday.—The employers and work-
ers in the boot trade of Maoriland will confer on
April 15 to consider a solution of the problem of
intermittent employment caused by the enormous
importations. —News item.
They took the old, misshapen cow—the cow
they’d milked quite dry,
A one-time goddess of the field! —and said
that she must die ;
They sent her to the city yards, aboard a
filthy truck,
And sold her for a pound or two; then drank
each other luck !
The old, scarred beast was led away—the
abattoirs were near;
The House of Blood wherein they slay ten
thousand cows each year!
And there they stunned her with a blow, and,
next, they cut her throat:
“Old cows were cheap and plentiful,” the
stock reporter wrote.
They skinned the dead, misshapen beast, and
salted down the hide ;
They cut the old cow’s carcase up and sent it
far and wide—
O’Brien’s lunch, and Tompkins’ tea, and
Jones’s dinner, too,
Were all provided from the beast the slaugh-
termen “put through”!
They ground her bones to feed the fowls, or
fashioned hafts for blades ;
They made from what remained of her man-
ures of various grades;
And then they took the grisly hide—the salt,
wet, odorous pelt—
And shoved it in a foreign ship whose name
could not be spelt!
This foreign boat, the Grosser-Wurster-
Something, took that hide
And landed it at Hamburg Dock —with thou-
sands more beside!
And foreigners with frightful names then
came, and saw, and smelt,
And —having reckoned up the price—they
bought and tanned that pelt.
They tanned that Australasian hide with
good Australian bark —
Which had been shipped to Hamburg, too,
I may as well remark —
And when the skin was nicely tanned to
leather, close in grain,
They sent the Australasian cow's pelt bach
to us again!
I’ve got a bit —you’ve got a bit —they’re on
our boots to-day;
Boots made from Australasian hides in Bos-
ton, U.S.A.!
For —as I may explain just here—The Aus-
tralasian cow,
Her hide, and all her sisters’ hides, are wel-
comed there just now!
But over here in Maoriland, and silly New
South Wales,
And other States wherein the Australasian
cow prevails—
The tanneries are shutting up ; boot-factories
close down,
And staunch “Freetraders” — wophing-men!
—are cadging half a crown.
The manufacturers who put their money,
brains and time
To finding jobs for working-men to keep
them out of crime,
Are twiddling thumbs, or catching fish, or
grubbing weeds and roots,
Or —finding foreign-trading pays —are just
importing boots!
And this is very kind of them. The German
and the Yank
Who sell them boots, they sell to us, have
money in the bank
And prosperous, large factories to which
they point with pride—
All built to make the boots we wear from
nur evnnrfed hide’
our exported mcie.
Meantime, the Australasian cow on Aus-
tralasian hills
Grows fewer year bv year—tinlike the Aus-
tralasian bills ! "
So every hide that’s sent abroad a double
loss implies,
And still another industry just languishes
and dies.
But who are we that we should growl, or
beat a loud parade?
Are not our workers boastful of “the glories
of Freetrade”?
Hail, German leather—Yankee boots! We’re
all importers now —
And God defend the aliens’ friend, the Aus-
tralasian Cow !
N.s.W.
O. C. CABOT.

GROSS, FEVERISH, BILIOUS CHILDREN
NEED "CALIFORNIA SYRUP OF FIGS.”
Look, Mother! See if the tongue is
coated; this denotes sluggish liver
and bowels.
Mother! Your child isn’t naturally cross
and peevish.. See if the tongue is coated;
this is a sure sign that its little stomach,
liver and bowels need attention at once.
When listless, pale, feverish, “stuffy,” with
cold, throat sore; when the child has tainted
breath and doesn’t eat, sleep or act natur-
ally, or has stomach-ache or diarrhoea, re-
member, a gentle liver and bowel cleansing
should always be the first treatment given.
Nothing equals “California Syrup of Figs”
fpr children’s ills; give a teaspoonful, and
in a few hours all the waste matter, sour bile
and fermenting food clogged in the bowels
pass out of the system, and you have a
healthy and playful child again. All chil-
dren love this harmless, delicious “fruit laxa-
tive,” and it never fails to effect a good
“inside cleansing.” Directions for babies,
children of all ages and grown-ups are plainly
on the bottle.
Keep it handy in your home. A little
given to-day saves a sick child to-morrow,
but get the genuine. Ask your chemist for
a bottle of “California Syrup of Figs,” then .
look and see that it is made by the “Cali-
fornia Fig Syrup Company.” Counterfeits
are being sold here. Refuse substitutes.
All leading chemists sell “California Syrup
of Figs,”, f/l-j and 1/9 per bottle.
Among all the big rulers the world con-
tains —the rulers who are big enough to
tight, but don’t want to —probably the most
patient is President Wilson, of the United
States. He stands for the crowd which has
always preached peace at almost any price.
The Democratic party holds that the Repub-
lican party is big, blusterous, bullying, trucu-
lent and unchristian; also that it has the
habit of bloodshed and disembowelment.
Holy Calm is the Democratic strong point.
Yet it was Polk, the Democrat, who drifted
into the first war with Mexico, and it is
Wilson, the Democrat, who drifts into the
second. Also it was Buchanan, the Demo-
crat. who wandered into the great U.S.
Civil War. The men of Holy Calm have
very little luck. Many people, when they
meet a Quaker, proceed at once to kick him
just because he is non-resistant. They
wouldn’t do it save for the fact that he is
non-resistant. And very often they discover
that a kicked Quaker can be madder than a
biffed bushranger. Unfortunately the coun-
try which is run by the Quaker has to pay
the bill, and if the bumptious Quaker wasn’t
so proud about his harmlessness there might
not be any bill.

“Port Fire” comes to light:—
The N.S.W. Coastal llocket Brigade got a long-
looktd-for chance to mention its existence on April,
17, when the collier Wallarah was blown on top of
a reef at Catherine Hill Bay, a dozen miles south
of Newcastle. Ma maintains an efficient life-saving
staff with well-equipped lifeboats, rocket gear and
apparatus of all kinds. But as the many vessels
which get wrecked or wreck themselves on Ma s
coast persist in keeping out of range of the brigades,
they have never had a real chance of distinguishing
themselves—the Newcastle lifeboat excepted. When the
news came that the Wallarah was ashore some miles
down the coast the local life-savers determined to
rescue somebody, so they swarmed on to their carts
and began a dreary march to the scene of action, in
the face of a succession of strong southerly rain-
squalls howling like all the Devils of Darkness. On
reaching the wreck they (the life-savers, not the
howling devils) found that II of the crew had res-
cued themselves and were on shore. Six were stand-
ing by the wreck, and the brigade fired a rocket at
the steamer. The captain’s eye was well in for the
first missile biffed into the rigging of the collier. A
line was carried on board and the six men were
yanked through the surf. While the crew was not
in imminent danger, the job was shipshape and Bris-
tol fashion. The Australian coast is too long and
too sparsely settled to have a really efficient service,
but it is something to know that, given a chance, the
few establishments there are can give a good account
of themselves. Had the catastrophes which overtook
the Ly-ee-moon, the Maitland, the Catterthun and
others occurred within reach of a rocket brigade
their death rolls should have been materially re-
duced.
Snuffle and Eastertide walk hand in band
with curious persistency. Heartened, pos-
sibly, by the repeated assaults of Arch-
bishop Wright, of N.S.W., on the Sydney
Show authorities for their irreligious, not to
say grossly unsportsmanlike, conduct in
opening their circus on Good Friday, the
editor of the Morwell (Vic.) Gazette let
fly this way (10/4/T4) :
Easter is upon us once again with its usual accom-
paniment of merriment and holiday-making. It has
more than once occurred to us how few are the
people who really understand the real seriousness of
Easter, because they don’t trouble to think what
the event means. Of course they know, but, sad to
relate, the remembrance. of the suffering of our
Saviour is scarcely given any consideration in com-
parison with the schemes for pleasure and recreation.
We do not wish to preach, but undoubtedly this
season of the year is far too lightly looked upon. It
is beyond our comprehension, etc., etc.
This is the way the matter appears to us, but, as
we cannot, alter things, we trust that all who can
will treat the season of the year honestly, as their,
hearts direct them. IFe hope that the rest from their
labors, combined with adequate spiritual renova-
tion, will renew their courage and energy, and help ~
them to live the lives of worthy, God-fearing citizens
of the Empire.
The issue in which those splendid sentiments
figured came out on Good Friday. One may
take it, therefore, that during the early hours
ot the sacred day comps, strove and readers
swore,, and lads burdened with proofs and
inkstains fell up and down stairs in pagan
haste, in an endeavor to get the paper out
to time. It is ■ possible that the sainted
editor wrote portion of his godly message on
Good Inday, and more than likely that he
*® aa procas ar, d saw the paper to press on
that day. Later, armies of newsagents and
their employees were encouraged to distri-
bute the Gazette. They were thus deprived
ot their Eastertide rest, and prevented from
connecting with “adequate spiritual renova-
tion. Ihe result was they were unable to
renew their courage and energy” with the
object of helping them to “live the lives of
worthy, God-fearing citizens of the Empire.”
because the Morwell Gazette was
unwilling to forego the filthy dross which
accrued to it from doing business on Good
, riday. When this paper sees a person
butchering a policeman it is not impressed
by the. assassin’s earnest protestations in re
the wickedness of slaughtering officers of
the law. The most powerful proof that any-
one — 1)6 be e( htor or archbishop or mere
, ° ke T can s P pply in support of the view
that the slaying of policemen is wrong con-
sists, simply, in leaving the Force unslain.
An ounce of live policeman is worth more in
this connection than a ton of dead exhorta-
tion.

From Sydney Telegraph (15/4/’l4) -
It is proposed to run night carnivals in connection
with the next Sydney Royal Show, and to include
chariot races in the programme.'
An intimation to this effect was made by Mr. Sorner
last night.
Chariot races are wholly in the picture in a
Biblical melodrama; and they would not be
entirely out of place on the bill of a vaude-
ville show r . A comic chariot race which
ended with the destruction of the chariots
and the disablement of the drivers (the King,
say, and the Dame) would go well in panto-
mime. Also small chariots drawn by trained
dogs and driven by tame monkeys or apes
would make a sure appeal to certain classes
of audiences. But the whereforeness of
introducing a chariot race, or, worse still,
a series of them into a State-subsidised Agri-
cultural Show is a problem that this paper
can in nowise fathom. The Australian
farmer is unlikely to revert to the chariot as
a means of conveyance for himself or his
goods, even though the country roads get
rottener and the railway systems more de-
generate than at present. The chariot, as a
utility vehicle, has had its. day. The writer
has never seen a horse of true chariot type
except in pictures. But he is able to say
without fear of being eiven the lie, bird or
other embarrassing gift, that this class of
animal will bring in no money to the local
breeder. It- appears to be a sort of galloping
Clydesdale. Such an animal would neces-
sarily be SDurned by all moderns who did not
go in for chariots. In a very short time the
industry would become insolvent, and
collapse in a state of irretrievable ruin. Mr.
Somer said to the Telegraph man, with a
certain pathos:—
Our programme is jammed full up with horse
events now, and it takes us all of our time to get
through.
Which statement embodied a tremendous
truth. The Show is already about half Show
and half circus. If it is gradually to be
transformed into a circus pure and simple,
well and good. But the electors, with whose
hard-earned money it is subsidised, should
have a say in the matter first. We are all
Socialists now, with the exception possibly
of Bruce Smith, but to have a State Circus

is a flagrant example of starting things at
