The Sydney Tivoli is in the same place as
it was when The Bulletin called attention
to its existence last week. Also, the pro-
gramme. is much the same—the only addi-
tion being the Gills, bunny-huggists—and
the Tango Tea girls are in much the same
state of dress or undress as before. To-
morrow afternoon they will demonstrate what
Indies look like in silk pyjamas and smiles.
This seems to be a deliberate attempt to in-
fringe the patent rights of the author of
Double-bed Dialogues,” who dresses his
best girl in low-necked nighties and kisses.
The medical men daily attending Fred
Niblo, at Sydney Criterion, report that the
date of his demise has been again postponed.
This announcement is apparently due to the
abounding popularity of the chief comedian
in “Never Say Die.”
At the Little Theatre, Sydney, the key-
note of the production of Pinero’s “The Gay
Lord Quex,” is the all-round excellence of
the characterisation. Thus, Hugh Buckler’s
fine performance as Quex scarcely calls for
notice, since his presentation of this famous
“man-of-the-Pinero-world” is so artistically
in keeping with his milieu. However, one
has only to compare Hugh Buckler’s concep-
tion of the cheerful, clever and resourceful
nobleman with his contrasting appearance as
“THE VILLAIN PURSUES HER."

nightly.
Ham Carve, that helpless, easy-going child
of genius in "The Great Adventure,” to see
with what subtle artistry he achieves his
quiet effects. On its merits as a thrilling
play and its adequate production "Quex”
should have a long run.
The Very Reverend Arch-beditor pro-
nounces the benediction upon “Joseph, Mrs.
Joseph and the Whole Hebrew Family” at
Sydney Royal, as viewed from pulpit 59a :
When this sad announcement is read, dear friends,
Joseph and his brethren, as well as Zuleika, the un-
blotted Benjamin who ate too much at the party, the
pyramids, the .'head cook and bottlewasher, and the
camel will be no more. The playhouse where these
kindly people and live stock disported themselves
vyill be closed in token of mourning-. Personally I
should have liked to see the performance; as I have
heard from members of my congregation who did not
occupy either seats 59a or la, that it was a pleasing
show. It is too late now for a suggestion which I
humbly make, in case the she-asses and the pyramid
are ever revived. It is that “Joseph and the Camel”
would have made an excellent revue. The temptation
scene could have taken place on the gangway, in
full view of even the occupants of seats 59a and la.
Then the camel could have made a sensational appear-
ance by walking the plank; and a dress parade of
tango whiskers, headed by Pharaoh, with the •chin-
adornment that looked as if he had stolen it from
Mrs. Pharaoh’s latest spring model. However, the
brazier that heated the curling-irons that put out
Zuleika’s nice eyes has been- put out, too. The
sheep have started by train for home and Homebush,
and are scheduled to arrive there, if all goes well,
by the end of the week. The pyramid and the
wigs have been packed up. The nice handmaidens,
with pleasing names like Mehtu and Arilennu, have
scrubbed off their brown make-up, and the coat of
many colors that looked like the new Chinese flag
has been folded up with the moth-ball that doesn’t
corrupt. On Saturday another melodrama, entitled
“Sealed Orders,” will take possession of Pharaoh’s
Palace.
The Bulletin makes a personal explana-
tion. A fortnight ago its Circus Expert re-
marked in this page that the Flying Cadonas
were possibly the Flying Someone Elses last
year, and the Cadonas object. They explain
that they could fly almost before they could
walk, and they were Cadonas, even before
that. Likewise they are in no way connected
with any other firm of flies of the same or
any other name, nor with Lizzie the blood-
sweating hippopotamus, nor with the trained
sheep., nor with Joe Cook, nor with Alice the
elephant; also, that their turn is entirely
independent of the other well-known act,
“The Flying Capitalists.” The only reason
The Bulletin can assign to its Circus Ex-
pert’s lapse is that the Cadonas turn would
be very much like The Flying Various Other
Peoples if it were a great deal better or not
much worse, or something. To conclude, it
adds, for the public’s benefit, that the said
Cadonas form part of Wirths’ Circus, now
doing excellent business at Prince Alfred
Park (Sydney) ; and that the programme
also includes the Bedouin Arab tumblers and
pyramidists, several good riding turns, and
Crotton and the Skremka gymnasts, none of
whom, by the way, are connected with the
Flying Cadonas or with anybody else under
the sun.
After to-night there will be one more
opportunity at the Palace (Sydney) this
season of observing Allen Doone and Co.
play “The Wearing of the Green.” On
Saturday “Molly Pawn” will be revived.
There is no particular reason why it should
be revived, but Doone has decided to do it;
and, as he has leased the theatre, what he
says will have to go.
“The Tenderfoot” is still making a big
effort to line up to its advertisements. And
the mere fact that it is trying is sufficient
excuse for this paper to hand out its blessing.
Whether it catches up to the press agent’s
idea of it or not is best settled by spending
an evening at Sydney Adelphi.
The pantomime at Her Majesty’s, Sydney,
shows no signs of the wilt that precedes the
end. Neither the codlin moth of slump nor
the white ant of imminent dissolution is ob-
servable anywhere about the premises in-
habited by Edwin Brett, Barry Lupino and
the rest of the talented family. On every
week night, with the regularity of clockwork,
a loud howl of joy issues from the box office;
and on four afternoons per month similar
howls may be heard emanating from the
same spot. Infallible indications these,
that the show is doing well. The howling is
expected to continue for months to come.
Sydney’s White City is neai’ing the time
when its myriad lights will be cut off at the
meter. Meanwhile the park continues to be
fairly well patronised. On Saturday after-
noon, a children's carnival is to be the fea-
ture of the amusement.
Leonard and Willard are the principal
laugh-mei’chants at the Sydney National
Amphitheatre this week, with “Peg and the
Limit” competing strongly for business. The
inevitable and weary tango and such-like
crank dances are in the capable feet of Carl
Bentzen and Phyllis Lawton. Also, “The
Poolroom” is inculcating its moral lesson, and
comedian Denis Carnev (whom this writer
thought had long gone to that burn from
which few comedians return if the Judge is
upright and impervious to graft) is on the
bill as in the days of yore.
The new craze for freak dances has not
often been blessed with such a handsome
advertisement is it received on Monday night,
when the Imperial Salon de Luxe (horrible
name), at the city end of William-street,
Sydney, was opened. Entrance was by
invitation, and if the youth and beauty and
fashion and enthusiasm that commingled in
the great hall is any forecast of what the
season is to be, shares in the I.S.L. should
soar like aeroman Guillaux. But, of course,
it is no forecast; no free show ever is. The.
hall decorations are the work of a man (or
men) of taste, and are schemed in lanterns
illuminated by electric light of 25,000 candle-
power. The two or three expert tangoists
and contortion dancers having been momen-
tarily whistled off, the floor space was
crowded with thousands of slow-swaying
waltzers. It was a vast, animated kaleido-,
scope. For public dancing the Salon w;as
opened on Tuesday, and will continue open,
afternoons and evenings, during the season.
This week’s Sydney pictures:—
Crystal Palace : “Hearts Adrift,” being a varia-
tion of the original Robinson Crusoe yarn with a
lady (the popular Mary Pickford) substituted for
the nigger Friday.
Lyric: “Mount Vesuvius,” about , the boilingest
thing in films this paper has struck.
Colonial: “An Award of Justice,” showing how
much easier it is for the evangelist who has been
wrongly accused of murder by a politician to escape
from gaol, than for the mere individual who has
been accused of threepenn'orth Of internal beer by a
policeman to get through the eye of a needle.
Empress: “The Shadow,” containing more domestic
troubles than any two ordinary divorce cases put
together.
West's Glaciaricm & Olympia : “Sold for a Title,”
which won’t be news to some titled gentlemen The
Bulletin knows.
Spencer’s Lyceum: “The Chevalier de Maison
Rouge,” one of the best known of the French Revo-
lution yarns, and probably one of the least reliable.
As a matter of fact, there was never any French Revo-
lution. The Bland Holt of the period got up a huge
drama of that name, with Robespierre as scene-shifter
and Napoleon Bonaparte as stage-manager, and it had
such a long run that people began to think it was
real. It was in the course of that drama that Switzer-
land was invented, and even to this day the card-
board mountains are put up in the spring and taken
down and packed away in the autumn.

THE CAUSE—AND ITS PREVENTION.
Dr. N. DUCLAUD.
Your hair falls out either because the roots
snnvel up and die, or because the scalp re-
laxes and loosens its hold upon the hair roots.
ihls is almost invariably due to dandruff
germs which rob the hair of its nutrition,
actually starving it to death. To stop fall-
ing hair and prevent total or partial bald-
ness the dandruff germ must be destroyed,
for this purpose, physicians and hair special-
ists now invariably 'prescribe a lotion com-
posed of J-dram menthol crystals, 3 ounces of
Day rum, and one ounce of lavona de cem-
posee. Rub well into the scalp with the
nnger tips, and every dandruff germ will be
destroyed within two or three days. The
Pair will stop falling out and begin to grow
at an amazing rate, and brittle and scanty
nair will quicklv become soft, glossv and
uxunant. The results following the use of
tnis formula are so truly remarkable that I
strongly, adyise every reader whose hair falls
out to give it a trial at once.*

The Taits had some braw, bricht fliclit
nichts at Melbourne Princess’s last week, ye
ken. The inflow of visitors to that new hame
of vaudeville is referred to in the advt. as the
“Pilgrimage to Lauder.” and Scots wha hae
wi’ haggis fed are leading the way. Also the
purely Australian public, that hardly knows
the difference between a philibeg and a clay-
more, is following suit in this direction. Evi-
dently popular opinion has made a. favorable
pronouncement on Harry Lauder and co.,
even at half a guinea for reserved seats,
which seems a wee bit stiff until ye get used
to it. Some folk are nae sae verra enthusi-
astic aboot Lauder’s introductory sangs, for
the refrains consist largely o’ chuckles, and
the braw Scawtch humor is rather foorced.
His story of how the lassies pleaded for locks
o’ hair from McKay’s bald head is the sort
o’ joke that only a large-salaried comedian
could bring doon the house wi’. But when
he ceases to chuckle hissel’, and lays aside his
huge pipe, and proceeds to be droll in other
disguises—weel then Lauder admits of no
further argument. He’s a’ richt: In other
word, is guid guids, likewise the
real McKay, also the Scottish equi-
valent for “It.” The essential melodi-
ousness of his sanging is the mair
marked noo that he is nae mair nervous.
His hold of the audience tightens every nicht,
and pretty soon he will have everybody deoch-
and-dorising. The co. remains as on the
first night, in all respects except that Kitty
Ryan, the Irish balladist, has substituted

Business has been buoyant at Melbourne
livoli since Ada Reeve and co. took charge
of the programme and the flags of Mclntosh
were nailed to the middle of the auditorium.
•Miss Reeve’s success, by the way, casts severe
reflections on the turns of many previous
songstresses and pattresses who invariably
changed their costume when they changed
their tune. Ada works for half an hour, or
so, in one frock. It was a white virginal
frock at the outset, but this week it is an
impressive gown of sheeny grey, or what not.
Otherwise the wearer is the same dainty
diseuse, with the same lot of songs. Two or
three of them need all the art and effort of
Ada to make them go, but they do g;o, and
nothing makes a stronger appeal to Tivolian
taste than the lady’s lapse into the lachry-
mose. Pathos is not her strong point. Her
artistry is purely theatrical in its most
vivacious moods, and when she strikes an
attitude of despair it is one of those safety
attitudes which strike only on the box of
tricks. Nevertheless the applause of the
audience is warmly sympathetic, as a rule.
The fact is, most men love a soupQon of
mawkish sentiment in their vaudeville stew T ,
and all women are at heart suffering heroines
of melodrama. As regards the rest of the
co., this writer has not yet discovered any
charm in the restlessness of Tucker, the
prancing violinist, who fiddles mostly in imi-
tation of the more expert Rinaldo. The
Golden Athlones have at least a surface ap-
pearance of novelty, and the latter portions
of the Gilday and Pox turn can be forgiven
for the sake of the conversation of those
Hebrew delineators before they start to sing.
When once they get on the warble their appe-
tite for encores is positively improper.

last death at the Royal to-night, but, owing
to pleasing financial circumstances here and
in Sydney, the season has been extended until

frivolity which prevails in Showland. The
poor morphia-maniac, who flies to ether for
refreshment, is a growing attraction. People
who have sighs to heave go and heave them
at the Royal, where the sad case of Miss
Muriel Starr provides a feast of sorrow and
a flow of weep. At Her Majesty’s the cry
is still “Come Over Here.” Nobody responds
to the invitation with any idea of feeling sad.
The Revue is officially declared to contain
“everythine but dull moments,” and perhaps
this is true in the ordinary way. All the
same, a dull moment is likely to get there
by accident, like a hairpin in hash.
“What Happened to Mary” would deserve
all the encouragement it could get at Mel-
bourne King’s if the heroine’s long-lost
mother (Mrs. Winthrop) were fitted with
a nice,, kind, musical voice, and the imper-
sonators of Mrs. Gibbs and Billy Peart had
a larger fund of histrionic talent to draw
upon. Billy is perhaps the most inefficient
item in a patchy cast. His manner of try-
ing to intimidate Mary makes The Bulletin
more sorry for the comedy-drama than Mrs.
Winthrop’s grating accents or Mrs. Gibbs’s
method of expressing her feelings re the new
boarder. The play is worth an adequate
performance all round, notwithstanding the
incredible audacity of Mary’s simplicity, and
the tacit suggestion that her sweetness, neat-
ness and mental culture are due to her having
been born of wealthy New York parents.
Although her adventures begin with the ab-
surdity of Cap’n Bert Bailey shipping her
to the Yankee capital from a primitive island
of oysters with only 20dol. in her pocket and
innocence in her heart, and in spite of the
crude melodramatic composition of her
troubles, Mary is interesting. Her fictitious-
ness tickles one’s fancy, until the infatua-
tion of lawyer Willis for the wondrous young
woman seems the most natural thing in the
world Miss Elwyn Harvey, Guy Hastings
and Bailey do quite admirably in their pre-
sent situations. Miss Oakley 'is equal tb the
character of the typiste who went astray;
Duggan and Bellairs are less equal to their
jobs, and the verdict on some of the others
is unfit for publication. Fred Macdonald, as
an oyster dredger, comes in with the two or
three who may be exempted from bad lan-
guage, but are not exonerated from blame.
To-night (Thursday) Alexander Watson,
the dramatic monoiogist who hit Melbourne
very hard on its headpiece a year or more
ago, will make his reappearance at the
Athenaeum Hall. For the first time in these
latitudes Watson will recite John Masefield’s
poem, “The Everlasting Mercy.” On Friday
he repeats his remarkable recitation of the
Dotheboys Hall portions of “Nicholas Nic-
kleby.” Saturday is a Kipling night, and
next week he will open his tins of potted
Shakspeare and feed the hungry with “Mac-
beth” and other matters. The Melbourne
nights of Alexander Watson are numbered,
with a view to touring dates.

On Saturday the Repertory Co. will have
a night out at St. Peter’s Hall, Melbourne.
Supporters of Australian drama are desired
to gather to the first production of “The
Image Breaker,” by Alfred Buchanan. The
3-act play will be preceded by “La Femme
Riante,” also for the first time, and any
person wishing to be present can get there
for 2s. fid. The charge for “gods” is only a
shilling.
Ellen Terry is to eventuate at Melbourne
Town Hall in Thursday, May 7. She comes
under contract to the London Entertainment
Syndicate, with Blascheck in charge of the
tour. Her engagement is for 30 appearances
in all at sundry places, and she takes no risk
in the matter, the stipulated thousands of
golden goblins having been placed to her
credit in London. The nature of her enter-
tainment can be left for newspaper inter-
viewers to reveal after she has come to land.
Meanwhile the box plan is preparing to burst
open, and prices are fixed at 10s. fid., ss. and
3s.
“Hearts Adrift” was reeled off at West’s
Palace, Melbourne, last week. It is rather
on the giddy side. A single lady and
married gent., dumped by Fate and ship-
wreck on a lonely isle, keep house in a cave.
Complications ensue when the hero’s real
wife appears on the scene. The amateur
missus, suspicioning that she is in the way,
projects herself and baby into the bowels
of a volcano. Thus all ends happily. The
spirited action of this admirable young
woman supplies a lesson to creditors, if
they could but read it aright. Next Satur-
day West is to render a full account of the
semi-religious spectacle, “The Miracle.” A
choir and an augmented orchestra will sup-
ply appropriate noises.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“O.K.”r Harry Lauder’s songs remind one again of
the simple fraudulency of Bobbie Burnsey versifying,
vvheiein “blow” and “away,” for instance, are made
to rhyme by converting them into “blaw” and
“awa’,” and “snow is “snaw” when required to
rhyme with “braw.” Lauder is the B. Burns of the
music-hall. He states in song that his bonnie Hieland
lassie is as sweet as the flowers in the dell, and if
you saw her you would fancy her yoursel’. It is
harmony, but it is not cricket, so to speak. If
Lauder had to liken his lassie to the flowers on the
shelf (and flowers often do bloom on a shelf) he
would restore the missing “f” to yourself, Anglo-
Scottish poetry being licensed to gang as you please.
By way of further illustrating the art of bringing
down a Scawtch ballad rhyme with your second barrel
I wish to suggest to Lauder a first verse of a pathetic
song about the Giddy Lassie o’ the Family:—
Fair Maggie McFarland, the joy of the hoose,
The hope of her puir mother’s hame,
Has gone far awa’. She is out on the loose
Wi’ other puir daughters o’ shame.
The bonnie canary, that warbled sae prood,
And brightened the hame wi’ its sang.
Is doon in the mouth and refuses its food
Since Maggie McFarland went wrang.
“Stargazer”: I like the morality of “Madame X,”
which 1 have just seen at Melbourne Royal. Madame,
comfortably married to a decent bloke, and possessed
of a little Willie, gets that tired feeling and goes
off with another after bright lights and the gay life.
Her second choice pegs out and Madame returns to
Louis, her husband, and pleads to be reinstated. if
she had sinned, she said, she was truly penitent.
Louis declines to take it on, and Madame rushes out
of the side entrance, and becomes a Bad Lot, with
the assistance of a low fellow, absinthe, morphia,
ether and cigarettes, her infamy only being relieved
bv her deep, motherly love for the Tittle Willie whom
she deserted when she first left 'home in search of
fireworks. Now the whole force of the drama is
directed against the dog of a husband who wouldn’t
take this woman back. He is held responsible foi
everything, just as if there was nothing for a woman
to do after rushing out of a side door but to lead a
Life of Sin. If Madame had made a decent living for
herself as a domestic servant, cook, laundress, house-
keeper, milliner, shop assistant, typist, nurse, gover-
ness, etc., there would have been nothing whatever to
reproach her husband for; but because she was no
good to begin with, and got worse as she went along,
Louis is considered an unmitigated hound. The world
is full of women who work honestly for a living.
Who or what is, or was, this “X ” female, that she
shouldn’t toil likewise? Was she so very Xtra?
“B.J.”: The Wowser has embedded his fangs in
the popular old pastime of “handies,” in so far as pic-
ture shows are concerned. He howled his views on
this question into the collective ear of the Melbourne
picture industry, the other day, and the industry gave
at the knees and said it would reform. And so, at
each house which fears its Wowser, a “spotter” now
makes a tour, at regular. intervals, and flashes an
electric torch along the rows of seats, to the great
discomfort of Percy and the blushful embarrassment
of Ermyntrude. An awkward problem for managers
is: How long will Percy pay to have his tenderest
moments made a matter for public illumination?
Alexander Watson, the dramatic monologist with
a fine memory, eloquent hands and impressive per-
sonality, is coming for a second Australian tour. He
opens it at Melbourne Athenaeum Hall this
evening, and will give a brief series of his own
potted Shakspeare plays. His first visit was to have
covered a lot of Australian ground, but all the time
he was not in Maoriland he was appearing and re-
appearing in Melbourne and Sydney. Adelaide, Bris-
bane and the West never saw the remarkable Watson.
Phis tour is designed to supply the want.

Governor. Mrs. Grundy was invented by Thomas
Morton, dramatist, in his comedy of “Speed the
Plough, ’ over a century ago. Farmer Ashfield’s wife
is always inquiring, “Oh, what will Mrs. Grundy
® a / ? ,„ She is the vic F’ s wife - When “Speed the
Plough was new, audiences waited impatientlv ,for
Mrs. Grundy, but Morton had created such a Frank-
enstein that he was positively afraid that his powers
would not run to her introduction. So she never
came. However, novelists and dramatists have since
been plentiful in adumbrations of her. She can be
traced under various names in Dickens, Thackeray
and Bulwer. Trollope more boldly seizes on her, and
she is promoted into the Bishop’s wife, his well-
drawn Mrs. Proudie.. He heard a reader say, “Bother
vr r !l, P ™ udl f ! ’ and straightway slew her. But
Mother Grundy, with uplifted gingham, is Immortal!

JOSEPH AND POTIPHAR'S FLAPPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Counsel (to Constable Vindin) : Did you flirt with
the girls?—No.
Did you kiss them or put your arm round them ?
No.
Oh, sing of the bobby who never would kiss ;
Oh, sing of his scorning of amorous bliss ;
Oh, sing of the fun he will certainly miss
If this habit he’s finally pinned in.
He carries a name that's a nuisance to rhyme;
It comes from a Swedish or Muscovite clime;
But that doesn’t alter the glory sublime
Which glitters round Constable Vindin.
In youth every man’s an inflammable coot;
Ilis love is a blossom that quickly will shoot;
And sweet is the sound of the silvery lute
When Cupid is blowing the wind in ;
But men have existed who run from a girl;
We know about Joseph ; we know about Tearle;
And the Force takes a pride in a similar pearl
In the person of Constable Vindin .
Oh, sirens with wooing and wonderful eyes,
We most of us yield to the arts you devise;
But what can you do with a fellow who shies
From your ditty however it’s dinned in?
Your glances are lances that kill where they drop ;
Except when they fall on the face of a cop ;
On the visage of Constable Joseph John Hop,
In the person of Constable Vindin.
Now Potiphar’s wife, though a Warwick she were,
Quite vainly would languish and loosen her hair
To tempt the policemen of Sydney; for they’re
Above every impulse we’ve sinned in ;
Just like the young man in the biblical play,
They murmur, “You mustn’t,” and “Please go
away” ;
And a shining example is furnished to-day
In the conduct of Constable Vindin.
N.s.w.

WINES
The Mayor of Melbourne, the town clerk
and some colleagues or confederates were
moved last week to inspect an aged build-
ing that the City Architect had twice con-
demned, and had advised the council to
have destroyed. They decided, in effect, that
the architect didn’t know what he was talk-
ing about, and that the contraption was an
ornament to the city. That night it shifted
itself bodily towards a vacant block along-
side that was about to be built on, and
would most likely have flopped down into the
basement excavations had it not been shored
up. Then the architect took the mayor by the
hair of his bald spot, so to speak, and led
him to the scene, and asked him what he
thought of himself. The unhappy potentate
was too shocked to reply. Instead he sprinted
back to the town hall and issued the order
of condemnation. Rarely does such as occa-
sion arise to show the depths of aldermanic
ineptitude and arrogance; but meanwhile
the owner is allowed, by law, 14 days to
decide what he will do with his property,
and anything may happen in the way of
ruin and sudden death. Rejoicings are
therefore out of place.
“0.C.C.” writes: —
The acquittal of St-arclilield in the Cold Country
in rather peculiar circs., practically on the plea that
the witnesses for the Crown were unconsciously biassed
in their identification of Starchfield as the supposed
murderer of his little son (whose body was found in
a railway carriage on the Underground, London),
because the witnesses had seen newspaper photos,
and alleged pictures of the prisoner, and, therefore,
were naturally ready to swear almost anything about
any man at all resembling those photos., reminds
me of a story told years ago by a prominent Aus-
tralian.
He, with two others, had gone to Lords to see a
Test Match, somewhere in the early ’Bo’s, when
Murdoch, Spofforth & Co. were raising J. Bull’s hair
in the cricket field. It was a sultry summer day,
and the heat most oppressive. The trio had donned
white duck suits, as befitted good Australians in
such weather, and they made their way to the
shilling seats in the hope of discovering a few more
from Down Under who might be prepared to cheer on
the Australian XI. Their dazzling white ducks
were received by the crowd in no friendly spirit,
possibly because they were too ostentatiously clean
for the murky City of Dreadful Fogt .
Remarks as to the bakers having a holiday, and
similar ’Arry-like witticisms were slung at the
Three White Australians. These were followed by
the apple-core of sheer cheek and the orange-peel of
dashed familiarity.
One of the Australians was a mighty fighter in
amateur circles, always ready (and able) to resent
any insult effectively. His blood was at boiling-
point, but a row was averted by the relator of the
yarn, who after trying vainly for some time to spot
one or two of the actual throwers, at last caught a
man in the very act. He was a rabbit-faced indi-
vidual with large protruding eyes and no chin
visible, and he was at that glad stage when he
began to think himself almost a celebrity.
The relator of the yarn happened to turn at the very
moment when this apparition stood with a piece of
orange in his hand in the act to throw. “Why it’s
you!” he shouted, at the same time pointing a finger
at the man. Stayed in the throw, the fellow stared
back. “Why,” yelled the Australian, “how did you
get out of Hell, Lefroy?”
That settled it. Not a week before, the notorious
Lefroy had been “swung off” by Marwood, and not
a man in the Three Kingdoms didn’t know all about
the case. The crowd looked, saw the indubitable and
startling likeness, and murmurs broke out all round.
The fellow slunk away. Had Lefroy not been caught,
but tried on the French system, in absentia, the crowd
thinking it recognised the real criminal from the pic-
tures it had seen —the unconscious impression it had
received —would have lynched that gallant orange-peel
thrower. There is a moral in the yarn somewhere.
* *■ * *
A Port Pirie (S.A.) man has been sueing
a girl, whose engagement to him was broken
off, for return of furniture and effects he
had given her—to keep for their future
home, he said (and it sounds reasonable) ;
as an absolute gift, she said. The lawyers
were astonished, and uneasy, on finding that
no reported cases seem to provide the prece-
dent they love to cling to, and the S.M. said
he must rule that a gift of an article, accom-
panied by delivery of it, is a gift, and can’t
be withdrawn at will (or, as in this case,
at ill-will). Nothing much was said about
the frame of mind of the girl who would
cling to the gifts, without having the man ;
but it is possible to think a lot.

“Whitehall”: Lord Alfred Douglas and his
entourage are in trouble again in England.
The latest charge against Douglas and his
former business-associate, Crosiand, is the
unpleasant one of having conspired to black-
mail Robert Ross. They are a lady-like lot,
suggestive of a breath from the Chelsea of
the early ’9o’s, or, alternatively, a gust
from a rank old cellar. Douglas and Cros-
iand ran the Academy for some years. Under
their guidance it became a peevish rag,
wherein all and sundry who didn’t happen
to be friends of the editors were soundly
castigated in the intensely genteel tone of
voice that a perfect suburban lady uses while
rebuking her cook. This was Douglas. Cros-
iand —author of “The Awful Englishman,”
“The Unspeakable Irishman,” “The Asinine
American” and other works of the same
order—has a meatier style. Ross was one of
Oscar Wilde’s closest friends. He went to
see Wilde in gaol. Subsequently he wrote
deploring the influence of Douglas on Wilde
—the “beauty” of Douglas was alleged to
have swayed the unhappy genius to an in-
ordinate extent. This set always referred to
the beauty of the various members of it,
even when the fair ones were well on _in
years. When they weren’t beautiful, which
was very, very seldom, they were either
“dear,” “sweet” or “perfectly charming.”
Douglas got annoyed and said that Ross had
misrepresented things in an absolutely horrid
fashion, and was a cat, pure and simple.
About this time Ross got into trouble with
his wife; and Douglas got into worse trouble
with his father-in-law. (a. bluff, military
person who had been living in a semi-stunned
state ever since Douglas entered the
family), and Crosiand became involved in a
libel suit. The members of the Wilde set
resemble old maids, of the stale, embittered
type, in many respects. They are always
ready to see slights and insults where none
are meant; and to row hysterically on the
least provocation. Also they gossip wildly,
and have tempestuous disputes with one
another, or with any stranger who enters
their circle; and when the matter is ad-
justed, and often when it isn’t, they make it
up, and start all over again. Australia is
fortunate, inasmuch as it has developed no
freaks of this sort up to date —or, at any
rate, not enough of them to be noticeable.
They are unsightly to look at, and of no
value to anyone but the lawyers and the
rouge-vendors.
An Adelaide law-court was almost frozen
with horror, t’other day, at an outrage on
its dignity. Some goods had been claimed
from a woman by a stranger, who said her
husband had sold them to him. The hus-
band said so too, but she claimed that he
had given them to her before he and she
separated. The Court believed her, and the
husband saw himself faced with a prospect
of refunding the other chap’s money. He
stepped across suddenly, and slapped his
lawful wife good and hard and very often
before any astonished officials could fall on
him. He was hustled across to the police-
court, where the only thing his lawyer could
put up was the forlorn plea that the assault
was quite an “open” one. That was just the
trouble. It was so open that denial was
impossible, and there was also the Court’s
bashed dignity to be considered. It was two
months without the option—two days for
the injured wife and 59 days for the bashed
dignity.
* * * *
The Royal Victorian Nurses’ Association
held a special meeting the other day, and
decided that the time was “inopportune for
the taking of a referendum regarding an
increase in nurses’ fees.” The meeting was,
of course, thoroughly unrepresentative. To
argue differently were tantamount to accus-
ing the Victorian nurses of being wrong in
the head. Victoria is one of the few remain-
ing places in Australasia which continues
to pay its nurses the starvation wage of two
guineas per week. Elsewhere the young
woman who attends to the community’s fev-
ered brow, and so forth, is paid a minimum
of three guineas. And little enough it is
when one takes into consideration the job she
has to hold up. The nurse is liable to be
kept going for about 20 hours in the 24. She
has a lot of mental as well as physical strain
to endure. Slie runs a constant risk of in-
fection. And she has to perform, as a mat-
ter of course, many of the duties of a gen-
eral servant. Dr. Clubbe, of Sydney, knew
what he was talking about when he said, a
couple of days ago, that “the first thing
nurses must learn was self-denial and self-
control.” The doctor didn’t mean _ his re-
marks to be taken as an argument in favor
of better pay for nurses, bul* unquestionably,
life on 6s. per diem calls for such self-
mastery and asceticism as seldom appears
outside a convent cell.
A RONDEAU OF HER QUEENLINESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Thou art a Queen,” so ran my lay—
“A Queen of Faery, tricksv-gay,
And shouldst thou weave a charm for me
And bring me spellbound to thy knee,
How sweet would be thy gentle sway!”
And so it proved, till one dear day
I hought a ring of ruby ray,
And cried: “My own —it bindeth thee
(thou art a Queen!”
Flqw by the hours of merry May,
The Moon of Honey passed away,
And now, indeed, I plain can see
How true mine ancient melodic,
For still in sooth my lips can say
“Thou art a Quean!”
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.


PURIFIED PETROLEUM AS A HEALING
REMEDY FOR DIGESTIVE DISORDERS.
The value of purified petroleum as a healing
remedy for digestive and bowel disorders is
now universally recognised. Unlike animal
or vegetable oils, petroleum passes through
the stomach unchanged, hence its soothing,
lubricating, disinfectant action is exercised
throughout the entire alimentary tract. To
obtain the best results from petroleum, it
should be administered in the form of an
emulsion. In this form, the petroleum is
broken up into minute globules which mingle
freely with the contents of the stomach
and are thus evenly distributed throughout
the digestive area, where the'r soothing,
healing powers are exerted to the fullest
extent.
The original and standard preparation of
petroleum for internal use is Angier’s Emul-
sion, which has been largely employed by the
medical profession ever since 1892. The
petroleum in Angier’s Emulsion is purified by
a special process and combined with the
hypophosphites of lime and soda in the form
of a perfect Emulsion that is pleasant to the
palate and acceptable to the most delicate
stomach.
Apart from its soothing, healing properties
Angier’s Emulsion (because of the hypo-
phosphites which it contains) exerts a marked
tonic, invigorating influence, which makes it
of value in a wide variety of disorders. The
medical profession prescribe it extensively not
only for lung troubles and wasting diseases,
but even more largely for gastric catarrh,
gastric ulcer, mucous colitis, nervous dys-
pepsia, chronic constipation and in many
obscure digestive and bowel disorders. Of all
chemists at 1/3, 2/9 and 4/6.

“John-P.”: Auckland (M.L.) is sheltering
something hovel in the parson line at pre-
sent. His name is the Rev. Jasper Calder,
and he pilots one of the Churches of Eng-
land in the boastful city. He has been at-
tacking the Wowsers in great style of late,
and has been preaching a series of refreshing
sermons on racing and sport generally. To
the utter joy of sports and all sensible
people, he has laid down the dictum that it
isn’t a sin to have a
bit on the tote if you
can afford it. This is
glad news. The sport-
ing parson frankly ad-
mits that he goes to
races and enjoys them.
He knows the points
of a horse with the
best, is reputed to be
able to pick the win-
ners occasionally, and
declares weekly from
the pulpit, to ever-
increasing congrega-
tions, that he has no
time for the wowsers
and parsons who rave
about alleged evils
they don’t understand
and have never seen.
The Church has been
holding meetings over
the sporting parson
and has been trying
to find cause to chuck him out of
the holy profession, but, to the horror
of the kill-joys, it finds the deed can’t be
done. According to latest reports this
novelty in the parson line has bought a
fleet-footed trotter with the intention of
racing it. If that prad only manages to flee
past the post pursued by a £l4 dividend
there will be more joy in Maoriland than if
the great lOU was redeemed.
* * * *
The unluckiness of the s.s. Dunmore, of
Sydney, is paralleled, or nearly so, by the
awful misfortunateness of the boat which
capsized in Port Phillip Bay on Good Friday
and dropped five men into D. Jones’s locker.
This lethal craft turned bottom up near
Frankston two years ago and left the popu-
lation two short. And only three months
ago it somersaulted near Williamstown, and
just missed slaying all aboard. A passer-by
who encountered it on the beach, after its
latest outbreak, delivered the verdict of the
public on the marine assassin, in no hesitant
manner. He drove a rock clean through the
calamity.
“0.K.”: The revolver has a terrible lot
of murders to answer for. It was with one
of four bullets from her little gun that the
French lady actually killed the Paris news-
paper editor the other week. Armed with
an old-time pistol or two (she could hardly
have concealed a pair of ’em in her muff)
Madame would not have had Buckley’s
chance of doing the deed so easily. True,
there were double-barrelled pistols as far
back as the old flint-lock days. A beautiful
specimen, said to have been found beside a
deceased oflicah on the field of Waterloo, is
now the pride of Arthur Russell, the Mel-
bourne dentist, who makes a hobby of col-
lecting ancient firearms. But this double-
barrelled, muzzle-loaded, hand-cocked, flint-
locked pistol was a slow, uncertain weapon
for murdering purposes, as compared with a
six-chambered breechloaded revolver. Even
the more modern duelling pistol, fired by
means of a percussion cap on a nipple, could
only be warranted to kill at close quarters
when the shot-at person got no time to
argue with the long barrel and the cap duly
exploded under the hammer, which was not
a certainty. Nor did the shot-at person,
unless he was dead, wait for the shooter to
reload and repeat the performance. The
revolving cylinder was an old idea, revived
in a practical form by Colonel Colt, the.
Yankee. Colt’s improved means of blood-'
shed was made possible by the percussion
cap, and the cap was really the outcome of
a patented invention which improved upon
the flint-lock. The patentee of the priming
pan that pointed the way to the cap and
nipple improvement was a Scotch- parson.
So the responsibility for all the easy mur-
ders accomplished by up-to-date revolver
shooters and riflemen can be traced in a
direct line of succession to an Auld Kirk
meenister named Forsyth.
* * * *
Italy, like every other country, is full of
spinsters anxious to get married to any man,
especially if he calls himself by a title. A
sham Italian count, living in Rome, appears
to have been corresponding with hundreds
of “Society” girls on the strength of his
advts. for a wife. He picked his marks
from among them, and easily persuaded one
dupe after another to hand him her jewellery,
or steal a bit of mother’s and give it to him.
They had all been lately educated in a con-
vent, and they all wrote love letters to the
wicked impostor. He had merely said in
his advts: “Woo me, girls, I’m full of matri-
monial intentions,” or words to that effect.
Isn’t it time that old-fashioned literature
and the drama ceased to depict lovely woman
as a shrinking violet who dodges under the
leaves when Gussie stoops to pluck her from
the parent stem, or as a startled fawn who
flies from the hunter? Also, isn’t it time
that the Australian damsel’s imported grand-
mother stopped shuddering at her lack of
Old World reticence? Perhaps woman was
formerly a slave to man-made tradition
(although Shakspeare’s nicest heroines were
not much built that way), but now she is
emancipated, and any bloke who wants hun-
dreds of her needs only to signify the same
by holding up the right advt. Among the
letters which the police found in the Italian
“count’s” yooms was one from a princess.
Her Royal Shyness was among the “also
rans.”
* * * *
One of the little colliers that hurry up and
down the N.S.W. coast showing a remarkably
small streak of freeboard struck trouble very
suddenly last Thursday night, when it ran
into a sudden blow oft Sydney Heads. After
a memorable tossing about a giant sea
swooped aboard, tore a bunker cover off,
poured down through the coal, put out the
fires and choked the pumps. But the little
steamer that could steam no more was still
in luck —the wind was blowing it away from
the cliffs, and, with all hands bar the captain
bailing with buckets, it drifted south all night
and most of the next day, until the engineers
got the works going again. Once during the
dark hours it was within a mile of the rocks,
but managed to claw off. The tug that had
been sent out to search by the owners was
met on the way to port. The incident is
remindful of the drowning of the Huddart
Parker steamer Alert, years ago, while com-
ing from Lakes Entrance to Melbourne. The
Alert sat very low in the water, and when
the big seas of the Strait had filled up its
engine-room its company had to start for
the shore on any trifles they could grab.
Ponting, the ship’s cook, was the only soul
who eventually got ashore alive. He was
found with his mouth half full of sand on the
back beach at Sorrento by two lady visitors,
one of whom was actress Eugenie Duggan.
Somebody who knew a story when he heard
it made a little book out of Ponting’s hours
in the water, and it was a fine dramatic
narrative full of flying scud.
* * * *
“Norbell” : The hoot of “Night Owl” anent
the scandalous pay of the humbler grade
of sky-pilots (B. 9/4/T4), is a just hoot.
The wages of a country parson is not
exactly, death, but it is semi-starvation,
which is rather worse. One instance: The
Anglican rector of a Western (N.S.W.)
town solemnly assured me (the other day,
that the stipend handed to him for his first
three months’ services was £3 10s.! And he
has a wife and children. Had it not been
for gifts of mutton, potatoes, and so on,
from a sympathetic squatter, he and his
would have had to beg, steal, or give up the
ghost. He ministers in a fine, large church,
to a well-to-do, God-fearing congregation,
but the average plate-returns are too paltry
to even mention. My view is that the
country clergy should join the A.W.U. I
presume they are eligible for the Shearers’
Union, under the sub-head of Shepherds.

CAT-O’-NINE-TAILS MARCH!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Berlin, Monday Evening.
The Left Party in the Reichstag is making a strong
protest against the Army Orders in respect of punish-
ments in East Africa.
Under these orders it i-s stated native soldiers wear
chains when on march, and are bound when in camp.
Officers are empowered to inflict 50 lashes on native
soldiers for offences, and non-commissioned officers
may inflict 25 lashes.— Surprising Cable Item.
We are the Death-or-Glory Boys—Perhaps!
We are the Kaiser’s Kaffir Fusiliers !
We are marching—marching—marching on the maps
Which the Vaterlander hails with hearty cheers—
(And lager-biers !)
Left — left! Left — right — left!
’Tis thus the course of Empire takes its way;
We’re the Death-or-Glory chaps,
And we’re sure to fight—perhaps!
If they’ll only let us live another day.
Oh, sweet it is and beautiful to die
For the German Fatherland far oversea !
We are yearning—yearning—yearning just to try
What it’s like—if they would only let us free—
(How glad we’d be!)
Left — left! Left — right — left!
In spite of chains we shuffle through the sand ;
Though our action chains may cramp
We’ll be easier in camp,
Where we’re only bound with cords, you understand !
The enemy hides yonder o’er the hill—
The enemy’s our father and our son !
And he’s shooting—shooting—shooting just to kill—
Ach Ilimmel! we are chained, and cannot run !
(Or use our gun!)
Left — left! Left — right — left!
The sergeant wields his sjambok fast and free,
But we’re dropping left and right—
. How in blazes can we fight
When we’re chained—and Captain Schmidt has lost
the key?
The soldier’s life’s a gay and splendid thing,
And warfare is one long and joyous spree
When you’re halting—halting—halting in a string,
Unable to advance, or fight, or flee 1
(Don’t you agree?)
Left — left! Left — right — left- — Halt !
Captain Schmidt is tearing up the sand !
And the Colonel is in tears —-
But the Kaffir Fusiliers
Will die to save the dear old Fatherland !
{Perhaps !)
N.S.W.

DREAMS PROM MELBOURNE.


The news came that the enemy
was advancing in overwhelming
force. In the dim, grey, sodden
dawn the Australian Wowser
Regiment pushed to the front.
The battle was mismanaged from
the first. The Australian Dago Regi-
ment, left unsupported, fought like
Romans, and perished.
The Australian Larrikin Regiment,
owing to confusion at headquarters,
received its orders too late. Still, the
charge it made was something to be
remembered.
The Australian Hebrew Regiment
put up a very good defensive fight
just as it did at Jerusalem long ago.
The artist just remembered that,
in this rush of “ national regiments,”
he quite forgot the plain, ordinary
Australian. There isn’t much room
left for him.
The Chinese-Australian Regiment
failed badly.
WHEN THE WAR CAME.
The idea of foreign “national regiments” (Scotch, Irish, etc.), which was adopted by the Cook Ministry in 1914,
spread rapidly. When the war came along in 1920 there was quite a number of these regiments.

“U.S.” writes: —
The will of the widow of Robert Louis Stevenson
is peculiar. Her first husband, Osbourne, was a San
Francisco newspaper reporter. He disappeared
utterly, and was supposed by friends to have gone to
South Africa. Stevenson met the deserted lady in
Europe, and followed her to America. He left her
about £20,000 in value. She bequeaths it to her
KWEI CHIH (Maori-
land's new Chinese
Consul), who is going
to reform the Chinese
of the Dominion.
daughter, Mrs.
Strong, Stevenson’s
amanuensis in the
writing of “Weir
of H e r m is to n.”
Mrs. S. is to give
her brother, Lloyd
Osbourne, 500 dol-
lars a month. The
peculiar clause of
the will is a refer-
ence to his divorced
wife. Widow Ste-
venson describes
her as a slanderous
woman of incred-
ible ferocity, and
leaves her five dol-
lars —a shade over
One Quid. There
is no limit to slan-
der in wills. If
you can’t get level
with your enemy
in life you can, in
your testamentary
disposition, leave
Is. to “that bigam-
ist, embezzler and
wife-beater, John
Smith, he to enjoy
the interest derived
from the legacy
during his life, and
on his decease the
principal to go to
his cross-eyed daugh-
ter with large feet,
E m m eline” — or
words to that

is a movement to
suppress this beau-
ful liberty of tes-
tamentary disposi-
tion by annulling
libellous wills and
prosecuting the
solicitors who
draw them.
Cameron, the Tasmanian politician, has
put up his third record. The first time was
when parties were so evenly divided in the
Federal House of Reps, that one vote
would turn the scale, and Cameron turned
the limelight on himself by being The One.
The second time was when he did the same
thing as a Tasmanian State member. The
third effort happened the other day when he
prosecuted a girl for disobedience under the
Master and Servant Act. He had ordered
her to eat dripping, and not consume the
butter of Solomon on a lordly dish, and the
girl ate the cow-grease with defiance and
bread.
A woman writer on racing is a novelty,
but Mrs. (Dr.) O’Leary was given two
columns in t-he Mail (Adelaide) on the eve
of the Oakbank meeting. She began by an
interview with secretary Yon Doussa, and a
plea for the S.P.C.A., of which she is local
hon. sec. Then, warming to the work, she
ran through the principal runners and their
chances, and finished with careful tips for
the whole programme, picking four winners
and a second in seven races.

Official Biography of Members of Parlia-
ment” (Year Book of Australia) :
Henley, Thomas, M.L.A., Burwood, August, 1904 ;
September, 1907 ; October, 1910. Alderman of Drum-
moyne 14 years. Mayor six years. Amalgamated the
municipal districts of Drummoyne and Five Dock into
one municipality in 1902. Member of the Metro-
politan Water Supply and Sewerage Board, 1902, to
date. Member of the City Council, December, 1902,
to December, 1906. Member of the Municipal Asso-
ciation of N.S.W., 1902-3-4. Trustee of ltodd Islapd.
Commissioner for N.S.W. at Franco-British Exhibi-
tion, London, 1908.
You put a heap of small, piffling “honors”
together and you make a Henley.
LOVE, S.M., SYDNEY.
The late Admiral Rawson has been bio-
graphed. He is alleged to have been a bril-
liant amateur actor in his early days. This
may explain his selection as Governor of
N. S. Wales. The actor’s art is invaluable
in the case of a Governor. There is a story
in the book of a backblocks mayor who thus
proposed the ancient mariner’s health:
“Gentlemen, we have had a good many Gov-
ernors in N.S.W., but we have never had
such a hearty, uncouth old sea-dog as Sir
Harry Rawson.”
“Melb” : The recent fuss about Latrobe’s
old house brought into public notice one
Smithers Gadd. He organised the somewhat
ridiculous demonstration. Queer how many
men get the right names. Look at the late
Judkins. It may well have been that when
Gadd’s horoscope was being cast someone in
authority said, “Call him Montmorency
Plantagenet.” “No,” said a wiser being,
“Smithers Gadd.” And in due course the
subject of their discussion became Smithers
Gadd. The thing was inevitable.
Prince Alfred College, one of Adelaide’s
biggest schools, with a Wesleyan tinge about
it, has just settled on a new headmaster, a
thing it has not had to do for 40 years.
Frederic Chappie will complete his 39th year
next Christmas, and spend his 40th on leave.
The new choice is W. R. Bayly, who, start-
ing very young, put in 20 years there as a
master, and was second-in-command when,
about live years back, he was made Head of
Geelong College. The appointment looks
like being immensely popular among the. Old
Boys, who have a voice in the management.
A Maorilander famous in Britain, but
about whom little is known in his native land,
is W. Phillips Barrett, one of the best-known
designers and engravers of book-plates in the
land where the loans come from. He is a
native of Christchurch. He went Fogwards
many years ago, and started designing book-
plates, as well as engraving them. Now he
is looked upon as the leading man in that
line. His work is remarkable for its deli-
cacy of design and its marvellously beautiful
execution. A French expert considers that
it is equal to that of Albert Durer, which is
certainly saying something. Barrett designed
the Coronation Prayer Book for Edward
Seven, and did ditto for George Five. Also,
he has designed and engraved book-plates for
Queen Mary, Queen Alexandra, Queen Maud
of Norway, and the Prince of Wales; besides
receiving commissions from many paler
lights in the social world and from quite a
multitude of novelists and literary blokes
generally.
“B. J,”; Piddington, of the Interstate
Commission, sems to be striving nowadays
to achieve a reputation as a humorist. He
interrupts with debilitated jests, which are
received by witnesses and officials with duti-
ful guffaws, and are duly dressed and labelled
for newspaper consumption. Piddington in-
spires respect as a cross-examiner, but as a
jester he is a depressing circumstance. And
only a very fine jape is justified when 400
applications for alteration of duties are wait-
ing in the Commission’s pigeon-holes, and
others are falling with thuds into its letter-
box.
“ Jeames”: The Duchess d’Aosta, who is
in Sydney at the time of writing, w&s thte
most beautiful of European royalties Wh.eh
I first saw her at St. Motile iff thb SwisS
Engadine some 20 years Ago.. * e ‘
member what a hottibH shock it was to my
loyalty when I <cotap&red her appearance to
that of our present Queen —then the Prin-
cess May. They used to go to mass together
in the little church down in the Bad near
the lake, being great friends of the old
priest who ran that institution, and I, in
common with the rest of the visitors, to the
town, got an excellent chance of judging
between them as they worshipped in a front
stall or pew or whatever one may call it.
The figure of the Princess was dreadful;
that of the Duchess well-nigh perfect. The
former looked chronically irritated; the
latter incorrigibly cheery and good-hlimored.
The Princess was the last wol'd in dowdi-
ness ; the Duchess the smartest thing imag-
inable; and so on. She (the Duchess) had
been exiled to St. Moritz, in a manner of
speaking. Her offence was the unforgiv-
able one in Royal circles in those days, of
having persistently ridden a bicycle. The
King of Italy urged her to. destroy the
shocking innovation. She, being a spirited
young woman, said that she would leave
the Court first, and presently did so. The
Duke d’Abruzzi, who was here half a dozen
years, or more, ago, is a cousin of hers;
also, she is an Orleans, and like so many
of that family, was born in England. I see
by the papers that she was unable to get
into the Australia Hotel, owing to the race
crowd, and was driven in the end to refuge
in a boarding-house. This is probably the
first case on record of a solvent Royal
Duchess inhabiting a hash-tower.
“Yarra” : I had just finished reading about
the Waterside Workers’ new award of Is. 9d.
an hour, and the more or less honest rage of
the steamship company directors over Hig-
gins’s irresponsible and disinterested extrava-
gance, when I turned my newspaper inside
out and read that some young couple (one of
them a close relation of a rich shipowner)
had gone off on a 12-months’ honeymoon. It
reminded me of the wharf-manager’s story of
how Bill Cargosling (one of his best. and most
reliable men) approached him sheepishly, one
morning when there was no boat in, and
mumbled: “Say, boss, could yer lend us ’arf-
a-crown till ter-night, when th’ boat comes
in? I got married this mornin’, an’ I’d like
ter take th’ missus ter Manly f’r th’ after-
noon fer a bit of an ’oneymoon.” He wasn’t
going to Rome and Venice and Florence to
view the masterpieces of Italian art. He
had cut the Alps and the castled Rhine out
of his programme. Bill just going
honeymooning to Manly on 2 j. Gd.

SMITH ,
Tasmania.
“Hawker”: Have just solved A mastery
that has annoyed me for quite a while.
Travelling round an outer suburb of Mel-
bourne I collided with a new, ornate villa
in the latest Queen Anniest style, the gate
of which bore the statement, in hammered
copper, “Emoh Ruo,” which, being inter-
preted, is “Our Home.” This kind of desig-
nation on a house mostly indicates a stodgy,
infertile habit of thought; consequently,
where money is obviously plentiful, as
in this case, one does good business.
Happening along again a few weeks later, I
was disappointed to notice the gate wearing
a new brass plate inscribed “Holyrood,”
but as the same family dwelt there and .busi-
ness continued good, I concluded they had
had a slight accession of wisdom, though
there was no necessity to thus advertise
their Scottish origin. But when next I
passed the place the brass plate had been
replaced by a neat slab of polished jarrah,
on which was the word “Arundel.” The
same family, consisting as far as I could
gather of parents and several adult sons
and daughters, most of whom toiled in the
city, judging from the number of bikes and
motor-bikes on the premises, still occupied
the property, and have continued to do so
during the last 12 months. But every time
I pass a new name-plate adorns the gate —
East Lynne, Ivia Ora, and others, with an
occasional return to the original Emoh Kuo
and Holyrood. These everlasting changes
worried me, and as they must necessarily
harass the tradesmen, I commenced with
the baker. I learned that the property was
being bought through a building society, and
as every inmate had contributed towards the
deposit and was helping to keep up the pay-
ments, each considered he or she had as
good a right to give it his or her fancy name
as the next; also that the name displace-
ments always occurred of an evening, and
were accompanied by discussions, almost
amounting to riots. I remarked that the
better plan would be to leave the place
without any name, and apparently, since
then, the occupiers have reached the same
conclusion. There is now no plate of any
description to be seen, but as the gate has
also disappeared it looks as though the
argument has this time been more strenuous
than advantageous.
THE RAIN MAKER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One J. C. Balsillie, a layman, is appearing as a
mighty rain-maker, having invented a. wireless con-
trivance with the aid of which he claims to be able
to call down rain on any part of Australia that needs
it. Patent applied for. Balsillie is a radio-tele-
graphic engineer, not an ordained clergyman, and his
intrusion into what is considered a celestial sphere is
resented by various ministers. One in Melbourne says
the claim of J. C. Balsillie “smacks of profanity.
Withhold your hand, 0 wretch profane,
Nor venture in with mortal tread
Where saints ordained alone should reign.
Their province is all over head.
To earthly business give your time.
All things celestial and sublime,
The clergy hold in strict reserve.
(Confound your nerve!)
Upon the wicked and the just,
Since Adam mapped his garden out,
The rain has fallen. So it must
Until the final, fiery rout,
Except the parson on his knee
Make rain in right conformity.
And would you thwart with carnal skill
High Heaven’s will?
The flood was sent to float the Ark
And punish human sin, in brief.
The drought is timed to-day to nark
The scoffer in his unbelief.
If earthly agent calls the shower
He straight usurps the awful power
Almightiness is sharing with
The Reverend Smith!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

The Royal of Queensland had no sensational cleaning
up in the year when the bottom fell out of almost
everything. It cleaned up a little, but in the main
it plodded, and relied on a steady writing off of the
dead horse when funds were available. Quite a con-
siderable amount is shown in the above table as
written off. The fact that the process has been sus-
pended for two successive half-years, despite an in-
crease in visible profits, and the fact that the divi-
dend has again been pushed up, suggests that the
trouble is about over. But the management would
have inspired, and deserved, a great deal more confi-
dence if it hadn’t pushed up the dividend. The
earnings represent about £5 2s. sd. p.c. p.a. on capi-
tal and reserves. On that basis the investments may
be absolutely sound, but the figure doesn’t suggest
that the Royal is doing a very profitable class of
business. It wants to grow if it is ever going to be
a great institution, and a lay-by of £ll9B isn’t any
growth worth mentioning. The reserves are humble,
and a new drought, or the failure of a loan, or even
a grasshopper plague of some magnitude, might make
a heap of trouble. 'The shares stand higher than they
were; still the last recorded sale for the ordinaries
( £6 10s. paid up) on the day of writing (Friday)
was £4 125., and there were buyers at £4 13s. 6d.
and sellers at £5. For a security which paid 54 p.c.
the quotation wasn’t too good, nor did it suggest a
wild excess of confidence. Perhaps a 4 p.c. dividend
and a much larger lay-by might have hurried up the
confidence.

The general verdict is that there is a good future
before the Royal Bank of Queensland if no unlooked-
for disaster strikes the State, and if the management,
subdues its hunger for increased dividends and other-
wise keeps as straight as it has done. The hunger
for increased dividends ought to be sat upon hard for
20 years.
* * * *
The disturbance between the Insurance
Office of. Aus. directors and shareholders isn’t
anything like settled yet. At the last meeting Allan
Taylor and E. H. Buchanan, two of the directors,
were so disgusted that they declined nomination, and
the two new directors were J. McLaughlin and E. V.
Saywell. Soon after the meeting chairman Arthur
Cocks also got full of it, and resigned. Now a big
body of the shareholders is trying to turn McLaughlin
and Saywell out, and are asking Taylor and Buchanan
to step back again, and at the same time requesting
Arthur Cocks to reconsider his resignation. It’s a
beautiful mess for a young insurance co. to be in, and
it isn’t likely to have a very cheerful effect on the
hunt after new business. It’s hard enough for the
infant fire insurance co. to pull through under the
best of circumstances, but all this internal row is a
terrible handicap.

That looks a little better than for the past few
years, but still it is not up to what profits were 10
years ago, although since then £44,444 new money
has come into the business—£33,333 in capital, the
balance in premiums on the shares. Even though
Queensland is having a splendid time the profits are
not as large as they were for the few years following
the drought of 1903. The reason of course is that
of late years this eo. hasn’t been the only one inter-
ested in Queensland’s capacity for having another,
and the: Tiew competitors have been “going some ”
Perkins and Co. showed signs of being left behind;
so they had to do some bustling, and the new capital
got in went mainly In new beer-making machinery
Like a lot of other Queensland cos. Perkins’ found
themselves with more froth than beer when things
began to happen round about 1893. The result was
that the capital was severely written down. Even
then the prospect seemed so gloomy that the old co
handed its business over to a new one at an even
smaller capital of £52,000. But Queensland’s thirst
stuck to it, and the new co. began to celebrate it.
The result was that it paid away far too much in the
shape of dividends and didn’t put by enough. So
that when it was forced to do some extending it had
to get in new capital instead of being able to use its
own money. But even with the increased capital, it
continues to gather together a fine collection of
liabilities:—

Weight isn’t mentioned. The Public Thirst of Queens-
land is a profitable institution, and if Perkins and Co.
will only remember that you can’t eat your wife and
have her, and then keep their dividend within bounds,
the future should be bright.

The gross profits may be briefly described as gor-
geous, which is the case with many gross beer-profits.
But when £750 is written off leaseholds, and £6203
off plant an d rolling stock, -and £s*ooo off premises,
and £15,000 off that shadowy Item called “good-
will,” and £4OOO is allowed for wear and tear and
old age of freehold premises, there is nothing very
special in the business after all. This concern per-
sistently lumps in “goodwill,” which is a paper asset
pure and simple, and “leasehold properties,” which
are a wasting asset, and “etc.,” which may be any-
thing, with machinery and such-like visible items.
Out of assets alleged to be worth £635,681 no less
than £499,866 is called Freeholds, Leaseholds
(amount of wastage unknown), Machinery, Plant,
Rolling Stock, Goodwill (amount not specified), and
good old Etc. (no information about Etc.). Cash is
less than £6OOO, and the dividend will presumably
finish that. The £50,000 reserve is described as
“Freehold Depreciation Fund.” If the Goodwill and
the depreciation of freehold amount to more than
£50,000, this item is, of course, dead, and is re-
placed by a deficit. There ought to be a rigid, cast-
iron law to the effect that the exact amount of
Goodwill must be stated, and that the management
must supply a conscientious and detailed estimate re
the wastage of leaseholds. Also the' paper valuC-of
“Etc.” should be stated separately. Many a man has
gone down financially by reason of “Etc.,” he having
stepped on it and found it a delusive crust on top of
a. quicksand.

During the year the co.’s Westfield works at Auck-
land were destroyed by fire, and the loss of £10,619
makes a big hole in the profits. However, the direc-
tors declare the usual 7 p.c. dividend, which on
account of the increased capital now calls for
£17,453, although the new capital did not receive
the dividend for the full 12 months. Next year
the 7 p.c. dividend will call for at least £lB,OOO.
Allowing, say, £l5OO for depreciation, this means
that the profits will have to be at least £19,500 to
barely pay the 7 p.c. Only once has the profit ex-
ceeded that figure, and that was for 1913. Even if
this profit were repeated (which it ought to be with
the increased capital) it would only leave, say,
£3500 for reserves and depreciation. Wherefore it
appears to this paper that a 7 p.c. for next year
can’t be relied on with any degree of certainty. Cer-
tainly no increase need be expected for a consider-
able time. This table will show how the financial
affairs have moved:—
1904. 1912. 1913. 1914.
Capital ... £195,477 £228,708 £230,000 £256,245 ’
Liabilities . . 46,004 59,599 61,851 50,985
Stock .. 140,729 165,677 173,402 186,693
Factories, etc. 86,590 111,908 115,539 101,489
Book debts . . 43,559 58,180 65,817 76,474
Cash, etc. .. 10,817 13,836 2,444 14,987
Stocks, of course, are very heavy, and moreover, are
divided among the co.’s numerous factories and
branches. That being so, it is a very simple matter
for their value to be over-estimated, and with stocks
worth £173,402 there is a good chance of an over-
estimation of anything up to several thousand pounds.
The difference would have to come off the reserves,
which would mean that in previous years the profits
had been over-stated; also there is the possibility
of stock being under-estimated, which is most likely
the case, and this would mean that reserves are really
more than are shown in the table. Gross profits for
the past year were £66,835 as compared with
£63,370 for the previous 12 months. Expenses during
the same periods were £42,333 and £42,376 re-
spectively, so that had the co. not had the misfortune
to make such a heavy loss on account of the fire the
year s figures would have been a record.

recent years of the trust estates, although, judging
by the growth of commission, etc., it would be of
great' advantage to the co. to publish complete figures.
The day of the private trustee is passing, and in the
competition the co. that can inspire the most confi-
dence will get the business. The best way to inspire
trust is to show to what extent you are trusted.

Seeing that the lay-by -during the 17 preceding half-
years was £3B (once), £62 (once), less than nothing
(six times), and hot very much (every other time
save once), the position may be regarded as satisfac-
tory—for the Ballarat Gas Co. That is to say it
is a better dog than it was, but it doesn’t necessarily
follow that it is much of a lion. Before last year
you could have skated down the reserve column and
hardly felt a bump. A few years ago the co. would
have paid anything up to 16 p.c. on the present profits,
but now it seems to remember the reserves, which
consequently get their share. The co. stored away
a pretty considerable tank of assets before that table
was started. That was when Ballarat was a goldfield
which really possessed gold. Then Ballarat became
a goldfield with no gold worth mentioning. And sev-
eral yearsbacka competitor happened along. So the gas
co., which had been lying wide awake on its bed in the
early morning about noon, and dreaming in the dark-
ness that it was fast asleep, saw a ghost and sat up.
It reduced the price of its substance, and steadied the
rate of dividend. This table shows the result of the
policy of dividing every ayailable penny:—
Jan., 1904. Jan., 1914.
Liquid assets .. £20,725 £24,246
Liabilities .. .. 768 790
Surplus . . • • £19,957 £23,456
Thus in 10 years the co. has scattered so much in
the shape of dividends that, after making any neces-
sary renewals, etc« it has put only £3499 back into
the business. Though the profit has looked up a bit
this year, there is no positive proof that it will stay
up. If it doesn’t, the 12, p.c. looks in danger. Sales
of gas have moved from £10,510 at Jan., 1913, to
£11,550 for the past six months. It seems pretty cer-
tain that the bigger Victorian gas cos. will soon come
under an Act somewhat similar to the N.S.W. Gas
Act. Perhaps it is with this in view that the co.
is putting something by, and not sky-rocketing the
dividend rate.
Some of the fuss about increased prices and
the increased cost of living in Australia is based, not
on actual fact, but on the vague recollections of the
oldest inhabitant, and the indistinct ideas of the
young housewife who remembers that her mother
believed things were different when she was young.
The statistics are never up to date, for it takes a
long time to compile them, but so far as they go they
have a certain interest. Thus the price of bread in
Sydney was the same in 1870 and in 1911. Meat cost
more in 1902 than in 1911. Butter has dropped in
price during the last 40 years. Cheese has gone up
on the whole, yet it was cheaper in 1911 than in
1902. Sugar and tea are both cheaper than they wete
in 1870. Potatoes have gone up on the whole, but
they reached the summit in 1905-6, and have since
shown a decline. But during the past two years
prices have certainly stiffened. -One reason why meat
is going to be dearer is the decline in Australia’s
stock of meat-producing animals. The number of
thickly-peopled countries which want imported meat
is increasing fast. The number of thinly-peopled
countries with a surplus of meat for sale is diminish-
ing fast. Australia had 10,000,000 less sheep in 1912
than in 1911; also 300,000 fewer pigs; and its sup-
ply of cattle declined by about 250,000. And as the
local meat-eating population increased the struggle
for the blood-stained variety of food naturally became
more intense. Yet —and this is another aspect of the
case—the meat has to be exported even if it is selling
in London for 6d. per lb., and this country is paying
Is. 6d. per lb. for its morning chop. Australia has
acquired a vast mass of foreign debt, and it has to
export the interest somehow. N.S.W. is the worst
case, and by reason-of its persistent foreign borrowing
it grows worse still. Something like £6,000,000 a
year has to go to Europe from the Ma State in golu,
silver, tin, lead, beef, hides, wool and sundries, unless
the poor old State is to strike a financial crash of no
ordinary size. One reason why the sheep and bullocks
and pigs are less numerous is that the foreign debt
is becoming more abundant, owing to the frantic
desire to build temporary and unproductive works
with Cohen’s money. Many a pig that might hg.ye

The Melbourne branch of the St. Pat-
rick’s Society, which is a benefit institution, seems
to have put up a new and dazzling record in down-
at-heels finance. The private tomb was opened when
the auditors appointed by the members declined to
go on with the audit for 1913. The secretary, it
appears, had been ill, and the books had not been
written up; so the committee thought it a fair thing
to hire an outside professional man to take off his
coat and do things. After getting his hair and
linen well inked, the worried investigator produced a
report which alleged that St. Patrick had a discre-
pancy of £509 16s. 9d. in his cash-book, and that,
through having no reliable register of members the
society had apparently been paying allowances for
members who had been off the books for years;
that there was no record of those members \Vhtt should
be paying subscriptions-. Wbfit fijjui-fes weren t miss-
ing seemed to, be mote 'dr mss incorrect, and “the
accounts Were iff ft. Stitfe ‘of chaos.” The cheerful St.
Patricks, on . being informed how things stood—or
leaned ,'dr fell down —had a long discussion, but
eventually a motion was carried that the highly
unsatisfactory report (or dilapidation) and balance-
sheet (or lopsidedness) be adopted. This despite me
ragirfg of a minority of more business-like Pats. It
was fi generous deed, and worthy of the. large,
kindly heart that grows fit Cork-, but it suggests that
some sort of skilled Government supervision Would
be good for such go-as-you-please benefit concerns.
If St. Pat himself refuses in lordly fashion to bother
his head about the way , the money goes, it. should
be done in the interests of Mrs. Pat and the childher.

Profits for the past 12 months are only £5Ol in
excess of those for the previous year ; but the 7 p.c.
dividend calls for as much as £1317 more than in
1912. The result of course is that, through paying
the 7 p.c. dividend on the ever-increasing number of
shares, the reserves are being starved. Although the
profit for 1913 is the largest on record, the amount
added to reserves is the smallest since 1905. And
the reserves want every penny they can get. Ad-
vances are £202,362, as compared with £193,390
last year and £28,536 in 1903 ; so that, on present
advances, the reserves are no more than 4.8 p.c., and
it would require only a slight slump to knock 5 p.c.
off property values. To date the co. hasn’t met with
any losses, but still The Bulletin’s advice is to cut
down that 7 p.c. dividend and strengthen the reserves.
When a slump does happen along, it will be a case
of £5 of reserves being worth more than £5O that
have been distributed as dividends.

As forecasted in B. 3/7/T3 the de-leading
process is making very rapid strides, so much so
that the Sulphide Corporation is reckoning on
about half a million in dividends from an
old slime dump, piled up 20 years ago.
The hard cash is being floated out of the slimes by
the new aeration process developed by Owen and
Mcßride. The Central, South and Amalgamated Zinc
(which owns the Australian rights to the patents
used) concentrated on the solution of the slimes
problem on the Owen-Mcßride lines, and the process
now in use on the Central mine is the result. A
duplicate of the Central plant will soon be running
on the Amalgamated Zinc. The actual result of the
process is, first, the selective flotation of the lead,
and afterwards of the zinc, in one continuous opera-
tion, which is so effective that it gives a 96 p.c. ex-
traction of the metal contents of the : slimes, and so
cheap that the old Central slimes dump is panning
out £2 per ton profit—and there are about a quarter
of a million tons there.
The slimes are first ground to a very fine powder,
which is run into a series of boxes side by side, where
air is introduced under pressure into the slush of
slimes and water. The blast of air cleans the tinv
grains of mineral. Assisted by oil and acid judi-
ciously introduced, an emulsion consisting of oil,
acid, water and fine lead is formed. This stuff, by
continuous aeration and agitation, becomes a mass of
black bubbles, which feels like a mass of soap bub-
bles. The lead in the slimy skin of the bubbles is
the stuff wanted, so all the bubbling mass is run off
into vats to settle down. Nearly all the lead is taken
out of the slimes by this operation. The rest of
the stuff then goes to the zinc boxes, where by similar
treatment the zinc is turned into bubbles, carrying
the remaining lead with it, which is afterwards
separated on shaking tables. The valueless residue
runs to form a new dump. What posterity will do
with the new dump when new ideas come along is a
question for the future.
The.separation of the lead particles first is appar-
ently due to the more rapid mixing of the lead with
the treatment liquid. It was this discovery that
really constitutes the invention of selective flotation.
