171 Clarence Street, Sydney.
The principal feature of the treatment is the intensive
aeration of the material. The more intense the aera-
tion the greater the bubble-forming results, and the
bubbles, which are all sizes, from a marble to a tennis
ball, carry the minerals on the outside just as soap
bubbles carry the soap, and the minerals are recov-
ered in the form of mud.
This method of aerating the material under treat-
ment is something similar to the Lyster process, only
Lyster’s method is to entrap air in a centrifugal
pump, churn is up thoroughly and discharge it into
the box containing the material under treatment. The
sudden expansion effects the aeration and permits the
float. In the Owen-Mcßride method the air is in-
troduced from below, under well-controlled pressure,
and the aeration is obtained by the rapid revolution
of an agitation or “impeller” that churns up the
contents of the box at a terrible rate.
* * * *
Great Cobar (N.S.W.) news gets drearier
and drearier. When lOug stepped in, to represent 8., M.
and Co., a drastic scheme of reconstruction was pre-
pared. Shares were to be violently written down
from £5 to 255., and each share was to carry a
liability of 10s., by which means it was hoped to
raise £90,000 new working capital. Debentures,
which, like the shares, had badly swooped, were also
to be knocked about, a£loo debenture being changed
for one of £5O with a consolation prize of 40 shares
attached. Bellinger has now left for other climes, a
badly-disappointed man for whom this paper has
already expressed a certain amount of sympathy.
The new man, Hogan Taylor, formerly of Westralia,
has arrived to go on with the mournful business.
But, before he has had time to swallow a mouthful
of the Cobar dust and flies, a cable message announces
that the reconstruction scheme is off, and that the
debenture-holders have put in a receiver. Klug
advises that the underground workings are in a posi-
tively dangerous state, and that £35,000 will be
needed to set things even approximately right. It
makes one shudder to review the history of this most
Eainful concern. And at last the mines themselves
ave taken a hand. Months ago they-—that is, the
Cobar Copper, Chesney and Fort Bourke —began to
look very sick at depth, and very little hope is now
held out for their recovery. No further words are
needed to close the chapter.
Amalgamated Hill End (N.S.W.) issues to
shareholders an artistic production from the pen of
John Wooleock, not unknown around the Cobar dis-
trict. Reporting on the above-mentioned show,
Wooleock is not of the opinion that the N.S.W.
Mines Department’s low-level tunnel project, for
which a subsidy of £30,000 is held out, is worthy
of serious consideration. “Why it should be withheld
from a company who is doing the same work in a
different way is a puzzling injustice beyond all
human understanding.” The prospects in the deeper
ground are considered splendid, for, after all, what
gold-mining field worthy of the name in Australia
has not had its barren zones? The position at which
the Amalgamated has arrived is this: If there is gold
to ,, be discovered it must lie at a greater depth
than, the present workings. And, presumably, the
more barren the zone which the co. has just been
working the richer must be the zone beneath. The
co. is advised to go down and see, and crosscut the
veins every hundred feet. So an underlay shaft on
the Star of Peace veins, at a point 200 ft. north of
the main level, is to be sunk forthwith—subject to
the approval of the N.S.W. Prospecting Board.
Metalliferous mining in N.S.W., outside
Broken Hill, is getting to the stage of chilling even
the heart of an undertaker. So it is pleasant to read
Of developments such as the following, even if they
aren t exactly true. At Bangadang there is a mine
"“■t -l ust returned, from a trial crushing, 4oz.
15dwt. per ton. The crushing was taken out in bulk
from a width of sft., but the footwall has not yet
been reached. A Melbourne co. holds the option;
and its mining expert enthuses largely over the
prospects. The mine is opened up to 100 ft. in
depth, and gold is reported to be fairly evenly dis-
tributed throughout the ore body.
* * * *
Mount David (N.S.W.) is under partial
exemption from labor conditions. The co. has worked
out all the ground above water-level and only de-
velopment work is in progress. The hopes held out
concerning that small, spasmodic stringer, which was
always on the verge of something better, don’t seem
to have amounted to anything as yet. But this co.
has a knack of holding on to life, in which respect
it resembles the house cat, which is dead to-day and
has eaten all the breakfast meat to-morrow.
* * * *
Amalgamated Zinc (N.S.W.) has treated
1,773,040 tons of tailings since it began operations
in 1910. Dividend No. 15 of Is. per share ( £25,000)
payable next month makes the total £475,000; so
shareholders have received a trifle over ss. per ton
of tailings. For the six months to December, 1913,
253,225 tons treated gave a profit of £61,841, or
4s. lOd. per ton. As the Amalgamated handles over
500,000 tons a year its quarterly distribution of
Is. per share requires a net profit of 4s. per ton.
Profits at present (taking spelter at £2l) are about
£lO,OOO per month. Dividends would become pre-
carious if spelter fell to £l9 per ton for the annual
profit would be reduced by about £69,000, and
spelter has been known to slump £4 13s. 4d. in a
few months. The Amalganiated is entitled to 45
p.c. of the royalty on the Mineral Separation process,
so it may expect to receive a few thousands a year
from this source. There are 1,000,000 tons of tail-
ings on hand.
For the first quarter of this year Broken
Hill’s net return was £1,078,528. Assuming that
the 83,646 tons of lead concentrates returned an
average of 60 p.c., there was 50,187 tons of metal.
A field that can throw 50,000 tons of lead at the
market in three months must be one of the world’s
largest producers. The 109,175 tons of zinc con-
centrates exported are valued at £278,030, just over
50s. per ton, and they contained over 40,000 tons of
zinc. The silver runs to between two and three
million ounces per quarter. Last year Broken Hill
hauled 1,744,177 tons of ore, and five years at that
rate will use up 8,720,885 tons. Under these cir-
cumstances, big dividends are partly a return of
capital.
Hawkins Hill Reward (N.S.W.) caught
•on with the market, because No. 2 winze struck a
make of soz. stone Bft. below the level (320 ft.).
During March the most important work was the
■sinking of No. 1 winze, which is now down more
than 40ft., and 40 tons taken from this winze re-
turned 71oz. gold. A crushing from No. 2 winze
(100 ft. north of No. 1) should appear in the next
report. Both winzes are on improving prospects,
but a reef only 18in. wide requires to be fairly rich.
Some people have great expectations of an enrich-
ment when the north level gets further in. The
report merely remarks that the reef has increased in
■size, and shows fair dish prospects. Prospects were
run in east and west—2oft. each way—but nothing
payable was met with.
* * * *
Eleanora Gold Mines, Hillgrove
(N.S.W.), has once again resumed the hunt after
'Khe elusive--dividend, now that it has managed to
clean up some of the mess that was made at the
last reconstruction. The mess was mostly due to
the failure of certain estimates of capital cost to
re-timber the shaft, and remove and re-organise the
old plant. Last month the ore drawn from stopes
over a length of 150 ft. on a reef 3ft. wide was re-
ported to have averaged 30dwt. per ton. This reads
attractively enough, what there is of it, and that is
the question the co. is seeking to solve. The job of
resurrecting skeletons never was any sinecure, ex-
cept to the ancient prophets. And prophecy has not,
so far, been a strong feature of this co. Yet that
is a point in its favor. Hill End provided a similar
venture, but with different accompaniments and with
different results to date.
* * * *
Ardlethan Options, Amalgamated Hill
End and Pernatty Lake Copper are getting a bit
mixed up in their relations, to the extent that it
is impossible to throw a brick at one without in-
furiating the other two. The Ardlethan failure has
reached the stage whereat the directors have been
enabled to take shareholders into their confidence —
fortunate shareholders! The directors accordingly
announced that they had underwritten some of the
new issue of the Amalgamated. Prospects were so
bright that the fact of the price agreed upon being
well above the existing market price of the Amalga-
mated shares didn’t matter a bit. The managing-
director happens to be the same for both companies,
so some “inside information” was thus divulged. Then,
again, the same Ardlethan failure had a share in the
Pernatty scheme, and that £5O share had risen to
£5OO. This may be due either to scarcity of mar-
ket scrip or to genuine prospects. On the latter
score John Wooleock testifies to 150,000 tons, pro-
bably giving an average extraction of 5 per cent,
copper at a cost of one unit of copper, spread over a
lake bottom half a mile long, with a thickness of at
least sft. Then there is gold, and a whole heap of
probable ore not included in the estimate, some of
which, being bornite, proves beyond a doubt that the
deposit has a deep-seated origin.
If the suggested tonnages and values are
realised the Laloki (Papua) option should be a good
thing for Mount Morgan, as well as a lifebuoy and
a sheet-anchor for Fitzroy. 1< itzroy’s announcement
on the subject indicates that Mount Morgan takes
over the 2s. per ton royalty payable to the prospec-
tors. From Morgan shareholders’ point of view the
worst feature of the deal is the payment of further
large sums of money—£7s,ooo for the mine and,
presumably, a similar amount or even more on
development and equipment. But when they remem-
ber that Many Peaks ore won’t last for ever, and
that Laloki contains 4J p.c. copper and 3dwt. gold
against 1) copper and trace gold in Many Peaks
flux, they will probably be reconciled to the further
deferring of the much-talked-of increased dividends.
Fitzroy shareholders ought to be jubilant if the
option is exercised. It will probably mean scrapping
the concentrating mill and using the ore for, fluxing
the Laloki’s profit-giving material. The latter won’t
happen for probably two or three years, as the Port
Moresby-Sapphire Creek Railway, about 20 miles,
isn’t started yet.
Mount Elliott’s amalgamation with the
Great Australia (Cloncurry) is interesting. This old
show was famous a generation ago for its huge
nodules of native copper and rich oxides. How much
rich ore is left cannot be told, but latest smelt-
ing campaign in 1908 was productive of large quan-
tities of high-grade matte which was shipped south
for treatment. Smelting with reverbs, (supplied
with wood fuel) proved to be too expensive, so the
Queensland Exploration Co. —which worked the Great
Australia on a royalty basis —had to shut down.
The average extraction per ton of ore was 6 p.c.
copper. Caleite ore from the mine contained low
copper values, but proved to be a good flux. So
Cloncurry may be a good smelting base. The
Dobbin mine, which comes within the amalgamation
scheme, has also been a large producer of rich ore.
Altogether, there are 2900 acres of freehold and 425
acres of leasehold to be amalgamated.
The closing down of the copper smelters
at Chillagoe, Mount Elliott, Great Fitzroy, Mount
Perry and Great Cobar suggests trouble ahead for the
industry. In a general sense, costs are high, but it
doesn’t follow that wages are out of fair proportion
to the cost of living and the large profits derived
from the mining industry. In 1913 the value of
metals was unusually high, and lead, zinc and copper
mines paid their shareholders over £2,000,000 in
dividends. There was a keen demand for skilled
labor, and, under those circumstances, it was quite
natural for wages to rise. Still Australian wages are
not unreasonably high compared with United States
figures for underground work. Our wages are sel-
dom higher than they ought to be, but the adminis-
tration of our mineral resources could stand a lot
of improvement. Over-capitalisation, bad adminis-
tration and the sale of products in the unrefined or
semi-crude condition is a frequent source of trouble.
The wraith of departed metallurgist Wes-
ley haunts the great Mount Morgan (Queensland)
mine. The new management hankered after an iron-
stone campaign and forecasted big reductions in the
amount of Many Peaks flux ore required. Wesley
would have none of it, hence exit Wesley and enter
ironstone. The result is reflected in the recently-
issued Many Peaks report. The table of tonnages
there given shows a big reduction at the start of the
ironstone campaign (in one period to less than 2000
tons), but the figures have been rising latterly, and
the latest Mount Morgan four-weekly return shows
7364 tons of Many Peaks ore were used to smelt
19,144 tons of Mount Morgan ore. The ironstone
figures for the period are not stated. The 19,144
tons of Mount Morgan ore yielded 503 tons copper
and 62250 z. gold, equal to 2.65 p.c. copper and 6/
dwts. gold per ton. The actual assay value of the
ore depends on the slag losses, which are liable to be
high when ironstone is used. At any rate, it is cer-
tain the ore assayed over 3 per cent, of copper—so
that the ore being treated is still higher than the
average of the published reserves.
»*, * *
Bitumen and petroleum have been re-
cently reported from the Nullabor Plains, north of
the head of the Great Australian Bight. A prospect-
ing license over a couple of square miles surround-
ing the spot has been secured by the South Aus-
tralian Oil Wells Co. The alleged find may, of
course, turn out as many others have done, but each
year seems to be nearer a genuine discovery. At
one time it was reckoned that the known features of
the geological history of this continent did not point
to the existence of such conditions as were considered
favorable for the formation of petroleum. But
exactly what those conditions should have been is
a point that hasn’t been satisfactorily defined even
yet, so there is plenty of room to doubt the old doc-
trines. Papua was put under the same ban as Aus-
tralia, but recent events look like blowing the
bottom out of that idea. Most of the world’s supply
of petroleum comes from sedimentary rocks of the
Miocene and Eocene periods. Round the Bight these
formations are to be found in abundance.
* ♦ * *
The Victorian Treatment Co., a Westra-
lian concern which acquired for £15,000 or so
various sand and slime heaps from W. B. Wilkinson, a
Maryborough (Vic.) cyanider, and proceeded to dem-
onstrate the value of Westralian systems as applied to
Victorian dumps, recently retired hurt and submitted
its properties for sale by tender. The failure to attain
sufficiently payable results, coupled with the failure
of the fine vacuum filter plant erected with S.A. and
Westralian capital bn the Magdala slimes heap at
Stawell (Vic.), furnished another proof that after
all Vic. cyaniding methods, crude and primitive as
they may appear to experts who are used to modern
systems in vogue in the West, are really not so far
astray when applied to local goods. Experts from
both N.S.W. and the West who have visited Victoria
have expressed horror at the ignorance of the local
savages, and have proceeded to demonstrate what
modern methods can do. But the local savages con-
tinue to cyanide their 2s. extraction stuff when the
mighty ones have gone. Probably the fact is that
the stuff is savage as well as the people who treat it.
Burtville (W.A.) is hopeful that the re-
cent performances of the Nil Desperandum will direct
attention to its “long neglected gold resources.
The co.’s latest yield was 1116 oz. from 265
tons, not 116 oz. as was stated in B. 9/4/’l4.
Between 1897 and 1913 the show mentioned yielded
£33,700 from 4000 tons, the wealth coming from
narrow quartz leaders. Less than a year ago the
owners started sinking on a lode formation, and from
this 425 tons have since been extracted and treated
for a return of £7860. The bulk of the values are
contained in veins of quartz running vertically in a
big kaolin and ironstone formation, but whether they
will extend for any distance remains to be proved.
Some usually well-informed persons are inclined to
think they won’t.
Bullfinch Proprietary (W.A.) announces a
drop of from 51s. 9d. to 365. in the value of its
ore reserves compared with last year’s figures, while
the tonnage is down a little. The shrinkage in grade
is, presumably, due to the ore treated during the 12
months being of much richer grade than the average
of the mine, a fact which the management made no
attempt to conceal. Returns for some time to come
will probably be affected by the recent creep, and,
meanwhile, they have, vide last month’s output, been
brought down to 325. 3d. per ton. During 1913 a
proportion of sulphide ore was sent to the mill. In
October this amounted to 12 p.c. of the total quantity
treated during that period.
Randalls (W.A.) seems to be fulfilling
The Bulletin’s expectations. A crushing of 700
tons, handled by six men and a horse, has just been
put through the mill by the Government-subsidised
Santa Claus syndicate for a return by amalgamation
of £450, with an alleged additional £5OO on the
plates and in the residues. But £5OO is, as has been
said over and over again on this page, only £5OO
when it is in the bank or some other safe place.
Dump and plate values are never worth 20s. in the
£ —some do not average that many pence. The
next return is expected to be better, and steadfast
in that belief, one or two supporters of the field are
asking London to lend a helping financial hand. If
it does, it might just as well lean up against
the nearest lamp-post and wait to be struck by light-
ning as expect to get any return for its outlay. By
picking and choosing and getting monetary assist-
ance from the State the district may yield a few
fairly good returns, but it is only a matter of
time for the end to come and the crowd of sorrowful
mourners to gather round. As previously remarked,
when the history of the place comes to be written,
the local pub will have been found to have been
the only profitable investment.

* * * *
Norseman (W.A.) is up in arms over the
threatened departure of the Mararoa. Local share-
holders demand (1) that the payable ore recently cut
by the drill at 300 ft. below the present workings of
the co.’s mine should be further prospected; (2)
that the co. spend all its surplus cash if necessary
on this work; and (3) that it look for something on
the spot, and not take its spare capital, and per-
haps later on the whole of its bag and baggage, to
another place.
* * * *
It is said in Adelaide that Pernatty
Lagoon Co. (S.A.) will be financed in this way by
Robinson, Clark and Co. of London. The capital
will be 300,000 shares of £l, of which the original
proprietors take 30,000 fully paid, and the pro-
moting syndicate 60,000 fully paid; 50,000 shares
are reserved to pay the above firm’s underwriting
commission and brokerage, and 160,000 underwritten
shares are offered to the public at 2s. on applica-
tion and 2s. on allotment, yielding £32,000. The
promoting syndicate was in 200 shares on which
£3 each was paid, or £6OO in all. So they will be
repaid a hundred-fold. The London underwriters
may or may not get 50.000 £1 shares for insuring
£32,000 cash. It looks like being chiefly “may
get.” The syndicate £3 shares went to over £4OO
apiece, which is a long .way ahead of the Bull-
finch deal, a similar one in every respect to this.
That is to say, Bullfinch and Pernatty are alike
because there was, and is, not enough ore visible
to justify the flotation figures in either case.
* * * *
An Adelaide syndicate has just started to
open up a seam of brown coal that was discovered a
dozen vears asm at Bower, where a Government bore
in search of water for railway purposes went through
a 42ft. seam at a denth of 342 ft. Bower, which is
85 miles from Adelaide, on the Morgan railway
line, has been noted for years as a base for the
supply of firewood, but as the supply is running out
the brown coal will be able to take its place in
the near future. A shaft 10ft. by 6ft. has been
started, and is going down at about 3ft. per day.
Captain W. O. Tennant, well known in Victorian
gold-mining circles, is at the head of affairs.
Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A.) is hatching
a scheme in the form of an amended sliding scale of
wages, depending on the price of copper. Standard
rate of pay is to be based on copper at £65, and
three rises of 5 p.c. each are to be given when
copper reaches £75, £35 and £95 per ton,

A
WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, April 20, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
G.-G. Denman and his tall lady put in the
tail of last week at Admiralty House, and
departed for the Falkiners’ sheep run in
this State on Sunday evening. Their flight
was more or less by stealth. Enthusiastic
friends and acquaintances stalked the Mel-
bourfae express after their Exes’ party had
been jolted out into the night. The news
that the Anglican Synod loaded the Den-
mans with an illuminated farewell address
(in spite of Sunday travelling) will be wel-
comed with tears by the incoming G.-G.
Munro-Ferguson. He will have to carry
about the Scotchbytei'ian forms of affection,
set in leather, as well.
Saturday’s races brought forth all the last
remarks in autumn goods. The ladies inside
them must have sweltered severely in secret,
for a warm wind fanned their heated brows,
and furs were as comfortable as horserugs in
sultry February.
The vice-regal pens were better filled than
on any other race day, and had the top-hatted
appearance that shadows the Gee-Gee during
his occasional peeps at this city. Lady Den-
man wore a thin, filmy gown that
matched the temperature, and watched
the flying hoofs of Cagou (who ran away
with the A.J.C. Plate) under the wide brim
of an imposing black hat. The Barttelot
lady, who grows slenderer and slenderer, was
in black, with the slash of grass green that
accompanies the rage for raven garb. Gov-
ernor Strickland brought his blue-eyed eldest
daughter along. Lady Patey, who has a
leaning towards millinery that shoots up a
long bow in front, was sandwiched in be-
tween the sub-royalties, with her Admiral
near by. !
Gee-Gee Denman, looking about 25, brought
to Sydney in his train the usual immaculate
Helps, who wear exactly the same kind of
bored expression, the same beautifully-oiled
locks, and the same manner of having been
turned out as mutes for a high-class funeral.
Captain Pollok, A.D.C., who is something of
a Stranger here, has this mournful appear-
ance of being martyred in his best London
clothes to such an acute degree that he paced
the Randwick lawn like a caged, top-hatted
Daniel waiting for the lions.
Mrs. Pat Osborne, in black on Saturday,
was heavily rugged in biscuit-colored furs
that would have gladdened the heart of a
Mrs. Esquimau.
The tall, impressive presence of Mrs. Claud
Macdonajd, of Wantabadgery station, was
like a watchtower of style in the fluffy pre-
cincts of the members’ stand at the recent
race meet. She is the lady who is always
having it cast up at her that she knows more
about the internal mysteries of majestic
clothes than most smart women, because she
once dabbled in the high-class dress business
in Melbourne.
The once popular fashion of dressing sis-
ters alike —or of sisters dressing themselves
alike, modern maidens being a law unto them-
selves—made an occasional revival in the
great Rag parade of last week. At Wednes-
day’s race meet two young damsels with
tunics in a kind of patchwork-quilt design,
over dark skirts, made the notion of doubling
a sartorial shriek merely a nuisance. Mrs.
Charlie Wade tactfully refrained from turn-
ing out her twin girleens in twin gowns dur-
ing the recent hubbub. And their appear-
ance only showed a discreet family likeness.
The tall Brennan sisters, Florence and
Adfele, went to Saturday’s races in fac-
simile starch white cloth tailor-mades, and
little black velvet hop-o’-my-thumb hats. They
didn’t stamp up and down the lawn like the
patchwork designs, so all is forgiven. By
this time I expect they both feel sorry. White
tailored garb promptly dusts every tram seat,
and has to be sent in disgrace to the cleaner
till it eats up a mint of dress allowance.
Mrs. Tom Rolin’s tall young daughter wore
one of the cosy crimson tailor-built affairs
(at Saturday’s races) that only need a little
white lettering to make them useful under-
studies to a G.R. letter-box. The coats have
snug little pockets (there’s a strong re-action
in woman’s breast for pockets in these bag-
snatching days) in which any absent-minded
man of learning could easily post a treatise
on sun-spots.
A German woolbuyer named Hilger, who
has been attracting the feminine glance at
the recent orgy of horse, is a fine, well-set-up
young Teuton, and understood to be still open
to engagement.
The Ralph Verneys have bustled back to
Sydney after a long spell in the damp Land
of the Cheap Breakfast Table and the under-
paid toiler. He used to A.D.C. the haughty
Chelmsford menage. A honeymoon in the
middle of the Help business occurred when
Aide Yerney married Senator Walker’s
daughter Nita.
The eternal Hat Feminine has taken on a
saucy, stuck-up look all round. When it’s
boiled down to the new French toque species
it grows a high, folded silk fence all round,
and looks rather like a closed concertina.
Only a few of these foreign importations
were dragged out of Paris hat-boxes before
race week, though they’ll march round town
in dozens presently. Little Mrs. Forbes
Angus wore one of these chic pioneers the
other day. It had a fascinating flick of pink
somewhere near the front. The deserving
poor who viewed this creation at the races
could only turn over in their minds the best
way to imitate it at bedrock price.
The mild anguish of trying to dissect the
fashionable dress so as to say what it is—
instead of what it isn’t —becomes acute when
one has to stare steadfastly, practically to
the bone, into the fixings of the woman who
seems to get an awful lot for her money.
Last week, being mostly warm and summery,
brought out the three-layer gown. The
under-stratum appeared to be built of some-
thing fairly substantial and sufficiently
heavy to keep off sunburn. Over this
goes a sub-stratum of stout lace. The whole
is covered with a dark, transparent film. Put
a feather boa and a few yards of pearls over
the whole, hang a collection of loose bric-st-
brac —roughly described as a chatelaine—
from the belt so that it clatters like falling
ironmongery, and you arrive (exhausted) at
the end of a heart-to-heart talk with Clothes.
One of the best-looking of those intricate
gowns, the outer skin or epidermis being navy
blue, covered the large, benign presence of
Miss Macldnnon, of Derribong station, on a
recent race day.
Gentleman Help Talbot, who now decor-
ates the vice-regal camp in Queensland, gra-
ciously looked in at the Easter festivities in
this humble “colonial” town. He has grown
a fine Australian coat of tan over his once
girlish complexion, to the great improvement
thereof.
One of the male persons who has made the
biggest Easter splash in “society” is Com-
mander Claude Cumberlege, R.N., of the
Warrego. He’s said to be the most dashing
beau of the moment hereabouts, and a dancer
of breathless energy. The local mamma is
not quite sure whether Captain Cumberlege
is an eligible bachelor or a merry grass
widower, or what, but she means to find out
even if she has to steam his correspondence
and open it surreptitiously to do the deed.
May Grice, one of Melbourne’s Society
damsels, has been enjoying the multitudi-
nous dances here. She wore a double-decker
gown, however, at the Matrons’ hop that
didn’t excite the local girl to grit her teeth
in any wild spasm of envy.
Mrs. John Osborne, who used to be Olive
Atherton, of Melbourne, has been raising the
little green monkey of envy in the breasts of
less dressy young dames. At the Matrons’
hop last week her deep-toned rose double-
decker frock was one of the best-looking
events of the evening.
Marjory, the recently come-out, fair, fluffy-
haired daughter of the Alfred Marks’, has
been gadding in the thick of the recent festive
fray. She got away with a lot of admiration
at the rose-trimmed matrons’ dance in a
white frock, with the usual 1914 drapery less
bunched up than in the more advanced styles.
Some of the advanced styles, by the way, are
so looped up all round that the lady inside
looks the slim ghost of an old four-poster bed-
stead after the curtains had been pulled
aside.
The Findlay dame from Maoriland, who
went off to Europe in the Orama the other
day, is the wife of the lawyer Findlay who
was Attorney-General in a Ward (M.L.)
Ministry. He caught a knighthood—a mod-
erately easy gaud to run to earth
during a visit to Bullopolis, about three
years ago. His lady is very keen about
the handle, and any Orama passenger who
chances to mislay the designation for an
hour will be a careless person and in search
of trouble.
Bart. Joseph Ward (M.L.) paid a rush
visit to Sydney last week. He hadn’t time
to unpack his after-dinner speeches before he
bustled off to Wellington on the same craft
that brought him this way. During his short
sojourn hereabouts the Dominion’s ex-
Premier camped under the hospitable shingle
of manager Owen Cox, of Birt and Co.’s big
shipping firm.
Lord Dick Nevill put in as abbreviated an
appearance at last week’s razzle-dazzle as his
extreme length would permit. He grasped
the hands of a few score of friends near the
rushing shadow of the horse at Randwick,
but was otherwise something of a hermit,
A polo ball of the heavily gilt-edged guinea
tickets and champagne finish is booked for
July 9. It is going to repeat the financial
success of last year in aid of the Civil Ambu-
lance. Much the same committee of town
and country dames will push the sale of
tickets. Hon. sec. Mrs. Macneill Simpson
and hon. treasurer Mrs. Spencer Brunton are
girding up their tunics for the fray. Just
here may I ask what the Civil Ambulance
horse is going to get out of this Society frivol?
He was mostly ribs and points when he at-
tended the Easter races. A regular stomach-
full of corn ought to be Ms perquisites. As
he is unable to attend any committee meet-
ings I mention his ribs on his behalf.
Artist Alf Vincent sends a joyous whoop
this way concerning the arrival of a brand-
new daughter. Miss Vincent is said to be
a most charming and accomplished infant.
Fancy having a Duchess d’Aosta wander-
ing this way without a brass band or a guard
of honor or a throne chair! The lady blew
in and out of this port so quickly that she
vanished while Society was looking for its
card-case. Like her brother, the much-
talked-about Duke of Orleans, the d’Aosta
dame has the Bourbon strain in her veins.
France has no. time for the male Orleans,
and has twice requested the pleasure of his
absence from Paris.
One of the chief attractions of the Shaks-
peare Festival in the Town Hall this week
is the stocks. A special disadvantage of
kicking over law in the Elizabethan period—-
and of course much later —was the incon-
venience of losing an ear or two and giving
the misfortune undue publicity -in ye olde
Englishe stocks. Two volunteers, who de-
cline to give their names, are sitting in the
Town Hall stocks turn and turn about.
Wandering minstrels will take up the collec-
tion at this crisis. Living chess is navi-
gated from the platform to suitable music.
A champion and ex-champion play ye olde
game, while a herald yells the moves to the
living knights, queens and pawns on the-
floor. When a queen retires she goes off
in tears to a melancholy growl from the
musicians’ instruments. The knights go off
in a huff. As for the pawns, I suppose the
band wails the “Three Golden Balls,” dead
march in chess.
Vivid shades of our old friend navy-blue
put up a good record during last week. Pretty
Mrs. Willie Andersen, with a dash of yellow
silk round her waist, was one of the nicest-
looking young persons in blue at the Rand-
wick crushes.
M.L. Tourist-Agent H. J. Manson passed
this way for Melbourne last week. In addi-
tion to his Government billet the man from
Wellington wears a new tail as Trade Com-
missioner for Maoriland.
The British Association which slogs
steadily at the advancement of Science, is
getting up its learned muscle for the August
invasion of this hamlet. Organising sec.
Dr. A. C. Rivett is rushing this way to knock
preliminaries into shape. He is the man
who has recently been in touch with the heavy
hand of Science in England. He leaves Mel-
bourne to shed the light of his countenance
upon us in a few days. Later he makes a
hurried trip to Brisbane. Our own Botanic
Gardens J. H. Maiden is putting in a solid
slab of advance work as hon. sec. of the
N.S.W. committee.
Mr. William Stuart, a Darling Point mag-
nate, petrolled up to Kosciusko with a. party
on April 18. The old mountain is becoming
respectable nowadays. There was a time
when many good old State Frighters wouldn’t
be seen in its company, lest they should be
suspected of “Victorian sympathies,” but now
they talk to it quite affably.
Artists Ethel Stephens and Dorothea Bar-
clay are exhibiting their wares this week.
Their studio in Kirkton-road (Darlinghurst)
is lined with samples of their brush and
chisel work.
H. N. Southwell, writing from Colombo, re-
ports all’s well and health improving.
Southie muses that his sun-tanned skin in-
cited the local natives to hail him joyfully as
“Father.” A longer stay in that beggar-
haunted settlement would remove Southie’s
illusion. After a tireless horde of native
mendicants have chased evey stranger’s ’rick-
shaw, like a flock of raucous crows, with
“This is my Pa-pa—this is Ma-ma” —the
hardened traveller would prefer to be a de-
ceased grandfather for a change. Sometimes
he feels like an uncle, and a very extor-
tionate, pawnbrokerish kind of uncle at that.
Mrs. Helen Jerome, a writer of graceful
verse, has returned to Sydney after a year or
so on the other side.
The Pierrot Ball, in aid of the old ladies’
Twilight Home, showed some very thin places
in its board of management. It was adver-
tised in such a slack style (apart from the
generous largesse of free pijffs in the social
columns) that various inqmsitive people who
searched the amusement ads. for days before-
hand failed to locate any mention of the
event. As the night was full of rain, and
howled with savage austs of wind, that
sounded the same as bears in caves, they
stayed at home as a cheap luxury. One irate
searcher for facts declares that she did locate
a Pierrot somewhere, but, as it didn’t conde-
scend to explain either the date or the loca-
tion, she concluded it was a semi-private
affair, and galleryites were to be severely dis-
couraged.
The Druitt-street entrance to the Town
Hall unfolded its portals about a quarter to
eight on the eventless night. A score or two,
or more, of very damp sightseers had huddled
up against its bleak, closed doors since 7.30
p.m. They made a joyous bound into dry
weather inside, to be turned back. Only
ticket-holders could pass on to the gallery.
The more stubborn refused to camp on the
wet tiles outside till someone who was re-
lated to someone on the committee—some
third brother once removed, or half-grand-
mother by a previous marriage—should come
along and sell the necessary passports. Half-
crowns were dross. No official would touch
them. Presently one woman guarding a few
tickets did appear. That portion of the wet
public which was nearest to her fell upon her
treasure and got rid of their half-crowns.
Then followed another deadly wait. Impati-
ent souls fled homeward. The dogged deter-
mination of women ficm remote suburbs,
however, saw them througn. After standing
from 7.30 p.m. till 8.15, one group was able
to buy gallery tickets —as someone chose to
come along and sell them —and stormed the
staircase with fierce and threatening remarks.
No coffee was available upstairs for the
rained-on waiters by the gloomy portal. If
too many cooks spoil the broth, too many
Pierrot balls would hash the temper.
After slam-whanging every Perfect Lady
on the Twilight Home Ball committee, the
gallery spectators sat down to see what was
to be seen, while they squeezed the rain out
of their wet feet. Decorations were attrac-
tive. Hanging yellow balls, yellow flowers,
and daffodil-tinted lights soothed the savage
beast—l mean breast. The Pierrot sets
varied from good to worse.
The Marie Antoinette lot were about the
best. The girls in fussy white satin paniers,
and all white effects —powdered hair, etc. —
were very well designed, and Miss Mar-
jorie Street, who pushed the idea into shape,
is here handed a large bouquet. She led off
with Mag-Captain Radcliffe, a most joyful
tar, who made things hum hereabouts.
li.M.A.S. Australia supplied all the male
persons in this lively set. Miss Georgie
Neild’s black velvet eyes sparkled under the
brim of her pert little white hat as she
stepped out amongst the Maries, with her
sailor-man Vaughan Lewis, R.N., in the
offing. Some large and baggy rose-pink
Pierrots led forth their Pierrettes —same
roomy cut about the bloomers —to dance.
Mrs. Macarthur-Onslow looked like an
amiable baa-lamb under a fluffy white wig,
that was exactly like bleached wool in the
distance. Ruth Lumsdaine was an emerald
green Pierrette in Mrs. Wesche’s set. But
too much Pierrot is a weariness to the eye.
The long willow-wand figure of Mrs. Neville
Mayman, draped in satin like an old gold
sunset, was a soothing sight after an acre
or two of the garments Mrs. Amelia Bloomer
wanted us to wear for Sunday best.
Captain Anderson, an Imperial man who
has been lent or exchanged to the Australian
forces, married a Roumanian girl, Miss Alex-
andra Ganesco, on a recent Saturday in Mel-
bourne. They were fellow-passengers to these
shores on one of the big ocean liners a few
weeks ago.
The M.L. Government Tourist Agent
in Sydney, W. R. Blow, who occa-
sionally uplifts a pleasing tenor in
the evening hymn, has a brother named
Reginald who is growing extensive
baritone in London. The latter has been
picked to warble in a competition for the
Goldberry prize. This goes to a senior
student at the Royal Academy of music—
presumably the youth who can pump the
loudest supply of lung-power into his vocal
cords, for only bassos and baritones compete.
“Snowy” Baker, pausing in his breathless
pursuit of the French Adonis, Carpentier,
scribbled a postcard on the deck of the mam-
moth liner Lusitania. He says that, with
Mrs. Baker, he dropped into the Metropolitan
Opera House, New York, and there heard
Caruso.... At this moment the Atlantic
waves were careering over the huge ocean
craft. Our office hieroglyphic expert trans-
lates the other artists’ names as follows:
Johannes Smithiannus, Gift, Trump, High,
Low, Jack, the Game, Right Bower, Blot,
Smudge and No Trumps.” (Some of these
were the names of the Seven Sleepers of
Ephesus, but that is a mere coincidence.)
Star-lady Mary Proctor writes of a pleas-
ant cruise on the Otway. At Suez she made
the rushed flight to Cairo with some fellow-
voyagers, and basked by moonlight in the
shadow of the Sphinx. She was due in
London on March 28.
Manager Freddie Shipman has been track-
ing, per cable, the French-Canadian song-
stress, Eva Gauthier, to some tropic village in
Java. Result, a wire from the small charmer
accepting the offer of an engagement in the
Mischa Elman Australian tour. Eva packs
her high-heeled shoes and other frivolities
forthwith, and trips up the Mataram’s gang-
way. She is due here on May 21. Percy
Kahn, a London pianist, who has dashed up
and down the keyboard to loud applause dur-
ing the last three years, while Elman fiddled
his fastest, is to assist in his old place at the
piano.
Black glace, which recalls the shade of
Jane Austin, is a 1914 autumn revival.
Mere youngsters fold themselves in its
gloomy embrace this season, as the newest
old thing they can wear. A sombre four-
decker gown of this Sabbatarian-looking
material, scolloped in flounces, tripped into
the sacred circle of the members’ stand the
other day. And the girl inside it was only
somewhere in the teeny age.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 20, 1914.
My Dear Tarrie, —
By way of holding the Empire together,
State Governor Stanley opened the Rose
Show, on Wednesday last. He and his lady
are enthusiastic gardeners —the intense kind
that hunt snails by lantern-light, when the
borders are dripping with dew and the dis-
turbed creepers throw icy water down the
unprotected neck; consequently, they en-
joyed the fragrant job and were able to
comment intelligently when they wandered
round. Her Ex. has already inspected the
Botanical Gardens, under the guidance of
the curator, and expressed polite surprise at
the botanical completeness.
_ There is a tuppenny mystery about the
vice-regal set planned for the big Repertory
Club Ball, on May 6, but a few hints about
it have leaked out or fallen over the edge.
Lady Denman and Lady Stanley are
arranging the magnificent thing in unison,
and if it doesn’t alter the course of sub-
urban history it won’t be their fault. The
Australian Opera Leaguers are taking the
Wagner “Ring” for their set. The floor is
being tested by engineers to see if it will
stand the weight of the assemblage, and
the welkin is getting tuned up so that it will
ring properly. All people who yearn to be
in the tableaux are implored to register
early at a frivolous address, to wit, “the Ball
Office, Town Hall.” The large McCubbin
lady is collecting supper supplies from all
quarters. If there should be a siege of this
city before the festivity the Town Hall
would be a good place to storm and loot.
The intended decorative scheme of the ball-
room is described as unique. The West
Balcony will be reserved for guests, but the
Eastern and Northern roosts will be thrown
open to the Gaping Public at 10s. 6d. a
gape, with supper thrown or dashed in.
The hard South gallery will be five shillings
a seat.
The Quamby Club has acquired, captured
or enslaved the Stanley lady as a member,
and is politely saying “Yah!” to all rival
disorganisations. The triumphant club will
indulge in a mass meeting and party, on Fri-
day, May 15, when the pretty captive will
be gloated over.
Jennie Brenan has been appointed dan-
cing-mistress to the three Stanley infants.
Their mother admits to being an admirer of
ballet dancing—or the grace that comes of
it —and she wants her eldest girl to strive
after a Genee style of daintiness. Jennie
(who is an ex-pupil of Genre’s uncle-instruc-
tor) also teaches the small Denmans how to
leap into the air and come down again with-
out stamping on their hands.
The second session of the Fifth Common-
wealth Parliament was opened on Wednes-
day last with so much pomp and circum-
stance that one really might have been ex-
cused for thinking Cook and Fisher had
buried the ass’s jawbone and were going to
do something useful. But it was only the pan-
tomime prelude to the same old untidy
wrangle. The same old crowd of idle,
well-dressed and politically ignorant
females squandered the best part of
the day in order to make sure of
seeing the fancy-dress parade, which was
practically the same as all the other parades
that have preceded it. Lady Denman, who
came along to w_atch her husband make his
farewell apeparance, was under a melo-
dramatic black hat, large and heavily
plumed. I forget what her dress was like.
The little Stanley lady was with her; but
her garments escaped my notice. These
two seem to play in each other’s distin-
guished backyards very amiably, and when
they are in a frivolous mood each climbs the
other’s apple tree and one chases the
other’s dog. The Barttelot lady was
in a sort of fire brigade costume, and
the Gaud Mayoress struck a vivid note
with waratahs on black plush. I will
not weary vou, my dear Yarrie, with
a lengthy description of the ceremonial futili-
ties and the numbers of feathers on view.
One fact I will tell you—we wound up as
usual with tea. Not with arsenic or cyan-
ide of potassium, mind you, but plain, harm-
less tea. So we all escaped, dear, and next
year we will do it all over again.
Pligh Conditioner Reid’s sister, Mrs. York
Svme, has been driving the Harry Lauder
couple round the country behind her stout
horses. She is giving a party for Mrs. L.
at the end of the week, and the fatted bag-
pipes will be slain in her honor. The
hostess-elect has a nephew or somebody at
Cambridge who is very friendly with the
Lauder son and heir. The young man is either
learning something or other, or clearing his
mind of knowledge acquired elsewhere. It
is very hard to say what these people do at
Cambridge.
Rupert Clarke starts off for his Papuan
adventures this week. His specially de-
signed and armed motor-boat is a sort of
Fly River ironclad —or wireclad —and it will
take a very ingenious savage to harm its
crew. A man who has just returned from
the mysterious river region says that the
little party of explorers will need all the
cover they are providing. The natives there
stick closer than a revenue stamp when they
take a fancy for a craft, and they are
clothed in cunning, if in nothing else. They
are probably one of the Ten Lost Tribes,
for they are hook-nosed and generally re-
semble the Israelites of Petticoat-lane, Lon-
don. Some little men with black, scraggy
lip-whiskers strongly resemble the Petticoat-
laners —or are what they might resemble if
all the clothes of Gad and Reuben were stolen
and they were bathed in coffee. They are
suspicious in their dealings, and won’t relax
their left-handed snap on a pineapple till the
right hand has closed over the stick of to-
bacco, or whatever the trade happens to be.
(Incidentally it might be remarked that an
absolutely new Jew or Cohen—even a naked
brown one —would be a blessing to the
thriftless Premier Holman, of N.S.W., who
appears to have financially exhausted all the
known Jews.) Traders have ventured up as
far as 30 miles, and still found 40ft. of water
under their boats. The naval tactics of the
brown river-men are clever. The defenders’
charging canoe is sent bows on down the
stream, and only one native is visible to the
defenders. He stands in the bow launching
spears and bad language, and, as soon as he
is potted he is bundled overboard and an-
other is in his place. Extra murderers swim
alongside under water. These people should
at least provide the adventurous Rupert
with a little entertainment; but if he opens
up a new Jew Holman will be extremely
grateful to him.
Heiress Marie Hall is giving a big Plaza
party as her first social outbreak since her
return. The invitations to girls include an
appeal or admonition: “Bring your own
tango partner.” The trouble about a 73-
step tango is that the average dancer can
only manage it with her own special partner
with whom she has practised the contortions.
Two tangoists are hopelessly at sea, unless
they have had rehearsals beforehand,
and even when they know exactly
what they mean to do their faces betray the
haunting fear that they may have forgotten
some of the lesson. Professional tangoists
rarely look quite joyful. The amateurs are
sicklied over with the pale cast of thought
all the time.
Lawyer Waxman, of this city, won the
Bowls Championship of Australia at the
Brisbane trundling. The slim Waxman does
not look like a confirmed bowler. He is a
living demonstration of the fact that it isn’t
necessary to go five laps to the mile round
the waist to excel at the devotion.
The Spinners’ Society is making a buzzing
sound just now. Mrs. Harper is the presi-
dent and her seconds are Mrs. Arthur Robb
and the Allen dame. They a , et together in
the morning to draw up the ground plans of
pinafores, etc., for the defenceless poor. For-
merly they used to assemble in the after-
noons ; but social engagements are so fre-
quent nowadays that the meeting-time was
altered to a less fashionable morning. The
Scotchbyterian Hall, Toorak, is their haunt,
and_ I mention the fact so that you may
avoid the place and not interrupt the good
work —at least I suppose it is good work.
Mrs. Hossen-Loff. who is off with hubby
to Europe for six months, indulged her
friends with tea, music and chatter in the
upper room of the Paris Caf6, before she
and her luggage got away. The Gaud
Mayoress, in black, was a guest of honor,
and Miss Maxwell and Mr. Morgan danced
a maxixe up and down the apartment. The
tall hostess added to her stature with long
black plumes, and wore the latest edition
of black moire and cloth.
Millionaire Carnegie, whose largest per-
sonal advertisement is the poor old Palace
of Peace at the Hague, is getting it fur-
nished free of charge by the fighting
nations, the peaceful nations being too
poor or too dead to help. The Common-
wealth is supplying a table of inlaid woods
and metals, which has been on view in a
Bourke-street window. Twelve Australian
woods are represented. The drawers have
handles of Broken Hill silver wrought into
the shape of ’possums, the locks are of local
manufacture, and Australian gold and opals
are set in the beautifully polished surface.
The “Ted” (K.C.) Mitchells have ac-
quired Brookong, the late Mrs. Kinnear’s
house at the Toorak tram terminus. But
alas! the once-farnous garden is to be sat
upon by a red-brick villa, while the picture
gallery is to be turned into a home two-step
apartment.
Marjorie, the Norman Wilsons’ girleen, is
off to Holland to stay awhile with sister
Mollie, who wedded a Dutchman, settled in
the Hague, and is now the mother of a real
Dutch baby that drinks schnapps and smokes
cigars all day.
The Harry Creswicks are off to display
their Australian-born infant to relatives in
Scotland, which is the country Mrs. Harry
sprang or jumped from.
The handsome Creswell dame is back in
Melbourne from Adelaide whither she went
to see a sick mother. She has literary
ability, and, but for her paralysing state of
affluence, might have tried to earn a living
with her pen.
Madge, the elder of Dr. Lillie’s captivating
twin daughters, has swooped oft the Society
perch into the barnyard of hat architects.
Her partner is Miss Nankivell. Two other
superior spinsters are said to be contemplat-
ing the same move.
Zara Morris, a pretty Melbourne brunette,
has had some newly : discovered waterfall in
the fashionable Lome district called after
her. It will be refreshing in the dog days to
remember that down in the heart of the
forest a fern-bordered cataract is keeping
your memory green as it splashes towards
the sea. If Zara lives to be very, very an-
cient and is wheeled to the Falls in a bath-
chair, it may interest the young tourists of
the moment to hear her burble over the
beauteous work of Nature and cry “That’s
me!”
The Mount Ivoroit Kirbys are mourning
for daughter Lila, a big, fair, athletic girl
in her earliest twenties, who died suddenly
after a few days’ illness. Mr. Kirby is at
present on a bed of sickness, so the home is
a very sad one.
The Lohengrin set, now being marshalled
for the Repertory Theatre Ball, is growing
daily in size, for the intensely musical set
realise that Pinschof himself will lead it in
person as one of the numerous gods.
Walter Withers occupies the Athen-
aeum at present, and the good old reputa-
tion of the artist (whom age withers nob) is
luring crowds up the beastly grey stairs.
The Best Fed enter panting and fling them-
selves into chairs; but the pictures on the
walls soon make them sit up. Withers is a
dreamy idealist with a genius for hard work,
and the result is a remarkable show of work
in many mediums. This year he is showing
crowds of small sketches instead of the big
canvases he used to produce. “Small pic-
tures,” he says, “can catch something fleet-
ing, while the big work needs too many
bites.’’ “Yes,” interrupts his business-like
wife, “and the small pictures sell freely.”
Th ' -xh.ibition, which opened on April 16,
will lie available for the usual fortnight.
Paul Dufault sang his way through four
concerts last week. This trip he explained
his sweet French songs “ver the footlights,
so. everybody understood what it was about.
His houses have been uniformly good—but
he has deserved them, his fine voice being at
its very best. Pauline Bindley, a local girl,
sang attractively in smart gowns. On
Thursday she wore a maroony-mauvey chiffon
over blue, and resembled a Blamire Young
impression of Evening. She towed a man-
ageable train, and didn’t trip or somersault
once. Harold Whittle, at the piano, played
the accompaniments even better than usual
—which is saying a heap.
Streeton returns to Australia within a
fortnight. He is to hold a show of work in
June at the Guild Hall, because the heavy
advance booking for the Upper Athenaeum
disqualifies the casual artistic looker-
in. Anyhow Streeton must feel well
disposed towards the Guild Hall, which
he “discovered” some years ago. On
that occasion he was safely billed for
the Athenaeum, but found at the last
minute that his beloved buff hessian
background screens could not be fitted in.
Sooner than do without them he careered
round town and secured the place at the far
end of the city, where the cohorts of the
Best, who had sought for him in vain at
the abandoned Athenaeum, found him prac-
tically unprepared to receive visitors. But
that didn’t deter them; they bought like
millionaires, Streeton cleared something like
£lOOO, and the artistic boom had started.
After that all the wandering Australian
artists gave a united whoop and charged like
dervishes from all points with canvases
under their arms. Nowadays nobody shows
stuff in Melbourne without selling some-
thing, and all the good men have made
money. Streeton has just finished the huge
panels for the. Commonwealth Building in
London. He sent word some weeks ago that
he would leave just as soon as that job was
through.
Artist Tom Carter, of Melbourne, sends
word from London that he is deeply im-
mersed in Arts and Crafts study. He speaks
worshipfully of the near presence of High
Priest Walter Crane and other leaders of
the A. & C. world.
McCubbin has just finished the Alfred
Deakin portrait for the Commonwealth gal-
lery, and Mrs. Mac is giving a party at the
Riverside house in order that friends may
drink tea before it.
From “Rosna,” in London:—
Oscar Asche told the members of the Playgoers’
Club, at their annual dinner, that the drama was
in a flourishing state in Australia. He attributed
the gratifying circumstance neither to the abounding
prosperity, nor to the esthetic taste of the Common-
wealth, but to the fact that “most Australians had to
dine at 6.30 p.m., it being illegal to employ cooks
after that hour , except in houses that had more
than one cook.” By the way, Asche, H. B. Irving,
Ethel Irving—in brief, all the stars who have shone
in Australia during the past half-dozen years—are
solid on the vast opportunity that awaits a new
theatrical co., backed bv really big money, in Aus-
tralasia. The practical monopoly which is held by
the J. C. Williamson firm at present wouldn’t endure
for 12 months if Frohman or someone of that calibre,
butted into the business. The big actors wish that
something of the sort would happen. They like
coming to Australia, despite the Cook problem and
other mostly bogus disabilities.
The recently-announced engagement of Noel Bux-
ton, son of a former S.A. vice-royalty, and Miss
Lucy Pelham Burn, cloaks a political romance. He
is Liberal M.P. for North Norfolk, and the lady, a
member of a sternly Conservative clan, had been
an ardent worker against his candidature for the
constituency. She had also organised a petition against
the Liberal party’s Insurance Act, which same peti-
tion Mr. Buxton had jeered at in public with more
gusto than civility.
Then they became associated in a scheme for aid-
ing non-combatants in the Balkans, or discovering
needles in bundles of hay or something, and almost
before Society realised that they were on speaking
terms they were engaged.
Some anonymous lunatic, who says he has just re-
turned from “a tour of the Empire,” purports to
have discovered “a passionate and extraordinarily
widespiead desire that Queen Alexandra and the
Prince of Wales should visit Australia and Maoriland
incognito.” The London Daily Graphic duly printed
the pathetic nonsense. Said the travelled idiot: —
“At dozens of places, by scores of old friends, I
was asked to make this wish known in Britain. If
Queen Alexandra and her grandson will go out to the
Southern Dominions in one of our warships they will
give the keenest happiness and delight to the people.
Why, in Australia alone there are ZOO towns and
villages named after Queen Alexandra.”
The Australian Year Book for 1913 gives in its
gazetteer—wherein are mentioned hamlets containing
populations as insigniiicant as 55 (Winchendon Vale,
N.S.W.) and 59 (Colosseum, Q.) —one Alexandria
(N.S.W.), one Alexandra (Vic.), and one Alexander
River (W.A.) —the latter a post town with a popu-
lation, seemingly, of nil. Neither Tasmania, Queensland
nor South Aus. possesses any city, town, village, hamlet
or what not the name of which can by any stretch
of the most infuriatedly Imperialistic imagination be
twisted into the remotest likeness to the name Alex-
andra. But the London papers never bother about
such trifling details. I have come to the conclusion
that the Jingo press of Britain would rather have its
leg pulled than eat.
Class is decided at the Palais de Danse
by your headgear. If you are just “a young
person,” you dance without a hat. If you
are sporty, you swathe your hair with rib-
bon velvet. If you want to be considered
smart, you dance in your hat. (Not on it,
mind!) It doesn’t matter if the feathers
brush the ceiling lights, or an extinguisher
velvet crown blots out your face and makes
you look headless; you solemnly take the
floor with the tile firmly clamped to your
hair. The Palais has apparently come to
stay. Languid young men, or young men
who are clumsy and nervous, need only dance
if they want to. No duty dances cast shadows
over their evenings, and the worried hostess
with the antique partner in tow is an un-
known horror.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
A crowd of 30,000 and a totalisator of £31,000
were the chief features of this year’s Onkaparinga
meeting. Other features were rotten roads, pleasant
weather, good racing and Kelvin’s £33 dividend.
Incidentally, Mirabilite returned £lO 12s. in the
Trial Stakes.
Society was there in force and motors, the female
section of it much annoyed at the absence of a
lady’s tote. The men were, if possible, even more
annoyed. They had, owing to this disability, to
fracture the even tenor of their own betting by
adding their women-friends’ half-crowns to their
sovereigns or fivers, or whatever the sum was.
Most of the Melbourne poloists were in the throng.
They were feasted by the Rosebank Melroses; also
the C. H. Angases, who were lunching the Alec
Murrays and the Alec Murrays’ pretty daughter; the
J. H. Grahams and the John Corbins, who came late
and left early ; a sprinkling of Milnes and Stirlings,
the Waterhouses, and Mrs. Herbert Rymill. Coats
and skirts were everywhere, and most of them were
of navy-blue serge, which, by the way, is unsightly
wear when the dust is six inches deep underfoot and
heavy in the air.
The John Darling estate hasn’t been lodged for
probate yet, but fairly authentic rumor puts it down
at anything from £750,000 to £1,000,000. Mrs.
McKinlay’s estate has been sworn or denounced or
objurgated at £55,000.
On Wednesday the Ways asked about 17,000
people to afternoon tea in the Government House
garden, and about 14,000 of the 17,000 turned up.
The hostess was in black, with garnishings of ninon
and Limerick lace, and Mrs. Herbert Rymill wore a
successful garment of delphinium blue and a black
hat. On the whole, dressing was mixed, and hovered
between lingering summer and dawning winter —
which reminds me that one unseasonable dame swel-
tered in a sable coat at Onkaparinga. Mrs. H.
Martin inhabited a smartly tailored black and white
check, and Miss Peacock’s mole cr£pe de chine was
a dream. Mrs. Julian Ayers and Mrs. Fenner and
Mrs. Lendon and her daughter were all enviably
clad; and parsons, members of Parliament and
heads of departments were thick as thie—-I mean,
flies. Also, I discerned Mr. Ligert Wood (who is
the Chief Justice’s Associate as well as the Lieu-
tenant-Governor’s A.D.0.), Captain Fletcher and
Major Connor. These three did a lot of unobtrusive,
but none the less heroic, work, in the way of showing
the visitors the way to the food.
The Ways’ term of office is up to-day, and already
the fickle crowd may be heard murmuring “Vive les
Galways”—that is those of them who have doings
with the Alliance Frangaise. Our new rulers arrived
at the outer harbor punctually to time, and were
welcomed on the spot by the‘Premier. They were
welcomed again at the Adelaide station by the
Mayor, and again at Government House by the
Ways. And then came the welcome at the Town
Hall at 3 o’clock. They both bore up fairly well —
more especially Lady Galway. She dresses well, and
has a delightful smile. The woman who can smile
delightfully after four official welcomes is some goods
surely, as they say in the U.S.
The Mormons are here. In point of fact, it appears
that they have been in our midst for 40 years,
during which period they have raked in 111 adher-
ents more than two converts per annum. They
have seven “missionaries” in S.A., and aspire to build
a church. They are not polygamists, and don’t want
to lure people to Utah. It all sounds very dull.
Also, it strikes one as out of place. Why do they
call themselves Mormons when it is evident, in
reality, that they are merely Methodies, or some
such stuffy people? It is like a particularly tame
bank-clerk styling himself Bandit Bill or Cut-throat
Algernon.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNS.

ABORIGIN- ALITIES
Central : Re “Gouger’s” par. (B.
5/3/’l4) in which he mentions a drunk made by
hanging up a bunch of bananas and catching the
juice when they ferment. It is commonly known as
banana rum,” but if “Gouger” wants a more potent
tangle let him procure a fair-sized pineapple, and
after having peeled or skinned it (whichever he likes)
cut it up and put it into an ordinary big jug, shy in
a breakfast cup full of sugar (not the cup,' too), and
fill up the void with good rain water. Let the mix-
ture stand for five or six days, until it ferments a
treat, bottle the stuff and cork well, being very care-
ful to anchor ’ the bottle. The result will be ready
for consumption in, say, four days. But the longer
the jnerrier. Pull tlic cork and drink “while on the
boil.” I’ll leave the rest to “Gouger.”

“Marloo”: When the North Coast
(N.S.W.) cocky really needs rain nothing but a
deluge approaching the one which swamped out old
Noah will satisfy him. The sun had hardly peeped
out after the recent downpour when many dairymen,
on whose land about a foot or so of liquid had
fallen, bellowed to Heaven that the drought was
not sufficiently fractured, and they prayed that more
might descend. And theirs is not wholly a hollow
complaint. The water runs off those lands very
quickly, and is whisked away by numerous creeks
before it has time to soak in. The reason for this
appears to lie in the lack of depth of earth above
the clayey sub-soil. Furthermore, during the hot,
dry weather the earth becomes serried by deep,
gaping fissures, till one would think a drunken earth-
quake had swept across the land. Down these
cracks an enormous quantity of water flows to un-
known depths. Saw a farmer, before the deluge,
satisfying the thirst of one of these fissures. It
closed its mouth after absorbing 30,000 gallons
from his irrigating plant.
The fool who peels off his clothes and
swims out to some distant rock when no one is
about nearly met the fate he deserves at Tauranga
(M.L.) the other day. After being on the rock for
about half a day waving and veiling for help he
was seen, but the sea was then so rough that no
boat could live in it. When the waves went down
a little the harbor-master tried to go out to the
tiny island, but the boat was swamped and the crew
only saved through their lifebelts. There was good
shelter on the island, but no tucker. After three
days of rough weather the naked Crusoe was rescued,
little the worse for the experience. That’s generallv the
wa '. Usually about three good men get drowned
trying to rescue one fool, and that fool, eve dually
comes out of the tight corner in good condition.

Q. ’ : YV hen I fetched up at one of the
main lighthouses on the Queensland coast it hap-
pened that the lightkeepers’ womenfolk had left the
place temporarily, and the two keepers were keeping
house. I found a fierce dispute on as to which' l man
cooked the best damper. Bets were made for pro-
digious amounts, and I was to be judge. The head
keeper was cook on the first day, and his effort was
only passable. llis assistant promised to beat his
damper by streets. Next morning a local fisherman
strolled up to the Head, and asked me what Jim
(the assistant) was up to. Jim, it seemed, was down
at the fishermen’s camp, and old Bill, the patriarch
of the camp, was teaching him how to fix up a real
damper. (Bill was famous all along the Barrier lleef
as a cook.) The informer had come up for some
flour with which to perpetrate Bill’s swindle. The
boss, promptly offered to get the flour, and in due
course the damper appeared on the table. Its looks
did old Bill credit, and the boss was loud in praise
(of Jim) when the triumph was uncovered. He then
started to cut it up. “These knives are damn blunt,”
he grunted, as he tried first with one and then
another to hack off a slice. “Here, Jim, you try.”
Jim wasn’t able to do anything but turn up the
* cary i n to'knife. “Funny damper
that, remarked the boss. “Reminds me of a fisher-
man s damper—mostly sand.” Jim looked sus-
piciously at his-superior, picked up bis damper and
went out to look for old Bill. On his departure I
asked for an explanation. The head-keeper had dosed
the flour liberally with plaster of Paris, and Jim had
cooked a grindstone.
“Eddyson”: Happy Valley, is a. Vic. health
resort, boasting a salubrious climate and a mineral
spring the waters of which smell like bilge after a
two years cruise. There were about 20 guests at
Green House, Happy Valley, Paul Perrin among the
rest. Twelve of these had been invited to the fanev-
dress ball at Boland's homestead, Paul Perrin in-
cluded Paul was an habitual tenor—a plump warbler
with shins like a stork, a large, pink face, and
profuse, black, curling locks. He prided himself
on.his ability to impersonate fat females, and made
up an excellent representation of the late Wilson
Barrett s grandmother. It was in this striking char-
acter that Paul elected to appear at Boland’s frivol
on the night of March 12, but P. Perrin was so
long titivating his figure and sweetening his expres-
sion and complexion that the rest of the party
drove off without him, leaving him the option of
walking or wasting his sweetness on the desert air.
aul walked, and, being a city-bred tenor with small
bushcraft, mixed his bearings so inextricably that
by 10 p.m. he found himself a lost, lorn female
in the general desolation. He was a hopeless stray
entirely surrounded by trees, struggling on in his
hampering garments, without knowledge of whence
or whither, and the farther he went the more sud-
denly he came to nowhere. At about six o’clock
next morning Jim Fisher, conductor-in-chief of
three teams taking machinery up to the Nip-and-Tuck
came upon a large, bedraggled woman collapsed in
the shelter of a rock. Fisher gathered her up with
what ceremony he had knowledge of, and wrestled
her back to camp in short stages. There four other
rough men, their sympathies stirred to the deepest
jy the spectacle of feminine distress, with its feet
g'von out, and its hat despondingly over its eye
rushed to offer service. Fisher’s camp couldn’t do
too much for the poor, misplaced lady. It waited on
her hand and foot, it revived her with weak whisky
and comforted her with abundant tea. It warmed
l ee > , and restored her hat to a becoming
angle, cooked her a special breakfast, and was all
lomage and solicitude, giving her her meal on a
special bed made up of all the available bedding, and
behaving generally towards the limp dame like a
large family of brothers. In the earlier part of the
interview Fjsher even devoted 15 minutes to the task
of tenderly chafing the fat hand of the buxom waif
with a vague idea that that was the proper measure
to take to restore vitality, or geniality or something,
then the gallant company made up a sort of nest
for the hapless matron on the spring trap, and
Tisher drove her tenderly all the way to ? Green
Ilouse, Happy Valley, and delivered her into the
hands of the proprietress. “You’ll want to buck

“Matey”: Cannot tell “Masamis” (P>.
2(>/3/’l4) where the water hyacinth first' heaved Jts
petals above water. Can tell him it grows quicker
than the progeny of the rabbit grandfather. But what
about exploiting it? The plant has strong fibres, and
should be of great value for textile purposes. The
fibre from the stalk, after being dried, makes very fair
rope and twine, as well as coarse thread. It is tena-
cious and takes dye rapidly; also, it has been proved
to yield more fibre from the green stems (41 per
cent.) than sisal (3J to 4 per cent.). Why shouldn’t
that eternal skiter, Private Enterprise, test the hya-
cinth’s capacity for outing jute? How about drink-
ing to the new company’s health—The Hyacinth
Fibre and Textile Corporation, Ltd. ?

“Sarto” : I have often lain awake o’ nights
listening to the rehearsals of a Frogs’ Glee Club in an
adjacent waterhole. And grand company I found it
too on a dark, lonely night in the tent. Each
“croaker” seems to know when (and when not) to
croak, under the experienced baton, I suppose, of some
old Bully Glee Master. I would strongly ad.vise anyone
suffering from insomnia to catch about four frogs,
teach them to croak in turn, put them under his bed,
and then try to lie awake. The other night I went
out to see if there was any chance of Parliament
dissolving, or rather of the weather breaking up,
when I trod on something soft. I looked round to
find the tiny child that began to cry just then, .when
the frog hopped away and the crying ceased. What
I have often mistaken for the faint crying of a little
child has turned out to come from nothing but a
big frog, llow, when, where or why they do it I
know not.
* * * *
“A.M.L.”: The aboriginals on the McAr-
thur Kiver (N. Territory) had never seen an engine
working. At a small saw-mill there the driver re-
cently fixed a broken old skull on top of the whistle.
Some natives gathering round he ’called their attention
to it, and pulled the cord. The skull started bobbing
up and down, with steam coming out of it in all
directions, and letting out, as they thought, a hideous
screech. The tribe gave one horrified glance at it
and started running. A few days afterwards some of
its members passed through Daly Waters still run-
ning, and a week later a few shadowy figures were
seen to dive into the water at Wyndham, holding
their hands to their ears as if to shut out the howls
of the 12 five-legged bulldogs of Sheol.

“The Pariah” : In 1910 I wrote thus to
The Bulletin concerning the planting of the Otway
(Vic.) sand-drift with marram: “It is debated
whether the sand will beat the marram or vice
versa.” In conversation with an official who has just
been through on a tour of inspection I learned that
the marram won in the third year, and that inland
the drift is flattening out. (It used to reach 60ft.
in height.) Where the shifting ford on the Aire
Kiver used to keep the traveller's nerves on edge is
a summer depth of 10ft. This has cleared the” bar
at the mouth of the river. As a result, two blocks
in the Calder swamp, round which we found it im-
possible to measure more than half-way, are now
drained and growing strawberry clover. I’ve often
heard the utility of marram grass debated, hence
this par.
Oibi” : In the boom days of the agricul-
tural industry in Papua, estate managers experi-
mented considerably with the growth of minor pro-
ducts of other tropical countries. The most success-
ful of these scientists was in charge of a plantation
not many miles distant from Port Moresby. He
allowed himself to believe that he had discovered a
valuable specie of what he termed “ramie fibre.”
Exercising the greatest attention to his crop in the
nurseries, his efforts were rewarded with pronounced
success. As to whether he ever planted out a large
area of his newly-found fibre I am at a loss to say,
but there is now spread all along the roads of the
district innumerable acres of this never-to-be-suffi-
ciently-accursed commodity. So dense is the crop
that I am sure the pioneer would be elated over the
success of his carefully-nursed plant of a short de-
cade back. But he has fled to avoid the multitude
of popular applause and left his glory behind him.
The awful thing wasn’t ramie or anything resembling
it-

The gay gums wave their green umbrellas—
Green umbrellas that seek the sky.
Out. of the branches green rosellas—
Pantaloons of the free air fly.
Over the green umbrellas swaying,
Offering shelter, safety, ease,
Fly the birds, while an earth-thing braying
Talks at length of “them ugly trees.”
The tall gums wave their green umbrellas—
Wave them gaily on ev’ry breeze;
While an alderman talks to his “fellers”
Somewhere down by their well-browned knees.
He bulges out where no bulge should be;
He sags—yet seems inclined to burst
And urges death for each queenly tree
W hen he, ’tis plain, should be murdered first.
N.S.W.

South Australia, through an unusually hot
and dry autumn, is having its worst vintage for years,
probably little more than half as good as that of
1913. It will pretty certainly be less than 2J
million gallons of wine, and if the distillers use up
their usual two millions the balance is trifling.
Anyway, the home market will not get it. The big
makers say that the one thing which London won’t
stand is any break in continuity of supplies, and,
sooner than damage the connection they have built
up there they will let the land that grows the stuff
go without.
“8.8. R.”: He was a bad cook. In fact. I
don’t think he was a cook at all. On the electoral
roll he was described as a piano-tuner. As a piano-
tuner I knew nothing of his abilities. As a cook
he was a blank. Also he was careless, not to say
dirty. He would not make yeast bread. We stood
his second-class dampers for a couple of days, and
then sent our representative to him. The piano
cook listened patiently to the rep’s timid plea for
yeast bread and then consigned the rep. to the Eternal
Bakehouse. The next day, when everyone except his
nibs was up at the shed, a travelling cook who was
looking for work called at the hut. As soon as our
operator scented a possible rival he hurried along
with the information that he’had changed his mind
and would make the bread. So far so good ; and
the wandering cook wandered on. Yeast bread takes
time to make, and when no bread turned up the
next day nothing was said ; but when the bread
famine continued into the second day we thought
that it was time to act. When the rep. tackled the
cook the reply he got was that his (the cook’s) was
a very changeable disposition, and on second thoughts
he had decided not to make the bread. Then we just
had to sack him. However he bore no ill towards
us, and said he would leave us plenty of tucker to
go on with. He kept his word. He got hold of the
galvanised iron washing tub —it held about 10 gallons
—and rinsing it out, at least I suppose he rinsed it
out, he left it full of blanc-mange.

“Maorilandlubber”: A M.L. farmer of my
acquaintance was approached the other day by his
grown-up son and requested to spar up some money.
“Whatter y’ want money for?” said the old bloque,
controlling himself with an effort. “Wanter buy a
watch,” was the answer. “A watch!” roared the un-
dutiful parent. “Don’t I call you when it’s day-
light? Aren’t you always called for meals? And
when it’s dark you know it’s time t-o knock off!
What the L do you want a watch for ?”

the saddle."
“ Yes, his legs look like it."
“Leo Lear”: In rooting over the continent
for national subjects Abo-scribes have overlooked
the commonest, largest and most characteristic of
all the Australian bird families, namely, the honey-
eaters. Only one of the world’s 251 species is found
outside the Australian ornithological region ; and
it is somewhere in the East Indies. Nature worked
overtime in moulding the honey-eaters, and turned
out a product with a brush tongue and a long,
curved bill to enable the owner to collect honey from
the indigenous flowering gums. The family is such a
varied and widely distributed one
that it naturally contains beauty and
plainness, harshness and song. The
commonest of all is probably the
noisy minah, a true honey-eater,
though, like most of his cousins, he
lives not by sweets alone, , but pur-
sueth the insect to his cost. The
wattle-bird (the largest of the family)
and the uncouth-voiced leather-head
are among the most unattractive
members, especially in the vocal line.
But who, on seeing the rich greens
or bright yellows of the white-
bearded, yellow-tufted, helmeted, or
rare Regent honey-eaters hasn’t re-
joiced ? The rich chestnut of the
unique spine-bill (him of the long,
curved proboscis) and the olive of
the bellbird cry for acknowledgment
from good Australians; but for
beauty my vote goes to the gorgeous
scarlet of the sanguineous honey-eater.
In the red bottle-brush flowering
shrubs of Queensland watercourses I’ve
seen this bird in hundreds; though,
but for his sweet song he’d pass undetected on the
bright blossoms he sucks. Most of the honey-eating
tribe build open, hanging nests; but one, the blue-
face, hr.s learnt the value of prepared maternity hos-
pitals, and Mrs. Blue-face retires to the deserted
nest of a babbler when the baby is expected. As
for song, I admit the family contains no Melba.
Still the bellbird heads an operatic troupe far above
the ruck in the bird line. The clear, ringing notes
of the “Greenie,” yellow-plumed, singing and many
other species are typically Australian; and call for
more than passing notice, even in a land where the
greater percentage of birds would be found in the
front row of the chorus, were all the planet’s
feathered tribe lined up at the Ballarat competitions
for adjudication.
The faithful dog once more. A N.S.W.
ploughman, who didn’t like the taste of his sardine
lunch, threw it to his tyke, which obliged him by
eating the lot. Shortly afterwards the animal demon-
strated what might have happened to the ploughman
by expiring. Inscription for the tombstone: “Here
Lies Fido. Without Exactly Intending to do so, he
laid down his Tummy on the Altar of Friendship.”
