Mr. J. G. Balsillie has applied to the patents
office for provisional letters for his scheme of rain
production. Should the patent be granted, and the
tests prove satisfactory, it is proposed to form a
company.
“Nobody ever thought of floating ME into a
company.”
THE COASTAL COCKY’S LAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Further particulars of the floods at Wollongong
show that thousands of pounds’ worth of damage
has been done. Many families were swamped out,
and numbers of people are homeless. The damage to
Bode’s Hotel is estimated roughly at £lOOO. Many
cows and pigs and large numbers of poultry were
washed away. —Daily paper.
It used to be the curse of drought,
But now it’s too much rain,
That helps to knock the cocky out,
And prove his toil in vain.
Last year he put his faith in spuds,
To make a little pot,
Alas, the rain came down in floods
And gave them all the rot!
The land he grubbed so carefully,
And sowed with grass last week,
Is sliding eastward to the sea,
Along the roaring creek.
The flats are white with new-born lambs
Which perished in the storm,
That swept away the new-made dams
The cocky delved to form.
And every day it’s growing worse;
The sun still holds aloof,
And there’s no sound that makes him curse
Like rain upon the roof.
Too wet to plough, too wet to fence,
Too wet for anything,
He prays the rain will have the sense
To hold up till the spring.
N.s.w.
CRIPPS CLARK.
The Superfluous Parasite and his Sub-
Parasite, or the Flea on his Tail.
The ill-paid clerk was the subject of a recent
dissertation to the N.S.W. Premier, and
everything that was said about his hard lot
had justification behind it. The clerk is
a fertile subject for dissertation. A very
large section of him stands for the incarna-
tion of shabby gentility. He thins early
about the hair, and, too often, he has a
reach-me-down sort of look about the clothes.
He is a dweller in the cheaper suburbs—
rather mild, rather humble, rather Tory, not
madly athletic, and not given to exploration,
insurrection, battle, murder, sudden death,
wife-beating, brigandage, riot, incendiarism,
cattle-stealing, burglary, bushranging, or
piracy on the high seas. He avoids the big,
strenuous vices. History tells of a person
in a bank who developed into one of the most
strenuous generals in Europe, and of a solici-
tor’s clerk who rose to be the hardest soldier
and most uncompromising statesman in the
worried land of Mexico, but these were rare
exceptions. The average clerk is never any-
thing more than what he is, and never
expects to be. Yet, despite his grievances,
all the ill-luck doesn’t come his way. The
ill-paid office drudge is sometimes quite as
well off as his boss except in the one respect,
that, if anything does “turn up,” the boss
gets it, and the drudge doesn’t. But
then, for a certain class of boss nothing ever
turns up. Sometimes he gets along in a
sort of way all his days. Sometimes he
drifts aimlessly and seedily into the Insol-
vency Court, and states that the liabilities
aren’t much, and the assets, consisting chiefly
of the office table, are less. After a long
struggle with Fate, as an agent, or a com-
mission merchant, or an arbitrator, or a dog-
tax adjuster, or a small jobber in cottages and
allotments, or as Australian representative
of Briggs, who makes weepers for funeral
hatbands in Donegal (Ireland), or as the
strenuous local pusher for the very dubious
Threecoughsandasneeze Fire Insurance Co.
of Kherson (Russia), the rent or the gas
bill has bowled him out, and he has thrown
up what remains of the sponge, and hasn’t
thrown it very high, either. Even this
kind of employer seems generally able to find
a clerk, and very often the one thing that
would madden the kind of clerk that this kind
of employer finds would be for the old man
to take him to his bosom and give him a
half-share in the business instead of a small,
fixed remuneration. The boss is an unsuc-
cessful journeyman pirate' on the sea of com-
merce. but he has an office and a door-plate
because, however great a failure he may bo,
he has the instincts of the pirate. His clerk
is the unsuccessful supercargo down below,
and because his instincts are that way he
doesn’t want to come up on deck and begin
pirating on his own account. If it was
possible to have just as many merchants,
importers, financial agents, indent agents,
insurance agents, house agents, company
floaters, fruit-barrow men, bagmen, hawkers,
solicitors, shonkeepers, insurance companies,
banks and other mercantile strugglers as are
required to carry on the business of the com-
munity there would probably be no reason
why the average cauable, full-grown clerk
shouldn’t earn £5OO a year. But too much time
is spent by Jones’s clerk in the unsuccessful
struggle to snatch, for his own and his
employer’s benefit, a twopenny scrap of busi-
ness from the ravening jaws of Brown’s
clerk, while Simpson’s clerk cruises in the
offing with hostile intent. Society badly
needs to eliminate the superfluous city strug-
and force him to take to some produc-
tive occupation, so that the country may
grow more, and import less, and borrow less
money to pay for its imports, and do more
real work and not so much unreal hanging
around. The proportion of the Australian
population which is engaged in “commerce”
is over the odds, and commerce, when it is
finally resolved into its component parts, is
merely a combat to find out the largest num-
ber of people who can handle a pound of
tripe on its way from the cow to the con-
sumer. The least successful of the many
agents who try to intercept the tripe natur-
ally makes little, and his clerk, who wouldn’t
be his clerk save that he lacks even the enter-
prise or vigor to be a humble tripe inter-
ceptor on his own account, makes less.
Meanwhile there are altogether too many
finger-prints on the pound of tripe.
“Lantana”: N. S. Wales’s Minister for
Public Instruction has got his travelling-school well
started. Two of them are on the tracks, one in the
Hay district and the other out Inverell way. The
school consists of a four-wheeled van covered with
a hood. Provision is made for lockers and cupboards,
the doors of which form a false floor, and so serve
as a bed for the pedagogue. The equipment includes
blackboard, writing materials, school readers, maps,
atlases, kindergarten materials and records. One of
the schools has been in operation at Narrabri for
some time, and the one which started out from In-
verell last week will visit King’s Plains, Yetman,
Gravesend and other parts of the district, staying
from one to three weeks at each place. It will take
eight weeks to complete the circuit. Then the teacher
will return, revise work left during the previous
visit, give another week or two’s instruction, and set
work for the students to complete before his next
visit.

You wili certainly be healthier,
stronger, and more vigorous, if
you take a few weeks’ course of
Sanatogen.
“ Since taking Sanatogen, I am
really better—a brighter, stronger
tone altogether,’’writes Lady Muir.
Even after the first few doses—
which, by the way, we will send

notice a marked improvement in
your condition.
Your nervous system, in par-
ticular, will be wonderfully in-
vigorated. Witness this statement
by Madame Sarah Grand, the fa-
mous authoress of “ The Heavenly
Twins” and other well-known
novels:
“ I pin my faith to Sanatogen,
because it certainly restored me
to health in the worst nervous
break-down I ever had. It is a
building -up process, but not
‘ jerry-building’— the work is done
to last.”
In Digestive Troubles,

poverty of the blood are described

anaemic, has been greatly benefited
by the use of Sanatogen. Her
appetite improved, her weight in-

Equally remarkable are the ef-

were rosy, and her weight had
increased by 1-lb. 10-oz.”
Sanatogen is of the greatest
value before and after confinement,
because nothing is so effectual for
building up and strengthening
the system. It is also strongly
recommended for nursing mothers
to ensure that the milk shall be

Other uses of Sanatogen—for

Headache, and Insomnia, etc. —

One Good Friday.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh! Good Friday’s sodden buns,
And the booming Easter guns,
And the Show crowd tramping heavy through
the town !
Oh! the tents amongst the trees,
And the Easter tragedies!
So the pageant to Eternity goes down.
Ev’ry year it is the same;
In a manner limping—lame—
We repeat ourselves like IPist’ry—only
worse;
And I would that I were dead,
’Midst this substitute for bread,
When we waste again ’neath Custom’s dullest
curse.
But one past Good Friday stays,
In old Mem’ry’s thick’ning haze,
Like a landmark viewed from something far
at sea;
Twas the day the madman stayed,
And with maddest discourse made
Our bun luncheon last from mid-day until
tea.
I remember how we sat,
Each as silent as a bat,
While the evening shadows crept into the
room,
And each white, familiar face
leaded slowly in its place,
While the Talker gabbled faster in the gloom.
We were drunk and drugged with words
That flew from his mouth like birds —
Words of travel—words of visions—words of
fire;
He had laid on us a spell
Midst the buns that sag and swell,
And we listened in a silence drear and dire.
In that parlor without light.
At the threshold of the night,
When the joyless day was done, he talked no
more ;
And; bleak panic raised my hair,
At the rattle of a chair,
And the scraping of a match upon the door.
Though I’m older now and glad.
That Good Friday, dark and mad,
Stands up straightly like a beacon stiff and
stark;
So each year my mind must turn,
Though to newer things I yearn,
To that madman talking—talking in the
dark—
In the haunted, yawning, lost, back-parlor
dark.
N.S.W.
THE RELIGOUS EDITOR.
The Bob Blouse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rTI HE woman with the black eye and
skimpy clothes sat on her 'verandah,
sewing industriously. A few doors
away down the same side of the street
another woman leaned listlessly against the
door jamb, taking a tired view of the few
people who passed by. At last she came
along with her arms folded, and a purpose-
less, awkward walk, as if she had no ulterior
motive for her progression. Reaching the
other woman’s '!ou«e she leaned against the
verandah post.
“Sewin’?” she said.
The other woman looked up with a faint,
encouraging smile, nodded her head, and
then went on with her work. After a few
stitches she looked*up again and said:
“You’re the new woman who’s taken No.
5, ain’t you?”
“Yes.”
“Thought yer was. Do yer like the
’ouse?”
“It seems orright.”
“Oh, it’s orright, on’y it’s damp.”
She sewed awhile, and then said:
“Everybody who lives in it six munce is
buried with consumption, or can’t walk
with the rheumatics after.”
“It won’t matter to us,” said the new
woman at No. 5. “We never stay so long
in an ’ouse.” And then as an afterthought:
“What with rent en that.”
“What does yer ’usband do?”
“Bread carter.”
“Mine’s a blacksmith’s striker. What
wages do your ’usband get?”
“Thirty-five bob.”
“Mine gets more’n that, on’y for the kids
an’ bein’ out of work a good deal. Black-
smiths is funny.”
“How?”
“Since me ’usband got converted he don’t
last so long. You see, blacksmiths like a
striker who’ll join ’em in a drink at eleven
o’clock en when they knock off. It would
be orright if he could get a job with a
blacksmith that’s been converted. He’s a
good striker. Does your ’usband drink?”
“Sometimes,” answered the bread-carter’s
wife. “But he’s not so bad in that way.
Pie always gives me warning first so’s I can
be prepared.”
“My ’usband used to boose up everything
before he joined the Salvation Army.”
She stitched on for a while, and then laid
down her work on her lap and looked
thoughtful. “Sometimes I think that I liked
him better before he gave up sin. He usedn’t
to knock me about at all in them days.”
“Does he now?”
"Well, you see, its’ partly me own fault,
and ’e on’y does it when we have a barney.
En when me dander’s up I says things I
oughtn’t to. He’d like me to belong to the
Army too, but I stick to the church. Yer
see, if yer cuts a poor mouth with the
church ladies they often give yer some of
their fashionable clothes when they don’t
want ’em enny more, en ’e is always so
suspicious if I puts on ennything new.”
The other woman nodded understandingly.
“They’re like that when you’ve bin mar-
ried a year or two,” she said. “I’ve ’ad a
few shindies meself through the same thing.
It’s like tryin’ to get blood out of a stone
to get a new ’at. They reckons that you’ll
ruin ’em with your extravagance. Did he
do that?” —indicating the discolored eye. •
The woman nodded her head and then
went on rapidly with her sewing. In a
little while she laid her work down again.
"One of the church ladies give me an
’obble skirt last Saturday,” she said. “It
was real smart, en wanted no alterin’. He
didn’t see it till the next day, at meetin’ in
the afternoon, when I went round to listen
en fill in an hour or so, as I sometimes do.
I saw the Captain’s eye fixed ’ard on me,
en then he turned round en said something
to me ’usband, en when me ’usband looked
at me I knew I w’as goin’ to cop out for
something or other. He came down, lookin’
black in the face as ennything, en he said :
‘What de yer mean cornin’ here en disgracin’
me?’
“En I said, ‘l’m not disgracin’ yer.’ En
he said, ‘You are, cornin’ here with nuthin’
on.’ En I said, ‘l’m just as dressed as you
are.’ En he said, ‘Why, yer can see all yer
figger.’ En I said, ‘l’ve got two petty-coats
underneath the ’obble,’ I said. Well, one
word led to another, en we made a reg’lar
scene at the meetin’. En he so exaspurated
me at last I said, ‘What about the bob
blouse?’ ”
“What did yer mean by that?”
“Oh, nuthin’. It’s something I alius brings
up when we have a proper barney.”
She went on sewing awhile.
“S’pose I shouldn’t bring it up, but he
makes me feel that mad,” she said at last.
The bread-carter’s wife nodded encourag-
ingly.
“It’s something what ’appened in his sin-
ful days,” said the woman with the black
eye. “He useter boose every penny he could
beg, borrey or steal, as the sayin’ is, an’
things was pretty ’ard on us just then, what
with butchers en bakers en landlords wantin’
money. Sometimes we useter go ’ungr.y. I
useter be fair ashamed to go out in the street,
I was so shabby. One Saturday night when
’e come ’ome earlier than usual I went
through his pockets while he wuz asleep en
I found a bob, en I went out with it to see
if I couldn’t get a bit of enjoyment too. I
wuz lookin’ at some things in the stalls at
Paddy’s Market, en a blouse took me fancy,
en 1 said to the Jew woman, ‘ ’Ow much?’
En she said, ‘A bob.’ En I bought it.”
She paused thoughtfully a little, and then
went on.
“Next day I put the bob blouse on, en
when me ’usband saw it, ’e said, ‘Where did
you get that new- blouse?’ En I said, ‘lt
ain’t a new blouse. It’s a second-’and one.’
En ’e said, "Ow did yer get it?’ En I said,
‘I didn’t, get it ’ow think, ennyway.’
En ’e said, ‘l’ll break yer jaw if yer don’t
tell me.’ En I said, ‘Well, if yer must know,
I bought it in Paddy’s Market for a bob.’
E. said, ‘Where’d yer get the bob?’ En I
said, ‘I took it out of your pocket when you
vvuz boosed last night.’ He said, ‘You’re a
liar. It 1 d bad a bob I wouldn’t have cuine
’ome so early.’ _ En he gave me a terrible
’ammerin’, the first since we wuz married.”
'■’E ougliter be ashamed of ’imself,” said
the other woman.
“‘I kep’ it in, and next Saturday I got ’im
to come to the market, en I found the Jew
woman who sold me the blouse, en I asked
her before me ’usband, en she said that she
remembered selling me the blouse for a bob,
en dirt cheap at the price. Well, me ’usband
didn’t say nuthin’. He was very quiet, en
from that day to this I never knew whether
he believed that I took the bob outer his
pocket or not. All I knows is that he
didn’t drink enny more that night, en next
week he came in en said, ‘Liz, I’ve bin con-
verted to the Salvation Army. Things will
go better now.’ ”
A more thoughtful expression came into
her eyes, and she leaned her chin on one
palm and looked before her.
‘‘l don’t know,” she said. “Sometimes I
almost wishes he had never joined it.’*
“You’d think it would make him better,”
said the other woman.
“Maybe it has, but you see he wuz never
one of them men who jawed yer when he
wuz in drink. He wuz always merry en
laughin’ till he went to sleep. Since he’s
joined the Army we never have a bit of a
laugh or jollification. If I look pleasant he
quite puts a damper on me. If I buy a bit
of pink ribbon for me neck he reckons it’s a
manifestution of evil in me, en if I have a
happy feelin’ come over me (as it will some-
times) he inst’ly wants me to kneel down
with him en wrestle in prayer, which I
always do to humor him, not that I think
it does much good. You see, I’m not a good
woman. At the same time I’m not a bad
woman. I’m one of the betweens, as the
sayin’ is. I’d like to have a ’at with ostrich
feathers in it, and patent tanned boots with
high heels en open-worked stockings, en I’d
like to be in company, if there’s a bit of a
sing-song, or go to bioscopes, or a theayter,
if there’s ennything good on; but I keep as
straight as I can, en if I have a doubt if I’m
goin’ too far I asks me ’usband if it is sinful.
But outside the real bad sins what never
pull me at all, ’e don’t know till he has talked
it over with the Captain, en it alius seems
to me that the Captain puts ’im up to a
good deal of devilment.”
She stitched awhile, and then said, peev-
ishly :
“When I first met ’im en useter walk out
together, if I put on a new bit o’ finery he
always useter say, ‘That’s nice, Lizzie. You
do look pretty in it.’ After we’d been mar-
ried a few years you’d think I wuz breakin’
up the ’ome if I turned a old skirt to make
it look a bit new.”
“It’s the way with all of them,” said the
bread-carter’s wife. Then a long silence
ensued, the blacksmith’s striker’s wife doing
her sewing more rapidly. The bread-carter’s
wife said at length : “He must ’ave landed
you a daisy to give you a eye like that.”
The woman who was seated looked up
patiently.
“He always does if I brings up the bob
blouse.”
ROBERT BROTHERS.

Application for Admission as Cadet-
Midshipman.
APPLICATIONS for admission as Cadet-
Midshipman to the Royal Australian
Naval College will be received by the Naval
Secretary, Navy Office, Melbourne, up to
30th June, 1914, from those boys born in
1901.
Forms of application and full particulars
may be obtained on application to the Naval
Secretary, Navy Office, Melbourne; or to
the District Naval Officers, Brisbane, Edge-
cliff, Sydney, Port Melbourne, Largs Bay
(S.A.), Fremantle or Hobart, as the case
may be.
S. A. PETIIEBRIDGE,

SECRETS OF A COMPLEXION .
SPECIALIST.
A SIMPLE PRESCRIPTION BY WHICH
A SPECIALIST QUICKLY RE-
STORED HUNDREDS OF RUINED
COMPLEXIONS.
At a recent conference of specialists an
interesting discussion took place in regard
to the ways and means for beautifying and
removing bad complexions, and the experts
in this line, all agreed to the great value of
what is known among specialists as Floxoin
Lotion. A well-known skin and complexion
specialist present gave some wonderful in-
stances of cases where his patients had
restored their disfigured complexions in an
exceedingly short time by applying this
lotion, which consists of two ounces of rose
water, one dram of Tincture Benzoin, and
two ounces Flowers of Oxzoin, and can be
prepared by any chemist, many of whom
keep it put up ready for use under the name
of Floxoin Lotion. He advised the applica-
tion of the lotion night and morning with a
soft cloth or sponge, and said that he had
never known it to fail in remarkably im-
proving the complexion and removing all
skin blemishes in a few days. It is particu-
larly valuable in all cases when the face has
been chapped by exposure to wind and
weather, and quickly restores the soft peach-
like bloom of youth, even to complexions
that have been apparently ruined beyond all
hope of redemption.*

RUNNING INTO THE ANIMAL.
Notice of motion was given that it is desirable
that steps be taken to protect Australia from the
American Beef Trust, and from extraordinary prices
now charged for meat.
JOSEPH: “Beef Trust? Bee / Trust? Hi don't
see no Beef Trust!”
The N.S.W. Ministry has a capacity for
falling into arrears which has seldom been
eoualled. At the end of March, 1914, when
nine months of the financial year had ex-
pired, the expenditure was almost exactly
£1,000,000 ahead of the revenue. The re-
venue was rather more than £1,000,000 ahead
of that for the corresponding nine months
of the previous year, but even that great
influx of money couldn’t keep the Holman
ship off the rocks.
“Is There Work In Australia?” was the
heading of a Sydney Telegraph clamor last
Monday. Well, there is a little. But if
more goods were made here, and fewer were
imported under a rotten semi-Freetrade
tariff, and there was less money borrowed
and charged to Posterity to pay for the im-
ported goods which prevent Posterity’s
father having a job, there would be a lot
more w T ork in Australia.
“The Financier” writes
The Liberal press tried to scare N.S.W. Governor
Strickland into refusing to assent to the recently-
passed Wharfage Act (which imposes export duties
on wheat, wool and other produce), on the ground
that it is not constitutional, and trespasses on the
Federal domain. Strickland, however, on Attorney-
General Hall’s certificate, gave his assent to the
measure. The merits of the case will doubtless be
argued in the High Court. Wharfage dues, imposed
by a Harbor Trust, and used for the upkeep of the
premises, may be regarded as wharf-rent. Imposed by
a State Government for general purposes, and with no
relation to the cost of maintaining the port, they
look like plain export or import duties, and these are
Federal concerns.
The Victorian Easter Labor Conference
did at least one sane thing when it rejected
the motion to debar employers of all sorts
from any office in the party. The inherent
absurdity of the idea was obvious —it re-
stricted labor representation to wage-earners,
yet debarred these directly they raised
themselves above that status. The weird
idea of quite a number of delegates seemed
to be that manual labor necessarily holds a
monopoly of political wisdom. Yet to carry
out its progressive policy Labor needs brains.
Presumably it fears treachery ; yet the man
who isn’t clever enough to go wrong is too
great a fool to go any way.
THE HAT- TRICK.
As manager of the last tour, Mr. Pawley asked the

THE 'ROO: “ I'm only too willing to assist

The emphatic announcement of N.S.W.
Assistant Treasurer Hoyle that “no matter
how high the wage is,” if it is paid weekly
the receipt meed not be stamped, may or
may not be legal, for Hoyle is a poor sort
of authority. If it is legal it will make a big
hole in the stamp revenue. * Also it will
make a big hole in the temper of the police
force, which is paid fortnightly. Meanwhile
the bald fact remains that, according to the
Act just devised by the Ministry which
Hoyle ornaments, every receipt for £2 or
more must be stamped, and no exemptions
are even hinted at. Assistant-Treasurer
Hoyle may take unstamped receipts from
Bill of the railways on his own authority,
but if the next Government sues Bill for the
penalty on 52 unstamped receipts per annum
that unfortunate person will become wild.
A Chair of Journalism is suggested by
Minister Carmichael as another experiment
(and expense) at Sydney University. After-
all, journalism, not being an exact science,
might be better left alone. Like cadging,
rheumatism, the flotation of wild cats, bald-
ness, lying, the gift of prophecy, and Dan
O’Connor’s style of oratory, it is. a thing
that grows. Very few bright, original jour-
nalists started in the profession. They fell
into it, or else it rose up and surrounded
them. And journalism is of many sorts.
When one professor has been appointed from
the S. M. Herald, and the next one from
Truth, and the third from Splashes, and
the fourth from The Bulletin, and the
fifth from the Australian Hen, and the
sixth from the Watchman, the teaching
will have become mixed. It would be better
to establish a Chair of Public Finance, with
a special regard to the necessity for keeping
the accounts square and not playing the
devil with the sinking fund. Also, a Chair of
Honest Politics might be a good idea.
According to George Ryland, ex-Director
of Lands at Darwin, the Northern Territory
“has a great future, but the path of pro-
gress is beset with tremendous difficulties.
Well, certainly, why shouldn’t it be? Look-
ing back over thousands of years of history
the path of progress was always beset with
tremendous difficulties. Even the sites ot
London and Paris had to be cleared ot
forests and bears and wolves ; and New Aork
had to contend with forests and wolves and
blizzards, and also with Red Indians who
wanted to burn the first Rockefeller alive on
Broadway. Sydney, in its early days, got
down to putting the inhabitants on a mere
scrap of diet and praying for the arrival
of a ship with stores, and it hanged the
person very high who stole J-lb. of flour
from the diminishing stock. Why be a
weeping female and lament about a few
difficulties ?
One of the very workt jokes of the recent
State Premiers’ Conference was their deci-
sion to refer the uniform railway-
gauge question to the. inter-State Com-
mission. The Commission is up to
its back teeth in tariff matters. In
fact, it has enough work ahead, in this
line alone, to keep three Commissions busy
for a couple of years. Even if it is able to
give an opinion by 1916 —which is unlikely
—it will be 1918 before the financial side
of the question is fixed up. Australia may
therefore look forward to the beginning of
this urgent work some time in 1919 (that is,
if it has unusual luck), by which time the
cost of converting the lines will have in-
creased by over 85 per cent. As a mere
side-line it may be noted that the point at
issue was “definitely settled,” at considerable
expense, some time ago, by a committee of
experts, who plumped for the 4ft. BJin.
gauge.
The other day Judge Winnecke, of Vic-
toria, felt called upon to remark that the
law re juries needed amending.
The jurymen in a criminal case
had informed him, after two hours’
consideration, that there was no
chance of an agreement, and he
might as well discharge them. He
said he couldn’t; they must dwell
in retirement for the statutory six
hours. The evident intention > of
the blessed law is that the 12, being
locked in a small room, without
refreshments or amusements, shall
become weary of the business and
each other’s society, and the
majority shall convince the obstrep-
erous minority with the greater
speed because of the unpleasant
conditions —which happened in this
case, for in another couple of
hours the jury returned to
court with, as was naturally to be ex-
pected, a verdict of acquittal. Then, as be-
fore remarked, his Honor made his pene-
trating discovery that something called for
alterations and repairs.
The N.S.W. Railway Department wants
an assistant to the Chief Mechanical Engi-
neer, and the astounding statement is made
that thlVe is nobody in the service “who
possesses the necessary qualifications in re-
gard to experience in the designing of loco-
motives.” The explanation sounds strangely.
A new locomotive isn’t a freak, like a new
religion or a hew constitution or a black
tulip or a fat girl. Except when the De-
partment decides to adopt a fresh design
the new loco, is simply a copy of any other
old craft of its class, and is cut to the same
4ft. BJin. gauge. As an excuse for giving a
good billet to an imported man this excuse
seems a rather poor excuse.
Kaiser William, states the cable, expressed
his devotion to his Fatherland the other
week by voluntarily paying his quota of the
War Tax on Wealth. William coughed up
£200,000. The spectacle of a king contri-
buting to the expenses of his own particular
game of war is about as impressive as the
enthusiasm of a cricketer who buys a new
bat for himself.
Fiji has, during late years, laid in heaps
of colored labor from India —the kind that
Australia’s Brucesmith politicians regard as
just the thing for this country’s Northern
Territory. In fact, it has Brucesrhithed so
lavishly that there are now more than 10
imported Hindus to one white man. The
Hindu came as a cheap temporary laborer,
but he remains as a rather bumptious per-
manent settler and often as a profierty-
owner. We get news of him occasionally—
like this extract from the Western Pacific
Herald (23/3/’l4) :
The position is becoming acute and our police seem
powerless. That -is.. the general opinion in to\vn
[Suva] with regard to the frequent raids that haVe
lately been made by colored night prowlers upon
European females, adult and juvenile. Within the
past three weeks five,'serious cases have come under
our notice, and the endeavor in each case has been
nothing less than an attempt to satisfy carnal pas-
sions. .. .Europeans at present dare not allow their
wives and daughters outside unattended, and amongst
the womenfolk themselves a state of terror exists.
Suva has only about 400 white females alto-
gether, and five serious assaults by colored
marauders of the night inside three weeks
suggests that our mild colored brother is
going it some.
“Y’r Washup,” said the Sydney police-
court solicitor, “my client admits that he
issued an unstamped cheque. But, y’r
Washup, my client is an artist, a highly-
strung man, and he was just recovering from
a very severe illness. In fact, y’r Washup,
the best medical authorities in Sydney rec-
koned that a sight of the stamp might be
fatal.”
THE TIME OF PEACE.
The alliance with Japan, however, reduces for the
time being the necessity for providing active and sub-
stantial defences. —Sydney Telegraph.
TELEGRAPH: " Let us sit here, Joseph, darling,
and enjoy ourselves. Don’t worry about
the blessed country.”
COOK: “Angel, nothing would give me greater
pleasure .”
“Nemo”: When the Irish Parliament
meets, where will it meet? A struggle will
be made to instal it at the old house on
College Green, Dublin. This place has for
114 years been used as the premises of the
Bank of Ireland, but there is still intact the
chamber in which the ancient Irish Parlia-
ment foregathered —that Parliament in
which no Roman Catholic could sit and for
which n 6 Roman Catholic could vote, so
that the ancient Legislature was made up
of English strangers living in Ireland,
Orange-lodgers from Belfast and thereabouts,
and nominee adventurers sent over from
London. It is a fine building, but perhaps
it might be better to drop Dublin altogether,
and build a new Parliament House at Tara.
Tara is a centre of old Irish tradition, and
it is a nice green spot, at present used
chiefly by the pig (God bless him!). Any-
how, there is more water there than at
Canberra.
IN TOPHET.
BLACKUS : “What is it?”
HAIRUS: “ They tell me it's a N.S.W. live-
stock train taking railway officials to the
saleyards .”
The latest speculation of the N.S.M ■
Government is the appointment of a director
for the Conservatorium of Music at a wage
of £1250 per annum. It may be regarded
as rather a pity that the music wasn’t post-
poned until the financial ship partially
righted itself, and the poor little sinking
fund was re-established. Even now, on a
pleasant summer evening, there are 10
pianos going strenuously in the average
dozen suburban houses, and sister Mary
Jane’s top note is howled in seven of the 10
houses, and brother Jack is playing the
violin in the eleventh house. And only in
one house of the 12 is there peace —such
peace as may be expected, seeing that the
fowls can’t sleep for the music and the cat
has fled up the peach tree to escape “The
Holy City.”

BLOGGS, M.L.A.
(A Story of our Great Sod-Turning Industry.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Berrigan, Wednesday.
After a lapse of nearly two years since the “turning
of the sod” ceremony, the Finley to Tocumwal rail-
way extension, a distance of 13 miles, has been made
available for passenger traffic by the Public Works
Department.— Casual News Item.
Two years ago they “turned a sod,” and gave Bloggs,
M.L.A., a spade—
A silver spade on which he trod with boots some
foreign firm had made!
And cheers went up, and beers went down, and
Bloggs, of course, began to blow
About the future of the town —
Two years ago!
Two years ago they cadged a loan from Uncle Cohen,
oversea —
Alas, how many pounds have flown, since then, of
borrowed £ s. d. !
How many sods have since been “turned” by skiting
fools like Bloggs and Co.?
What gorgeous profits would be earned —
Two years ago 1
Two years ago, in honeyed tones we heard Bloggs in
the local hall,
Above the bottles and the bones, remarking that
both Tocumwal
And Finley would arise and bless the railway, which
would make them grow
Like Melbourne, and like Sydney, yes—
Two years ago!
Two years ago, ’mid cheers and smiles, behold the
Strong Sod-Turner speaks:
“My friends, we’ll build these thirteen miles in rather
less than fourteen weeks.”
To loud “Hear, hears!” and wild applause, Bloggs,
M.L.A., continued so:
“Work would go forward, without pause”—
Two years ago !
Two years ago! But, why pursue those foolish utter-
ances more?
The cash is gone; the lOU is safe behind the strong-
room door
Of Uncle Cohen, oversea! But interest is paid, we
know,
On thrice the sum it was to be —
Two Years Ago !
’Tis ever thus —or so it seems —with cash on Works
and Railways spent;
Two years ago the grandest schemes “won’t cost the
country one per cent. ! ”
“A splendid asset,” is the phrase— We’re splendid
asses, without sense!
We’ll find it out, one of these days—
Say, two years hence!
N.S.W.
O. C. C.

AGED LADY
DERIVED GREAT BENEFIT FROM
COMSTOCK’S NERVE AND BONE
LINIMENT FOR RHEUMATISM.
The powerful, penetrating qualities of
Comstock’s Nerve and Bone Liniment acting
through the skin on the delicate nerves and
musdes was never more forcibly illustrated
than in a letter from a lady describing the
great benefit her aged mother had received
from its use, for rheumatic pains. Miss A.
Mantle, of Eastern Terrace, Queenstown,
Otago, N.Z., writes: —
“I have pleasure in stating that my
mother (Mrs. Mantle) has received great
benefit from your very valuable liniment tor

SPORTING
NOTIONS
With the exception of Belove, who was
placed in the Melbourne and Sydney Cups, and had
meanwhile won a good race at the V.R.C. Autumn
meeting, and the trusty but unlucky Gigandra, the
gees that bloomed conspicuously in the Spring were
mostly disabled or “out of form” in April at Rand-
wick. Aurifer, winner of the Caulfield Cup, after
being placed in the Epsom and Metropolitan—where
was he ? Nowhere in the Doncaster and last in the
Sydney Cup. The glory of Aleconner and Cagou
had departed. Four or five weeks had sufficed to
make an awful difference in lownit —who won the
Newmarket Handicap at Flemington and came in
last for the Doncaster. And yet a close finish in
any race is always described as a “triumph for the
handicapper,” as though he had any means of judg-
ing the exact number of pounds difference between
half a dozen horses when they are all in the best of
health and condition. The fact is that no specified
six ever are in the best of nick in the same week.
It rarely happens that they are all wanted to be
fit to run for their lives at the one meeting, and if
they are wanted- their trainers are mostly incapable
of doing the trick. It is more by luck than judg-
ment that any particular stable is in “form” when a
big race meeting conies due. Even then its good
intentions are like!}' to be upset by foolish or roguish
jockeyship. But the difference between the Spring
and Autumn running of the topnotch handicap horses,
and the fact that so many of them get knocked clean
out between October and April, cannot be charged
to the alleged “jockey ring.” The trouble seems to
be in the too frequent running of horses when their
trainers are in a more or less dense state of ignorance
as to exact measure of their fitness or unfitness.
“Public form” is ridiculously variable on Australian
racecourses because the stewards everywhere wink at
it, instead of legislating against the rotten system of
sending horses out unbacked by the stable, for exer-
cise gallops. This system suits the trainers, who thus
spare themselves the difficulty of judging a nag’s
preparation on his track performances only. And
it suits the jockeys, who get a good idea as to the
relative ripeness of the animals that are out for an
airing, and who also get paid for the job of not
trying to win the race even though their mount hap-
pens to be going unexpectedly strong. But the sys-
tem doesn’t make good sport for the poor punters,
or promote the well-being of the gees. Their terms
of usefulness to the owner are not., long as a rule.
They get damaged in scrimmages and are worn out
before their time. The “deadest” steed has to travel
faster in a race which one or more live horses are
trying to win than it would’ have to travel in an
ordinary respectable exercise gallop; And the chance
of a mishap on the journey is not to be ignored.
Moreover, the frequent racing of a nag when he is
not out to win is a bad thing for his temper. The
beast turns sour on it. The odd thing is that pun-
ters don’t turn sour on it. If they were to stop
away from race meetings until handicap form grew
less grotesquely in-and-out than of late years, the
stewards would have to do something. The first thing
to do would be to disqualify for two months every
horse of proved merit that was sent out for an airing
public when obviously unfit to do itself justice.
YVhen the high-bred Mucksweat, by Perspiration out
of Pores, needs “a few gallops to get him into racing
+£ lm ’' aS le mealy-mouthed horse reporters put it,
the trainer of Mucksweat should be placed under the
necessity of keeping the untrimmed gee off a race-
course for at least a pair of months.
Cider’s capture of the *rich All* Aged
Stakes at Randwick last week was one of the satis-
factory results of the meeting, except for punters
who let last year’s Derby winner run loose. Cider
had been putting in some great work on the track
previous to the Doncaster Handicap, and he and
Beragoon were the only representatives of classic
form that started, in the subsequent w.f.a. mile event.
It was up to a Derby winner to beat a comparatively
small field of handicap horses that had mostly been
meeting him on much better terms in the crowded
struggles. As for the fact that he wasn’t carrying a
“crack” jockey—well, he’s a hot-headed gee that
prefers to run his own race, and the public by this
time ought to have had about enough of backing
jockeys at short prices.
The most awful crime in Turfland is to
own a horse or pony that runs stiff when a few
jockeys have backed it as a good thing. The owner
probably was guiltless of connivance in the swindle,
and the jockey punters see no crime in “pulling” pub-
lic fancies when they themselves are on top.
But when their particular fancy happens to get
stiffened behind their backs—oh, the rascality of it!
How do they perform ! They vow vengeance on the
owner, and wait for an opportunity to do him a
mischief. This seems rough on the character of
horse-owners as a class. Evidently they are sup-
posed to be leagued with trainers and jockeys to beat
the public. It is a fair thing, from the jockev
point of view, for an owner to be taken down.' But
if an owner is suspected of being privy to a swindle
whereby an uninformed jockey syndicate happened
to suffer, then the suspected party is not a sport,
nor even a man. He’s a dog, and ought to be kicked
accordingly.

The A.J.C. (Sydney) has that, on
and after May, the gee-gees with deficient sight or
only one optic shall not race on any of the courses
under its sovereignty. The decree' will not take
effect a day too soon, and, following the precluding
of horses in hoods and blinkers, will make the calling
of horse-pilotage somewhat safer.
In the hills behind Adelaide the Onka-
paringa Racing Club held its one day’s sport of
the year on Easter Monday. It gives £1550 now
for its Great Eastern Steeplechase, and £3400 in
stakes for the day. A £32,000 totalisator, yielding
some £2400 as the club’s commission, makes this
kind of generosity easy. The steeple went to Quinn’s
grey 10-year-old Vanguard, the 1912 winner, with
12st. 61b., the highest winning weight for 15 years.
He beat a better favorite in Moffat’s Pinto. ” Old
Tramp, winner last year, and second the year before
that, was this time third. Jockey J. E. Mason —-a
Victorian; his best win was on Marmont in the
Australian Cup of 1904 —was thrown, and rolled
on, and fatally hurt. Other winners were mostly
well backed, a £33 dividend on the Hurdles being
the only surprise. The dust on the roads was the
unpleasant feature of the day. South Aus. has
not yet had any rain worth talking about

P. A. Connolly, of Westralia, is the
luckiest Cup-gatherer ever was. In fact, he might
claim to be the cup-gatherer of ages. Seven times
has he collared the Perth Mug, and he has raked in
the Melbourne Goblet, two Sydney Chalices, the
Moonee Valle}’ Utensil and the Kalgoorlie Pannikin.
Jockey W. H. McLachlan has also had a monopoly
of “pint-pot” successes, no less than 16 drinking
tools scattered over Australia having been picked
up by him. No other jockey nor owner has ever got
near the record of the aforementioned.
Easter Monday saw the finish of Vic-
toria’s pennant cricket season, when St. Itilda,
after making 168 to East’s 145, put the latter out
the second time for 167 and then ran up the re-
quired 145 for the loss of five wickets. The peren-
nial Peter McAlister was the losers’ top-scorer in
both innings, Hotchin, Lord and Ivory doing the
most of the Saints’ scoring. This is the latter’s
second premiership, the first being captured three
years ago. The gate, though a holiday one, was
painfully poor—£7l 125., as against £3OO odd
which ’Wood, ’Roy and the V.C.A. divided a year
ago.
Sydney First-Grade Cricket Competition
dragged itself to a weary conclusion last Saturday.
As the premiership was already won past all possi-
bility by University there was not much interest
in the concluding struggles. As indicated on the
first day of the final round, University went down
smash before the battery of Waverley by 56 on the
first innings. Waverley accumulated 145, and 154
for eight, to University’s 89, and, generally, got all
over the place. But it could not affect the result,
though it must have taken a lot of the sweetness
off the glory of the premiership. Glebe used a
battle-axe on Petersham, the figures being Glebe 62,
and 81 for one, while Petersham was 62 and 80,
and by the rule of three the margin was nine wickets.
Central Cumberland, with 368 for nine wickets, over-
whelmed Sydney (243) by one wicket and 125 runs,
on the first innings. Gordon, with 218, fell, on the
first innings, to Western Suburbs (338), by 120
runs. North Sydney sprawled all over Middle Har-
bor, with 352 to 90, and 94 for three wickets, by
262 on the first innings. Redfern, the home of the
Assyrian, came down like a wolf on the fold of
Paddington. It closed its innings with 324 for eight
wickets, smashed up the defence for 205 and 90
for seven sticks, and so devoured the ewe lambs
by 119 on the first innings. It was not a great and
gorgeous finale, the only century being the 115 of
the Rev. Waddy for Parramatta, which styles itself
Central Cumberland. Diamond banged 88 for
Western Suburbs. Sayle ran up 87 for Paddington.
Moore did 84 for North Sydney; L. Pye 83 for
Parramatta; and Prentice 78 for Western Suburbs.
Among the bowlers E. W. Adams, of Redfern, was
the best, with six for 24 and six for 32, equal to
12 for 56—an appalling score. Flynn, of Waverley,
bagged six Uni. wickets for 41. Randall, of Middle
Harbor, caught five North Sydney men for 143. C.
Kelleway, of Glebe, got four Petersham sticks for
27 in the second innings, and nine for 59 in the
two days. In the battle for second place in the
Competition, Glebe, with 59.73 per cent., just beat
Central Cumberland, with 58.33 per cent.

The North versus South cricket Arma-
geddon in Tasmania last week was quite a strenuous
affair. South pushed together 295 for the loss of
seven sticks and closed; whereupon North went in
and whacked up 296. In the second biff Souths again
declared with 243 for eight wickets. North was
going strong with 114 for seven wickets when Time
ran out, and the thing fell down dead and drawn.
Adelaide club cricket shows some odd
results for the past season, as compared with its
inter-State representation. Crawford batted only
twice (62 and 69), Clem Hill once (95) and Gehrs
not at all. Best averages: Chamberlain 66, Middle-
ton 65, then Leek, Steele, Mayne, Hack, Rees, Run-
dell, Down, Jose, all over 45. Middleton’s aggregate
of 790 is the most ever made in a local season. In
bowling, Whitty, though not playing often, mowed
down 50 wickets for an average of eight. Then came
Clutterbuck, Crawford, James, Howard, Moyes,
Wright, Thurgeland and Travers, all under 16 runs
per wicket. The discrepancy between names and
figures was very noticeable in the club placings.
East Torrens was top, for the third season running;
it plays Mayne, Whitty, Middleton, Chamberlain
and Andy Smith. But Adelaide was an excellent
second, and the clubs each won and lost once when
they met; the veteran bowlers Wright, Travers and
Howard, though dropped by the State, are almost as
useful as ever in club games.
There was a heap of inter-State sport in
Adelaide at Easter (besides racing). A polo four
from Victoria (Calvert, Urquhart, Affleck and Hood)
beat Mount Crawford (Angas and three Murrays),
8 to 3. It lost twice to the Adelaide Club (Downer,
Cudmore and two Law-Smiths), 2 to 7 and 3 to 10.
On the other hand, it beat a mixed team of the two
clubs, playing as South Australia; which shows the
extreme value of combination. At tennis, Sydney
University (including Barbour and Gregg) won from
Melbourne (including A. O’Hara Wood) in possibly
the closest fight ever seen: 11 rubbers to 10, 24 sets
to 23, 242 games to 240. Then Sydney beat the
Adelaide students (including Nott and Fisher) horri-
bly. The individual championship was won by Wood,
from Nott. At bowls, Victorian railway men and
Ballarat Central were hospitably entertained, with
varying results. And a Broken Hill Soccer team
played South Australia twice, collecting one loss, one
drawn game and one broken leg.
Australian sculling champion Jim Paddon
at the beginning of the week shifted camp from
Evans Head (N.S.W.) to the Parramatta River,
where he will train to the encouraging shouts of
enthusiastic barrackers. This is the first shift—the
last will be to the land of our Father Bull, on May
2, in search of the championship of the world, now
in possession of Arthur Barry.

TRIED IT ON THE HORSE.
“When driving from Tamworth to Bowling Alley
Point one of my horses took bad with gripes, and
got down kicking and struggling,” says Mr. Sam
L. Pvrke, of Bowling Alley Point, N.S.W. “Those
who passed me considered the horse would die. As
I always carry Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and
Diarrhoea Remedy for my own use in case of emer-
gency, I put a whole bottle of it in half a pint of
water and gave it to the horse. In less than a
quarter of an hour he was up and started to feed.
It is Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea
Remedy that I have to thank for saving me a good
horse.”*

* * * *
Motorists all over the world are taking a
keen interest in the annual 500-Miles race on May
30 at Indianopolis (U.S.A.). This is the most
gilded plum in the motor world, the first prize being
equal to £4OOO, and in addition to it there are a
number of valuable bonuses which about double the
amount. Among the starters this year will be the
all-conquering six-cylinder Sunbeam car which in its
brief life has broken most of the world’s records up
to 12 hours. The machine is being specially fitted
for the Indianapolis track, and will be driven by <T.
Chassaigne, a mighty machine jehu before the Lord.
He will be up against Goux-, the dare-devil French-
man who won the event last year.
Milburn Saylor and Tom McCormick raise
some dust at the Sydney Stadium.
Some time ago Captain Kelsey started on
a motor run from Capetown (Africa) to Cairo, and
high hopes were indulged in, as the South Africans
are very anxious to be the first to make the trans-
continental run. But Kelsey is not a rich man, and
running a motor-car from Table Mountain to the
mouth of the Nile seems to be a job reserved specially
for millionaires. When the captain got somewhere
north of Livingstone his funds were so low that he
hied him to the nearest port and made for Johan-
nesburg and home. It is now said that a millionaire
will make the effort in a specially built 25-30-h.p.
Talbot. Another suggestion is that four cars of the
same make should start together, and the car that
proved strongest be fed with spare parts from the
others as required. Which seems an eminently sensi-
ble scheme, although it does smack somewhat Of can-
nibalism. For, come to think of it, that was the
way Gabbett worked his long expeditions.
* * * *
Queensland lias some claim to the right to
indulge in swelled head and extended chest. At
the recent inter-State Bowls Carnival it won right
through without a single defeat. Victoria won three
games and lost one. Ma got away with two victories
and a couple of defeats. South Australia won one
and lost three, and Tassy lost the whole lot. In the
championships, Waxman, of Victoria, scooped up all
the glory by gathering the individual honor and
skippering the winning rink four. It was the biggest
Bowls upheaval ever let loose in Australasia.
* * * *
There is some talk of compelling motor-
cars and motor-bikes to consume their own noise, so
to speak, and run silently. If this is done there
will be a fine large crop of accidents and fatalities.
The noise of an approaching car or motor-bike is
much better warning than any given by the horn.
Drivers are, all in all, fairly careful; but there is a
fair sprinkling of blighters among them who, when
they do sound the horn, do so too late to give an
unheeding pedestrian time to get out of the way
without doing an impromptu athletic turn which, if
worked up and given on the vaudeville stage, would
be worth about £5O per week. In cases like this
the noise of the car or bike is a boon and a blessing
to men. Someone recently proposed that cars should
be fitted with a standard horn. The same objection
applies. If every motor-car and bike sounds exactly
the same note, in a little while we should have be-
come so accustomed to it that the ear would fail to
note it. The present variety is dreadful, but in its
own way it makes for public safety.
Tom Reece, the billiardist, was to have
started for Australia last week, but he relapsed into
the sickness which downed him immediately after
his heart-breaking go with Inman for the champion-
ship. Reece is a somewhat nervous, bilious-looking
subject, and the strain of the final match after he
had beaten Gray and Stevenson seems to have left
him in a bad way. Inman must be the toughest of
the top-notchers at billiards. A dogged man, who
plays the “Gray game” better than the other Eng-
lishmen, and takes a long time to do it. Also he
plays more for safety than they do, and what with
his tendency to slow down the game and his occa-
sional tedious runs of losing hazards, he wears his
opponent out. When Inman visited Australia, years
ago, and gave points to Fred Lindrum (then only a
vouth), he didn’t appear likely to get to the top of
the tree and stick there. His eyes were not strong
and he hardly pretended to be more than a plugger.
But nowadays he plugs well enough to make 700 or
more in a break when his championship is at stake.
Whispered some time ago that the British
War Office was secretly constructing a huge aero-
plane, a mammoth sky ship capable of carrying heavy
artillery, and built of ferro-molybdenum to resist gun
fire. If it has been constructed it has not, so far,
appeared in public, and it has been left to the
Russian Sikorsky to build the first skycraft worthy
to arrogate to itself the proud title of “ship.” This
is a cloud yacht called the Ilya Mouromets, after
some legendary hero. It weighs 3 i tons, and is capa-
ble of carrying 16 people through the empy) ean for
90min. It has winged its way through space for two
hours with its ordinary crew of eight; and its horse-
power is only 400. With even this low power the
ship carries, besides its own weight, Bcwt. of oil and
fuel, and 21-Jcwt. of passenger. And it is a real
liner, with a fine cabin 37ft. long, with windows at
the sides and one in the floor for photographic pur-
poses ; and then there are a number of private cabins
spread over another 28ft., so the full length of the
hull is 65ft. The crew and passengers can move
about with perfect freedom without impairing the
equilibrium in any way. It has ail the comforts of
a ship, the cabins being lit at night by electricity
and warmed from the exhausts of the motors. It
has a wing area five times greater than that of the
bar man biplane (which is the largest winged of the
garden variety of aeroplane), and seen from below
is said to be quite free of the pitch and roll of the
ordinary flying machine. Sikorsky'"is making a for-
tune carying passengers over and around St. Peters-
burg, and his daily successful flights have moved the
Russian Admiralty to order no less than 10 airships
like unto the Ilya Mouromets.
Georges Carpentier is reported to have
turned down Reg Baker’s offer of £lO,OOO for three
scraps. This was at the rate of £3333 6s. Bd. per
mill, and if the three fights went the full 20 rounds,
£166 13s. per round, or £55 11s. per minute for
actual fighting. However, on the religious ground
that the biffer is worthy of his hire, Carpentier
demanded £4OOO per battle, £2OO per round, or
£66 13s. 4d. per minute. If Carpentier isn’t careful
his beastly modesty will be the ruin of him.

Eddie McGoorty, having disposed of Dave
Smith, and Jeff Smith having outed hard-hitting
Pat Bradley, the victors will meet again in about
three weeks of time to decide which of them shall
have the honor of slamming or being slammed by
Jimmy Clabby, who is now on his way across the
Pacific. By the time these two meet, Clabby will be
here and able to see the sort of propositions he is
up against. And the spectacle is not likeh' to cheer
him up immensely even if it does not depress him.
He has never fought Jeff Smith, but has indulged in
a couple of 10-round mills with Eddie McGoorty. On
May 2 last year, at Denver, Eddie beat him, but on
June 13, at Butte, Clabby managed to turn the
tables and land the bacon in the shape of the deci-
sion. The next meeting between McGoorty and Jeff
Smith is very eagerly looked forward to, and already
throughout the Ma Capital bets are being regis-
tered. Many judges are beginning to suspect that
so far we have never seen Eddie McGoorty fully ex-
tended. That last round of his recent battle with
Dave Smith was a revelation, and Eddie is suspected
of being a cynical young man who is quite prepared
to sacrifice a sclinapper to catch a whale. McGoorty
is also to be put up against Lang if that battle-
scarred warrior is willing to take him on. Dave
Smith got all over Lang by sheer skill, and McGoorty
has knocked Dave Smith out three times. On paper
form he should be able to wipe the floor with Lang
with ease. But, all the same, - this 'writer will be
very astonished if Lang doesn’t make a n.uch more
stubborn battle of it than Smith, and also get
through without being knocked out. McGoorty will
find Billiam’s long, straight left a very tough thing
and hard to get past.
To give any adequate account of the scrap
or howl between Milburn Saylor and Tom McCor-
mick that raged at Sydney Stadium on Saturday
night is quite impossible. It was indescribable. It
was a wild whirl of arms ; a drizzle of flying fists; a
scattering of feet; a cataract of bodies. McCormick,
who weighed lOst. 41b. to Milburn Saylor’s 9st. B]lb.,
was quite unable to take advantage of his weight.
Saylor’s reach, which is about the length of half a
whale, overwhelmed him, and the American’s habit of
hitting all the time, and hitting whether he was
punched or not, and his terrific right-counters, slowly
wore down the rugged Englishman. McCormick did
very well the first round, and landed a couple of
counters that shook Saylor very badly. After that, it
became mostly an exhibition of iron endurance on his
part. The American’s fists fell like Rocky Mountains,
and the power behind them made every blow tell.
Saylor beat McCormick to the floor twice in quick
succession. McCormick made the same mistake as
Herbert McCoy, and clung to Saylor’s left —which
was exactly what Saylor wanted. He used the
pinioned left as a lever, and swung his other meat
club exactly as a man swings an axe. Every time he
swung it it landed on the back of McCormick’s neck,
till even that iron man twice went to the floor. But
he got up and again faced Saylor, and although he
was quite unable to hit for several seconds, Saylor
could neither knock him out or down; and when
Saylor’s blizzard ceased McCormick sprang at him
and worried him as a bulldog might. Then the gong
sounded, and Saylor limped to his corner. Two col-
lisions had landed on the point of the hip-bone or
somewhere in that quarter, and thereafter Saylor was
a sore man. For a time it looked as though the
endurance of McCormick had broken the heart of
Manhattan. He dodged and seemed unwilling to face
the music, and hundreds expected to see him quit at
any moment. But he pulled himself together, and
sent in some staggering blows. He did not hit so
often, blit he hit harder ; and then McCormick would
get in close and send along a quick succession of
short arm jolts that made Saylor feel very sick.
Meanwhile McCormick was going to the devil. His
left eye was bulged right out and looked like a
toy balloon. His right eye was cut. His
jaw was swelling visibly; and his mouth
commenced to lose its bearings. Saylor looked
sick, although his face was unmarked, and after every
round he limped to his corner. Finding McCormick
could not see clearly enough to aim properly, but
still came in like a mastiff, Saylor took to counter-
ing. McCormick was knocked through the ropes, but
came back and fought like 17 devils. At last in the
10th round Saylor got him on the ropes and swung
his two fists like flails. He hit McCormick on
the face - , on the top of the head, on the back of the
neck, across the shoulders, on the back, the chest and
the ribs; and the Englishman went down. Saylor
stood off for a second to get his breath, and as
McCormick went to rise rushed in and was about to
recommence when the towel soared in. McCormick did
not see it, and got up to resume fighting; but the
referee pushed him back. Then for a second it looked
as though McCormick wanted to have a roll on the
boards with the man who had thrown the towel.
What lost McCormick the fight was a moment of in-
sanity when he twice held out his jaw for Saylor to
hit. The first time Saylor smote it with both hands;
the second time he smote the jaw with one fist and
banged the eye with the other. That was the begin-
ning of the end. No man. may play those asinine
tricks with Saylor.

From The Bulletin’s Chief Romish
Minion: —
I see by the cable that Monsignor Ceretti, secretary
to the famous Archbishop Falconio, has been ap-
tttrinted Apostolic Delegate in Australia. He will
act the part of a sort of referee, settling any eccle-
siastical points -which Archbishops Kelly and Carr
may subih'it 1$ luhm The idea of Rome is to liberalise
the Church in the Commonwealth, as was done in
the U.S. quarter of a century ago or more. One
Father McGlynn, an able and radical-minded cleric,
declared about this time that many Old World prac-
tices would have to be abandoned by Americans.
His superiors reeled, in a scandalised condition, he-
fore the shock. He was inhibited by the Archbishop
of New York, and went to Rome, on appeal. Pope
Leo sent Monsignor Satolli as Apostolic Delegate to
the US To the stupefaction of the Old Hands he
supported McGlynn. In a religious manner of speak-
ing Australia is the Toriest place on earth. This
applies to all denominations. Boxed in by the ocean,
and 11,000 miles from the centres of Lib-
eral Thought, it gets the crusted Old ;Port of theology
and practice. It really needs not Ceretti, but a
whole cargo of them.
“Bung” uncorks himself: —
Some vigilant has discovered a “bloomer” in the
consolidated Liquor Act of N.S.W. By one of those
oversights which have disfigured or transfigured quite
a lot of the consolidated statutes, the provision that
a shop or dwelling must not exist under the roof of
an hotel has been altered to read that an hotel must
not be under the roof of a shop, etc. Though appar-
ently the same thing there is an obvious distinction,
and the effect of the alteration is that no new license
can be issued except to those premises which are
self-contained under one roof. If this ruling is to
apply to the renewal of licenses, there is going to be
serious trouble to many of the chief pubberies in the
State, which are not occupying the whole of the
buildings in which they carry on their trade.

Don’t set your mind—set Big Ben
Don’t bother your head about get-
ting up. Leave it to Big Ben.
You ought to go to sleep at night
with a clear brain —untroubled and
free from getting-up worries. You
men, if you are up-to-date workers,
work with your brains as well as with
your hands. Such a little thing as
“deciding to get up at a certain time
in the morning” and keeping it on
tour mind often spoils a needed night’s
rest and makes a bad “next day.”
Try Big Ben everywhere in the house
for a week. He makes getting up so
easy that the whole day is better.
Big Ben is not the usual alarm.
He’s a timekeeper; a good, all pur-
pose clock for every day and all day
use and for years of service.
He stands seven inches tall. He
wears a coat of triple-nickel plated
steel. He rings with one long loud
ring for 5 minutes straight, or for 10
minutes at intervals of 30 seconds
unless you shut him off.
His big, bold figures and hands are
easy to read in the dim morning light,
his large strong keys are easy to <wind.
His price, 12/6, is easy to pay, because
his advantages are so easy to see. See
them at your storekeeper.
If you cannot find Big: Ben in your town, send a
money order to the makers’ Australian office.
We stclax, 63 Pitt Street, Sydney. They will
have one mailed to you by the nearest jeweller.

“Cog”: That phrase of fearsome slovenlin&S, ‘‘re-
ceived a reception,” was well flaunted by Sydney
Telegraph on 23/3/’l4. On the leader page a
column carrying three headlines commenced: “Mr.
Cook received a most flattering reception on Satur-
day afternoon.” And on the next page, under six
headlines, appeared: “Sir Edward Carson received an
enthusiastic reception on arrival at Belfast. Ha
either been handed some token of unexplainable
esteem, the Telegraph would, presumably, have
reported that he had been “presented with a presen-
tation” or “given a gift.”

“OC.C.” Who says the Japs are out of touch with
modern life? Not the bards of Australia, I hope—the
neglected and despised and generally useless, but
amusing or quaint persons who write the tripe-
songs of a nation at 2d. per line! The Japs also
have bards among them, but they are honored and
haughty persons, Wearing good clothes, and a luii-ieci
air They do not write sonnets to their mistresses
eyebrows, nor do they sling off topical verse on the
local show or the illogical politician. No I Ihe
Chrysanthemum Poet gets hold of his subject—or
lets it get hold of him —m-a way Grant Hervey,
“Kodak,” McKee Wright and “Petromus Earbiter
would describe as piffling, and turns out four little
lines like this:— a
Waka-no-ura ni
Shio michi kureba
Kata wo nami
Asribe Wo sashite tazu naki-wataru I
Which, being translated, would run :
On the shore Of Waka
When the tide comes flowing in
There being no dry land, , , a
Towards the Place of .Reeds the storks fly across,
crying.
This is the poetry of the Obvious, which never
arrives anywhere. But Japan is full of it, and
every year (listen, Carmichael, and-ye other blight-
ing “uplifters !”) the, Japan League of Poets holds a
mighty conference under the. aegis, or palladium, or
linoleum, of the Imperial Poetry Bureau! At this
Homeric meeting—l presume it must be Homeric on
account of the thunder and surge of its strophes,
and the noise of the poets all declaiming against
one another—prizes are given, amounting in English
money to about £700; the first prize being the not-
to-be-sneezed-at sum of £2401 Every year the
Imperial Poetry Bureau (ye gods! Is Asquith listen-
ing?) announces the subject for the prize poem,
and the 456,786 bards of Japan then rise (or fall) in
all their majesty and wade in for the glory and yen.
For this year the Bureau has set the subject of the
glorious trees at Nikko in front of the Shrine of
leyasu, the founder of the Tokugawa Dynasty the
giant Crj'ptomeria (Suyi in Jap), of which one
avenue is over 25 miles long, and not a tree less
than 200 ft. high. My competing verse runs:—
Up at Nikko, the Beautiful,
Where sleeps heroic leyasu,
The trees are green and cool.
/ But the falling dew weeps
From off their green crowns
For heroic leyosu in the Hot Place!
I should about win if I could put that into Jap.
But what price the Imperial Poetry Bureau and the
£7OO divided yearly among the Herveys of Japan?
* * * *
“Tiaki”: I have always taken an intelligent
interest in the methods employed by the astute
Yankee in arresting cold coin and taking it into
custody. I had noticed that Amurica was just
dying to buy “song-poems” and I determined to in-
vestigate. i sent for a dozen “free booklets,” and
in due season reaped a fine crop of literature at the
front of my residence. I gathered from each pub-
lisher that he was the true and only buyer of ‘ song-
poems” and that every other . fellow whose free
booklet had accompanied his in the mail-bag was a
robber, a thief and several kinds of burglar. On one
thing they were unanimous. Billions of dollars
were being paid to song writers and about the
easiest way of collecting heaps of green-backs was to
write “song-poems” of the “I love you true, you
know I do” variety. I determined to collect a few
bushels of green backs, and, selecting what appeared
to be the most honorable of the brigands, made a
song. I sent it away and, in many days, received
the joyful intelligence “that they thought very kindly
of it,” and, although they wouldn’t definitely guar-
tee that my hand-painted ditty would shake the
very foundations of the Universe, they hinted that
some very pronounced earth tremors would certainly
result from its publication. An agreement contain-
ing 11 clauses was enclosed in which “the p ?; r a°l
the second part agreed to pay the party of the first
part 535” for advertising and boosting a certain song,
to wit, “The Wairoa River”; the .first party to
present the second party with 200 copies of the same
(“Tiaki,” song writer, was. the second party who
had to part up ?35 to the first party). I immedi-
ately decided to cause no seismic disturbances, and
the truth then dawned on me that the great Amuri-
can public make its millions by writing song
pomes” and selling them to each other. When I find
a bandit who is willing to buy my song-poem (not
sell it to me) I shall immediately erect a factory
and install up-to-date machinery for the manu-
facture of the goods.

The Receipt is Worth Money to You
Most receipts are of value only to the person who receives
them.
Phe National Cash Register receipt is of value to you,
because it means that at the time it was printed the amount
was added on the adding counter under lock and key, and
must be accounted for.
It makes you sure of your profit on every transaction.
It saves money that might have been lost through care-
lessness and mistakes. ■
It enforces a correct record inside the register, and

No Situation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Acting Premier, Mr. Cann, stated yesterday
that, so far as the Government is concerned, there
is “no situation” regarding the Upper House.
The position in which the Government stood in
regard to the Council was, Mr. Cann added, that all
the measures the Government had sent to the Coun-
cil had been passed by that branch of the Legisla-
ture.—Daily press.
The Upper House is very tame and meek.
It passes all the Bills we send along;
It makes no fuss; it sings a simple song,
Like some large cat that slumbers all the
week,
And leaves the pale canary piping strong
Within its gilded cage, or some slow creek
That babbles faintly on its rural way
From blushful morn to quiet evening grey.
The Daily Press is gentle, trustful, kind.
Its harmless leaders almost speak our praise;
Dead is the bitterness of other days,
Our ways and works it does not seem to
mind,
No longer does its indignation blaze,
Our faults grow few and very hard to find ;
In fact, the Telegraph and Herald stand
Almost prepared to eat out of our hand.
Fat sits at ease within his ample chair.
He does not snort, he does not curse and
rear;
In fact, we note a tender smile appear
And creep along his waistcoat large and
fair
When we approach and murmur in his ear.
Our confidences he is glad to share,
And, by the sun of office large above,
We hope to very shortly win his love.
No “situation” need arise at all.
For all we do there still is ample room.
The whole bright garden is in perfect bloom.
The shadows pass. We watch the sunlight
fall
To chase away all trace of strife or gloom.
Wade has no terrors left that may appal.
All that we need is just to sit down tight,
And keep our Labor pledges out of sight.
N.S.W.
CURSE O' MOSES.
A Council that has the Importing Craze
Badly.
Those in charge of the municipal affairs of
Melbourne City are notorious victims of the
Foreign-Trade mania, but two of their latest
follies create a loud suspicion of acute mania
or “graft.” Practically' all the Council’s
tenders, contracts, etc., are decided in com-
mittee—which often means that three men
may settle a matter involving thousands of
pounds. The recommendations are then
placed before the full council and rushed
through without much dejbate or considera-
tion. Recently three tenders were received
for the supply of explosives—£l9B 13s. 4d.,
£l9B 7s. Id. and £l9B 7s, Id. The first was
from an Australian firm, the other two from
importers, and the remarkable coincidence
in .the figures of these two hinted at an
“honorable understanding.” But the Coun-
cil’s P. W. committee, obsessed by its For-
eign-Trade fetich and perceiving nothing
unusual, coolly passed over the Australian
tender—6s. 3d. higher—and recommended
that the contract be divided between the
two importers. Fortunately the few genuine
Protectionists on • the Council were so : in-
sistent that the matter was referred back to
the committee. The other case wears an
even more foffiish complexion. Last Decem-
ber the electric-light committee recommended
the purchase in England of lOOgals. of
resin oil at 4s. 6d. per gal., plus duty and
landing costs, which made the price equal to
ss. lid. per gal. That honest Protectionist
and oil-expert, Ciias. Atkins, declared that
oil of equal quality could be imported at
Is. 6d., and made locally for Is. Bd.! While
the argument was proceeding he sent a
sample of the four-and-a-half-bob oil to a
large firm of English resiu distillers, and
asked them to match the sample and quote
a price. They did so, and quoted Is. 6d. !
Yet in February the council, in defiance of
the protests of Atkins mnd his fellow-Pro-
tectionistg, ignored an Australian offer at
Is. Bd.. and the English quotation of Is. Od..
and calmly accepted the tender of 4s. 6d. —
thus to all intents making that firm a free
gift of £3O. This may be nothing worse than
sturdy, not to say rabid Freetradism. but
what it looks like is plain, ordinary b<»;d-
ling.
Tiie Old Soldier:—
i'&ti the last Islington (England) Show someone
who had used Australian horses in India said:—
“Why is it that Australian horse-breeders do not
send more hunters to England? There is a good
market there at prices that should be very profitable
to Australia.”
The Sydney Telegraph reckoned the idea was a
splendid one, and published an enthusiastic leader to
that effect. In point of fact, there is nothing in it.
Australian dealers aren’t going to breed horses fit to
carry from 12 to 14 stone over an English country
on the off-likelihood of- getting from £BO to £l5O
for them landed on the other side. The risk and
expense of sending horses 12,000 miles are too big.
for one thing, and for another the Australian hunter
is so ill-suited to British conditions that the chances
of his selljng at all would be highly problematical.
His chief defect is that she “jumps too big,”
to use the jargon of the trade. He does this because
the fences of his native land have to be cleared,
unless the animal that attempts them wishes to have
its knees or its neck broken. In England and Ire-
land, except on rare occasions, a hunter can brush
through a fence to the extent of a couple of feet or
more. When Daimio, the famous M.L. steeplechaser,
ran in the Liverpool Grand National, he lost lengths
at every obstacle by his practice of jumping inches
clear of the top of the tremendous fences on the
Aintree course. And so it would be with most
Australian hunters. They would never fall, so long
as they remained more or less unfatigued bv their
needless exertions, but they could not live 'for 10
minutes with the rushing thoroughbreds of the Shires
—-animals which have been carefully trained to race
over everything but timber.
With tlfe sporting maxim “Ktorses for courses” may
be coupled the axiom “Horses for countries.” A
Leicestershire champion is of little value in the
stone wall regions of Galway, and similarly the tim-
ber-jumping wonders of the Sydney Show would be
wasted in most 1 parts of England, where wooden
fences .are as rare as virtue in Piccadilly. And it is
just as well. .What with Yankee, Japanese, Russian,
Indian, Cingalese, Straits Settlements, and now, at
latest, Siamese buyers, Australia is losing all the
nationally-serviceable horseflesh that it can afford, and
a considerable bit over.

“STOP! Mind that Step.
How often when entering or leaving an automatic lift have yon heard that warning? If the cage floor does not come
to the floor,level it indicates that the lift is not provided with a “Floor Setter,” or else it is defective.
All A.E.G. LIFTS are provided with a FLOOR SETTER
which stops the lift when the cage is level with the floor. It is operated by the car itself, and is thus not
affected by stretching of the ropes —the simplicity of the “Floor Setter” guarantees perfect operation.
