IN BANANA LAND
Hylands. N.T. Director of Lands, just
sacked by the Cook Government, remarked
in leaving Port Darwin that settlement in
that country was impossible without a direct
transcontinental line. Rylands is not quite
right. Settlement in the N.T. without a
transcontinental line is quite possible—to
the Asiatic!
"'• - &
The Apology.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tlle worm cannot live more than two feet below
the surface, so the tales of his ravages' in the grave
are only delusions.— Fabre.
Here’s an apology—
Jolly old germ !
Hong have we libelled thee,
Brother and worm.
Here is the stuff we
Believed in of old,
Took it as gospel—the
l’lirest of gold—
Down in the sepulchre
There in his bower
Peasant and prince did lie
Gaily devour;
Little he cared for the
Fame of the dead,
So but his greediness
Duly were fed ;
Kaiser and Ivaiserin
Served him in turn.
Save when their ashes were
Locked in an urn.
Slaves of his appetite
Food for his jest
Hero and heroine
Hailed him as guest.
Marfyr and miscreant
Slaves of his wish
Figured impartially
Down on his dish.
Cliiefest of democrats.
Rank he despised ;
Beggar and potentate
Equally prized.
Now we discover that
Long we’ve abused him ;
Scientists tell us we’ve
Shamefully used him.
Never a grave doth he
Spoil with intrusion ;
lie and his ravage are
All a. delusion.
We shall escape Him —
O Brethren —be gay !
Him and his kin in white-
Coated array ;
Never again shall our
Sepulchres lodge him.
No matter what comes:
So long as we dodge him.
■ But. as we’ve libelled him.
Brother and Worm —
Here’s an Apology—
Jolly old Germ! !
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
Concerning Papua and a Dutch Commis-
sioner’s Reflections.
Myniieer Coenan, the Dutch Commissioner
who has been roaming through Australian
Papua with a view of finding out for the
Dutch Government liow things are done in
that progressive territory, has finished his
job, and sailed for the rice-fields of Batavia
once more. The shrewd, small Hollander took
care not to make his inquiries exclusively
in the company of Government officials.
Every now and then he broke loose, and wan-
dered alone among the plantations or up into
the hill country, asking as he went (like an
officer putting his head inside a barrack-room
CONCLUSION.
WIFE: “ I don't quite believe that story about Jonah and the whale. At any rate , I'll
get the rights of it from Jonah when I get to Heaven .”
HUBBY: “But Jonah may not be there !”
WIFE: “ Very well, then , dear, you can ask him
door) : “Any complaints?” Coenan’s con-
clusion is that there aren’t any com-
plaints Worth speaking about. He thinks
there ought to be Government tech-
nical schools for the natives, and that
every Papuan who goes to gaol ought
to be taught boat-building or carpentering
or house-building, or something of that kind,
before he comes out. Also, he thinks they
ought to tax the native. But he thinks a
good deal of the said native’s brain capacity,
and believes, on the whole, he is little behind
the much-praised Malay. It has been proved
on the Papuan plantations that a native of
the country can tap as much rubber as a
Javanese, and that he can handle machinery
wonderfully well. Coenan doesn’t think
Papua would gain by introducing Chinese,
or other, alleged cheap labor. According to
the Dutchman’s tabulations, the plantations
of Papua are paying at the rate of Is. to
Is. 4d. a day (the latter an extreme figure)
for their labor—including food, clothing,
wages, recruiting expenses and all holidays.
Chinese, Javanese and other mixed races
cost more. The color question, of course,
does not trouble him —the Dutch have no
objection whatever to any sort of a racial
mixture, even when some of the ingredients
are white —he simply says it wouldn’t pay.
Asked by certain discontented citzens why
the country wasn’t getting on, Coenan had
a simple reply. He said :
If the inhabitants talked less, and thought more,
Papua would be a better place. But they seem to
do their thinking with their tongues—perhaps, in
the absence of a more suitable organ. As regards the
land being taken up, I heard here and there that land
development had ceased. I looked up the returns,
and find that in 1910 10,053 acres were taken up.
In 1911, 15,881. In 1912, 24,707. In 1913, 35,363.
And in 1914, up to date, 42,000 odd. During the
last six years, 221,895 acres have been taken up in
all. There are 10 pastoral leases, including 20,042
acres, and eight timber leases, including 81,935.
There are 136 miles of road and many hundred miles
of tracks; the route for a light railway is being
surveyed ; and the oil fields are being developed. The
revenue is increasing steadily with every year. We
should be very well pleased indeed if' Dutch New
Guinea could say as much.”
The Dutchman is nothing if not thorough
and painstaking. Somebodv—many people-
told Mr. Coenan that labor was short for
the plantations. He went round to every
plantation in the Territory, and asked the
managers if it was true. Out of the lot,
three had complaints to make. One had 400
boys, and said he could do better if he had
more. Another said that he had boys enough,
but it was sometimes slow work getting them
recruited and signed on. A third was short
of boys (the explanation, according to his
friends, tying in the fact that he did not
know how to treat them, and had got “black-
listed”). There are over 10,000 boys now
at work in the Territory, according to the
Commissioner, and new sources of labor are
being opened up. He could not see that there
was any shortage, or likely to be any. On
the whole, Coenan has gone back to Dutch
territory much impressed with what he has
seen, and determined to say so. In Merauke,
the capital of Dutch New Guinea, there
are only 37 whites, and there are not as
many more in all the rest of that vast coun-
try. Nobody is taking, up land, nobody is
bringing in money, nobody is preventing any-
body from hunting, killing and eating any-
body else. It is impossible not to draw the
most significant comparisons. Coenan has
one hit to deliver. He is not impressed
with some of the settlers in Australian
Papua, especially amongst the townspeople.
He thinks the Dutch regulations, which
sternly fence out the beachcombing type and
the loafer, are better. It may be so, but Aus-
tralia herself has made a fine country out of
some very curious foundation stones. Any-
how, the Dutch administration has paid the
Commonwealth a big compliment.
♦
The Rev. Sams left Longreach under a
presentation shower of “opals, sapphires,
rubies, topazes, aquamarines and a purse of
sovereigns.” No wonder Sams remarked in
departing that what struck him most in
Queensland was its possibilities. A parson
who is a pug in Bananaland may pile up
popularity, and a pug who is a bit of a
parson may do ditto, but a parson who is
just. plain parson won’t get pelted with
specimens of Q. precious stones when he
levants from the Backblocks. A straight
left and a dirty right are of more value to a
Western sky-pilot than all the tongues of
Pentecost.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 18, 1914.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
After enjoying the breezes of the bay at
the commencement of the holidays, his Ex-
cellency shortened sail at Southport and
came to an anchor, more or less, at the local
Show on Easter Monday. The Chamber of
Commerce people seized the opportunity (it
was the only thin; that the Macgregor
would part with at the moment) to explain
to him their labors. William the Speech-
maker, having surveyed these efforts, burst
forth into page after page from his notes
and departed, leaving enough boiling enthu-
siasm to necessitate a safety valve being
attached to the Mayor of Southport, or the
president, or dictator, or leading cannibal, in
case the boilers crack. To hear the .words
of the City Patriarchs one w T ould imagine
that, shortly, from sand and saltwater, there
would spring up a splendid monument to
their energy and far-sightedness, and that
none of the cost would be added to the
handicapping of posterity. Yet even the
sea wall of. the village is said to carry a debt
the weight of which is gradually pushing it
into tlie sand.
On Friday the Governor unloaded some
more speech on the University scholars, and
they, in retaliation, made a nuisance of
themselves. It was commemoration day, and
although Police Commissioner Cahill for-
bade the budding brain-boxes to “procesh”
they proceeded in a dreary line through the
streets. It was a poor display. Here and
there a penny trumpet sounded, and such
notices as “Are We Downhearted?—Yes,”
or “Will We Be A Long Time Dead? —Per-
haps” were shown. Detectives are still out
looking for the joke.
The bowlers have been feted during the
week, and their games interspersed with less
strenuous festivity. There have been the
usual At Homes and euchre parties. In
fact, the gay young bowler is altogether “in
it” in the city.
Henley, from a financial point of view,
was a tremendous success. The location
(Hamilton Wall), however, did not seem to
meet with overpowering approbation from
the public ;in short, there was much growl-
ing. Those who did not have large quanti-
ties of sand in their shoes walked
along the retaining wall in the blazing sun
and waited for the programme to show
cause. As events were about one hour and
a quarter late they were not disappointed
about the waiting, though the cause might
have its drawbacks. His Excellency and Lady
Macgregor entertained members of Parlia-
ment and their wifr.es and a number of other
guests on the State yacht Lucinda. Also,
the United Service Institute held a reception
and the Brisbane Club did likewise. House-
boats were conspicuous by their absence.
Interest centred in the race for the Queens-
land Ladies’ Championship, which was won
by the crew stroked by Miss Lea.
It is rumored that a well-known and
prosperous Brisbane man has been served
with a writ worth £2OOO for breach of pro-
mise. Most other men envy him, for to be
worth £2OOO of breach is a fine big circum-
stance.
A particularly popular Brisbane girl, Miss
Barbara Brand, was married suddenly and
without warning last week. It appears that
Mamma wanted to take her to England, but,
as the young man was remaining, the young
miss couldn’t find it in her heart to leave
him on his lonely in an unfeeling world.
So there was nothing for it if happiness was
to prevail but to sound the wedding gong
and pass the blessings. Mr. and Mrs. Doug-
las are now considering the moon. No com-
plajnts have been received.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Governor Macgregor pointed out recently
at the annual meeting of the General Hos-
pital that during last year £1036 was spent
on nursing 233 alcoholics in that institution,
and suggested that there ought to be legis-
lation making this a charge on public houses.
Perhaps future hospitals may each be
fitted up with a Bung ward, equipped with
so many fantod and jimjam cots for the
benefit of them that imbibe too freely and
see too much. On the other hand, there is
a saying that men dig their graves with
their teeth and that the stomach is the root
of death and all evil. And if the farmers,
grocers and butchers were charged with the
maintenance of all patients whose troubles
arose from over-eating it would be quite a
new departure. Incidentally (to go back to
the original topic) the farmer who raises the
grain which makes the beer should contri-
bute to the upkeep of the alcoholic ward;
likewise the brewer, and cooper and bottle-
maker, and the liquor-importer, and the ship-
ping co. which carries the liquor and the
wharf-laborer who unloads it; likewise the
Governor, who draws his salary very largely
from drink revenue, and the Church, which
draws part of its revenue from teaching
about the miracle of Cana in Galilee. Many
other ramifications of the trade ought to pay
up, but they are too numerous to be enume-
rated.

The bacillayy treatment of bacterial and fermenta-

Lactobacilline pits the vigorous lactic ferment
against the less resistant organisms of decomposition,
with the' result that the latter are immediately
destroyed.
Lactobacilline is prepared under the scientific
supervision and control of
PROFESSOR METCBNIKOFF.
It is prepared in tablets to be eaten with meals,
and by checking intestinal putrefaction exerts a re-
markable influence towards the attainment of perfect
health. It is of special value in all affections of the
digestive tract, auto-intoxication, colites, intestinal
catarrh, chronic dyspepsia, etc.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MSS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Cutter: Re that remark about “the
•hepherds and sheep of Bethlehem” happening at
Easter (instead of Christmas). The official date of
Christmas is supposed to be several months out.
One reason is that no shepherds of Bethlehem would
watch their sheep in heavy snow, and it is beastly
cold there in the middle of winter. Another is that
the 25th of December was a pagan festival before
it became Christian, and the sacred date is pure
guesswork. Another is that the oldest inhabitants
about Bethlehem, who are a curious mixture of
Arab, Hebrew, old Philistine (the Philistines being
a mob of exiled Greeks) and sundries (mostly sun-
dries), hold the legend that the real date was about
April. The comet, or other unexpected constella-
tion, which is called the Star of Bethlehem can’t
be accurately located. There is no trace in history
of that amazing uprooting of population when
Augustus ordered all the world to be taxed (as if it
wasn’t taxed before), and made everyone tramp to
his or her native town to pay the tax, so that date
is lost. How the wise men from the East came
westward to Jerusalem, and then south and a little
west to Bethlehem, because they saw a star in the
east is quite unknown, so the points of the compass
are lost. The paddock where the shepherds watched
their sheep is still exhibited; it is mostly rock and
contains only about enough feed for 10 sheep. How
many shepherds watched them isn’t known. Close
by is the boulder (tw* tons) which Abraham carried
into the country (reason for carriage not stated),
and the indigenous boulder (10 tons) on which
Elijah slept, the mark of his body being still visible.
By way of a casual, careless allusion to the local
tradition Tub Bulletin fixed the shepherd and the
sheep at Easter-time. It is a mistake to do such
things. Allowance should be made for the mental
limitations of religious papers such as the
Church Standaub. .. .Fishcase: Curse you and your
pointless yarn. We wasted five minutes finding out
that it ended nowhere... .Ruby P.: There is some-
thing in it, but it is not so much a story as a series
of shrieks and yelps. A string of sentenoes consist-
ing of three to five words each soon exhausts and irri-
tated the reader... .G.R.: The thanks of the basket
are yours.... Weemac: Not so much of a lie....

“Y’s” reminiscence:—
The value of death-bed depositions (referred to in
The Bulletin of 9/4/’14) recalls one of the most
peculiar deposition cases ever tried in N.S.W. The Judge
who presided was the late Alfred Stephen, and the
official who took the deposition* was Dowling, for a
time police magistrate and afterwards District
Court Judge. It was reported to Dowling that a
German Jewess in George-street, Sydney, was on her
death-bed and that the circumstances pointed to a
tragedy. He grabbed the Court Bible and pen
and paper and hurried to the house. The husband of
the woman was also of the Jewish race, and was at
the bedside when Dowling entered. The medical re-
port was that the case was unmistakably one of
poisoning. The woman emphatically accused her
husband. “Tear, tear,” murmured her husband,
“vat a meestake; vat a meestake.” It came time to
swear to the statement, and Dowling, holding the
Bible towards the woman’s lips, was noaring the
close when her husband jumped at him. “Vat you
doing?” he cried, “Vat you doing? Dat is no good.
Jew only svears true on his own Bible, de Hebrew
von.” Dowling paused. The husband rushed to a
near shelf and dragged down a book. “Here,” he
cried, “dat is de Hebrew; dat is de fella to make de
true oath. Svear her on that.” Dowling, pained at
the scene, accepted the Hebrew book and took the
dying woman’s oath. At the trial before Stephen
the Jew objected to the depositions going in. “Was
it a Hebrew Bible?” asked Stephen. “It was a book
in Hebrew,” answered Dowling. “Do you know
Hebrew?” asked the Judge. “No,” answered Dow-
ling, “but the accused said it was.” “Then you don’t
know that it was a Bible at all. I must rule that
the depositions cannot go in.” Without the deposi-
tions there was little evidenee and the German
Israelite was pushed into the sunlight.

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
It has a hogskin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years cf wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It lias a
deep, “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of Tub
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from J. I*. Talty, 388 Sus'jx Street, Sydney.

“Chasuble”: Archbishop Clarke, while in
W.A., laid it down: (1) that the Church of
England in Australia will have to change its
name. (He proposes “Anglo-Catholic” as a
fitting disguise.) (2) That the said Church
must have its own denominational day-
schools for the poor as well as the rich, if
it is to dodge extinction. Carr and Clarke
will yet be seen taking wine together, as a
friendly preliminary to a joint demand for
State aid for secular schools.

AUSTRALIANS
IN LONDON
will be glad to know of the numerous
amenities and pleasant sides of hotel
life presented by the Hotel Russell.
The visitor feels more like coming
into the atmosphere of English home-
life at its best than entering an hotel.
c There is that domesticity of service
and general environment that make for supreme comfort and homeli-
ness. The cooking is faultless ; the style and scheme of furnishing
luxurious but restrained ; the tariff modest.
At the centre of London’s throbbing life,
it is yet peaceful and quiet, overlooking the
park-like Russell Square. A penny ’bus fare
from almost any spot of interest in London.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The very first item of the loan Estimates laid be-
fore the N.S.W. Parliament for 1913-14 is a stun-
ning piece of effrontery to set down among
Permanent and Reproductive Works. It is only: “Roll-
ing stock to meet additional traffic requirements,
and equipment of new lines, £548,000.” How any
sane and sober man can pretend that such things as
railway carriages and waggons, which are liable
to be smashed up every time a wrong signal happens
to be made or a fog happens to hide the right one
are Permanent is a mystery. What is the average
life of rolling-stock on the N.S.W. railways? It
would be an exaggeration to put it as high as 10
years. There was a time when George Reid tried to
include cups and saucers for the Jenolan accommo-
dation house among the Permanents and Reproduc-
tives, and being scoffed out of that he charged them
to a 30-years’ loan. Of course somebody dropped the
cup long before 30 years expired—in fact, one cup
was dropped inside a week. The locomotive and
passenger carriage and cow-truck aren’t quite so
perishable, but they aren’t Permanent by a long way.
Nothing is—not even the oldest inhabitant.
Just before Easter Australia’s wireless expert, J.
C. Balsillie, lodged an application for a patent for
the rain-making apparatus on which he has been en-
gaged the last two years. No details are given
until the complete specification is lodged, inside nine
months, but it is known that Balsillie’s idea is to
employ electrical forces to artificially bring about a
condition in the atmosphere similar to that which
Nature produces when rain is precipitated. (Any-
body who can frustrate the patent rights by working
on this information can have them.) Arrangements
are alleged to be under way for a trial of the device
over a tract of country embracing something like
10,000 square miles, which is a large and expensive
proposition (like the £5,000,000 capital co. into
which it is proposed to float the concern later on).
“Wonga”: The butter barons and their agents have
spent much time and talk in the vain hope of crip-
pling the margarine trade in John Bull’s country.
It was hoped to do this by persuading Bull’s Parlia-
ment to prohibit the use of coloring matter in mar-
garine. Said coloring matter is used to make the
stuff look like butter, and it does it with such suc-
cess that between the good looks and the low price
of the substitute it is reckoned that for every 10 of
Bull’s residents who purchase one pound of butter
five or six buy' margarine. The latter circumstance,
backed up by the enormous vested interests at stake,
makes the anti-coloring agitation in Australia look
hopeless. This fact is, no doubt, coming home to
those concerned, and the alternative —viz., the pro-
duction of more first-class, and less fourth- and
fifth- and 13th-class butter —is so obvious that
it cannot be much longer dodged by the dirtiest and
laziest cow-farmer. For the rest, the best he can
hope for is to insist on the local manufacture of
margarine being honest. Money is going into the
business, now that science has found a way of pro-
ducing a high-class margarine from cocoanuts; and
if this local margarine is allowed to be colored to
loolk like the butter that it isn’t, there is going to be
a. horrible slump in the Cow Kingdom. The local
Shutter trade is still the biggest thing in the dairying
industry, and if a big slab of that is lost, in addi-
tion to what goes through the competition of mar-
garine abroad, a lot of dairy farms will be for sale
cheap.
* * * »
“McNorth”: Lots of Papuan rubber plantation
shareholders are wondering when Papua is going to
start producing the milk that spells dividends. For
the last three years it has been standing matter in
managers’ reports that “tapping will commence next
year,” yet they haven’t exported much or tapped
loud enough to make anybody open the door. One
factor of the delay was caused by the importation of
managers and others from “the East” —men who
were really bookkeepers and clerks, and quite igno-
rant of the practical side of the business. They were
able to roar “boy” when their boots required to be laced
or their cigarette to be lighted, and were, no doubt,
suited to the old-established plantations of Malaya
and elsewhere ; but they were unfit to carve a place
out of the scrub and put up with the hardship conse-
quent on pioneering in a new country. Consequently,
mistakes were ipade and money lasted. Trees were
planted badly and at the wrong season. General
chaos reigned. These facts, coupled with a complete
ignorance of local conditions spelt ruin for more than
one company. Many of the survivors, however, have
chased the rien from “the East” off the premises and
substituted local men, who are chiefly Australians;
and as the Australian is a grafter, it won’t be for
want of hard work if Papua doesn’t grow rubber.
But there are other reasons why the rubber industry
may not flourish in Papua, not the least of which is
the possibility that the conditions of Papua may not
be suited to the Para rubber, a native of South
America, and the variety chiefly planted. This variety
was chosen in preference to the indigenous kind, as
it was reputed to hear sooner. So far it hasn’t
borne anything to speak of, though the people who
run the show have borne a load of anxiety, likewise
the heat and burden of the day.
* * ♦ *
“Tiaki”: Our brown brother, Renata Topoi, had
a small piece of land with a small Maori whare on
it. There were only five acres in the section, but
as it adjoined the farm of Peter Collins it was
extremely necessary for easement and roading pur-
poses that Peter should acquire it. Peter offered
more than the market price. Renata, suspecting that
the ground must have a secret value which he
couldn’t fathom, shook his tattooed top-piece and
replied, “Me no wanna sherrim.” He hinted that
sentiment accounted for the refusal. His uncle was
buried there, or his dog, or something. Eventually
a joint inspection of property was arranged, and,
strange to say, a sale was arranged next day at
Peter’s own price. The arbitrator, a base English
creature from Somerset, gave me the key to the
whole thing. He said: “Yew knoa them green liz-
zurds —they burkes like a durg? Theyur wuz wun
uv ’um spitting en’ burkin’ on the dooer step when
ole Rennatur got theyur.” Somerset had been hired
to catch a green lizard and glue him down to the
doorstep, and, when poor old Renata arrived, the
green devil, fearful lest he should be trodden on,
opened his purple mouth and barked like a dog. The
M.L. lizard ( Tiligua Zealandica) does this sort of
thing. He is a beast of ill-omen and is about as
welcome to Henare as a visit from Beelzebub or a
bailiff. Then I understood what Renata meant when
I asked him why he had sold. “Rei aha maku te
whare ngararaf Me hoko,” was his answer, which
means. “Hang the reptile house. Sell it.”
MY CREDITORS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The birds they are my creditors,
And they, each morning, will
Come to my window after crumbs
Each with his little bill.
Yet when I see them on the sills
One thought my pleasure numbs:
There’s always such a lot of bills,
And such a dearth of crumbs.
With envy on my feathered friends
I look. Oh, would that I,
Unheedful of the falling due,
Had feathers left to fly!
Then would I fly from one gross man
Who threatens me with gaol;
Each day he brings his little bill
And nags his weary tale.
My feathered creditors who come
Will sometimes bill and coo;
This bloated monster threatens me
That he will bill and sue.
He makes a burden of my life,
So close he clings to me;
Alas, I am not like a bird
Though I am up a tree 1
N.S.W.

A Natural Remedy
Time was when disease was thought to be due to the
direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’

Elder Man’s Lane.
“I’LL BIDE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS story, or sketch—or whatever it is
—does not really run through Elder
Man’s Lane; and yet it does. It is a
vague sketch of an Old Place back in a
Garden, and a rainy day. At least it is
written on a rainy day. An old place back
in a garden is generally vague, and sad, and
faraway-like, however near, like rain on the
Harbor hills.
Down across from my balcony window are
the mean, brown, rusting roofs of “Skull
Terrace,” and against them —and against
the light green of some willows at the lower
end of the “terrace”—the countless bright
raindrops march steadily down the tele-
graph wires, like tears on the lines of Time,
and every here and there one drops off-
just like a tear. And, down below, again,
in the hollow of Lavender Bay, are other
old places in gardens. And, on sunny morn-
ings I like to lean on some railings (and sit
on the rail of memory, so to speak], and
look down on those old places. When a
man is in the Elder Man’s Lane of Life,
and well past Middle Age Corner —say five
or six years—he often begins to take a sad
sort of comfort in looking at old places back
in gardens. They remind of what has been,
and might have been, or should have
been, and, in many ways, of the best in the
past. The age, and the philosophy that
comes with it, helps you forget that there
is just as likely to be a Family Hell in
an ® ld „ place back in a garden that the “de”
or “le” Somethings have lived in for gene-
rations as there is in the brand new and un-
speakably ugly “villa” built for the Bloggses
at Mosrnan.
But my old place back in a garden was on
a level, and well out beyond Ilidge-street
and round towards Neutral Bay. And I
never saw it, because it had been more com-
pletely wiped out of existence than if it had
been exterminated by fire, bombardment or
earthquake before I even heard of its exist-
ence.
There were two old places in Sydney like
the house of Miss Havisham in "Great Ex-
pectations. One was in Newtown, where
tne fittings ot tiie carriage would come to
pieces between your finger and thumb, in
the rotten-roofed old stable. The woman
lived and died—in her bedroom, the upper
liait or tlie door of which opened so mat
her old servant' could attend to her. I be-
lieve there was a mouldy, dusty, cobwebby
ghost ot a wedding breakfast laid out in the
uining-room, and they said her tragedy had
happened be]ore Dickens wrote Great Ex-
pectations. The other house was in Darling-
liurst. Two sisters lived there, they said,
much alike. Tlie front was shut up, and
the windows battened. They got their neces-
saries through a wicket in the back wall, or
gate. They were never seen by outsiders
until one died. They said that she was the
one that had the money—or got the pension
. that she was buried under the name
ot the other. The other died a long year
later. The baker found his loaves dried
hard, or mouldy, in the box, and the police
broke in and found her—like the bread.
But it was my esteemed acquaintance,
Benno, dealer in dead marines, who first
told me about the old house round towards
Neutral Bay—during an unduly prolonged
voyage from Blue’s Point to Dawes Point
on the Ilors’n’cart Ferry. The subject came
up over an article in that morning’s paper
about another old house—probably the pro-
posed demolition of the Bangers over there
with its famous picture on the wall—that
someone was reading.
"There use ter be a ole place back in a
garden round there above Bidge-street tords
Neutral, Benno told me, “but I haven’t seen
it for years now” (remember Benno is any-
thing from 25 to 05), and Benno described
the exact locality. “I remember it well,” he
said. “The funniest thing about it—or per-
haps it wasn’t funny—was that the back of
it was tords the street. A very old
stone wall, with broken glass in cement on
top, an a door-gate in it, ’n’ two big, old
coach-house gates at the side. They was
choked with dirt and grass grown at the
bottom, and the boards was dry rotted ’n’
vvorn off a bit along the top. I reckoned
they was hardwood. You could see the loft
or the coachhouse above, and a rotting. bar-
ricaded-up door in the gable end. Blimy!
i never, could find tne front of that house.
1 raps it was on the rocks, with steps run-
ning down to the water, ’n’ you could only
get to it by water—or, maybe, on a bit of
old, lorst road. There’s bits of old, lorst
roads at the back of them streets, an’ along
the rocks on the shore. I’ve dropped on one
or two of them. (Didyer ever write about
lorst roads: You might apply it to human
natuie you seem to nave lost yours more
than once.) But I did catch a glimpse of
the back o< tlie house from streets higher up
on the hill, once or twice before I saw it
close. You know the sort I mean —stone
verandah at back, with rooms in the end,
an long, high roof with gable windows—-the
sort that seems to turn its back on advancin’
civilisation n’ mordern progress, ’n’ all that
sort of thing—and bury its elbows, so to
speak, m garden greenery an’ trees run
wild an look—yer can’t tell what its
lookin at. The Blinded Parst, perhaps.
“They said there was two old maids
living in the house, but they never showed
themselves (it’s always that way) The
things were taken in at the gate an’ the
bills paid bv a dried-up old woman, an’ er
little, withered ole cove, sometimes- an’
sometimes a rusty old codger went in.’ that
might have been the old lawver or doctor
Just like one of them faked up English
novels. ® u
“So at last.” Benno went on. “I ma de up
my mind to have a shot at that gate, in the
wav of business, and investigate—’n’ se e if
there was anything cronk about th e place
like a respectable citizen ought. You seei
went in more for old iron, an’ lead, an’ brass,
an’ what-not, in those days, and often picked
up bargains at them sorter places for
nothing. I picked up a little, old har-
monium once, an’ the Old ’Un tinkered it
up and scared all the neighbors’ cats an’
dogs out of their wits with it; an’ nearly
drove the neighbors mad, too—till it busted
up for good. Our cat left, too. You see
the Old Un thought he could play. I never
like to interfere with the ambitions of old
people—Blimy! it’s cruel sad, I think.
“Well—so one day I pulls up outside that
gate an nulls the old bell. In a Hash, it
seemed to me, a trap-door in that gate was
snatched open, and I saw a woman’s face; ’n’
I backed a bit. It was of the handsome
kind of oldish face, but the color had faded
—V seemed to have run a bit, too, under-
neath—yer might know what I mean. A
squarish face with one of them longish chins
that come out, but with fine lines—one of
them chins that are chins. And she had
those blind-like grey eyes; they glared at
me as if I was a ghost from the past, but
she wasn’t frightened. Of course I didn’t
notice all this in a flash, but thought some
of it afterwards. (The sort of chin gnd
eyes that take your attention away from
everything else.)
“ ‘Who are you?’ she said after a minute,
and what do you want?’
“1 told her.
“ ‘What’s your name?’ she said.
"I told her that, too.
“Presently she opened the gate, in a dazed
sort of way, and looked at me.
“‘ls that your name?’ she said, ’n’ I told
her aghin it was.
“‘Well, she said, sharp-like, ‘you’d bet-
ter come in. I can’t stand talking here in
the full glare of the street.’ And so she
stood aside to let me pass, and I walks in
an’ she shuts the gate agen. She stood
with her hand on the bolt, looking at me
sideways ; as if I was something in a shop
that might suit her—say somethin’ in the
furniture or hardware line.
“She was tallish and broad in the shoul-
ders and hips, an’ she stood like —like—
well, something said ‘old opera singer !’ to
me, sudden-like. You see I judge people I
don’t know, by instinct like, from someone
I did —that they remind me strongly of.
Not so old, neither, but old like a woman
of her sort of 30 or so might be, that’s been
worn by trouble —or champagne—or some
sort of divilment.
“ ‘lt’s all right,’ she said presently, ‘you
reminded me at first of someone I knew.
Now, what do you want?’ she said, and I
saw her hand slowly shoot the bolt! ‘lt’s
grand opera now. all right!’ I thought—’an’
in a bloomin’ ole garden, too,’ Only the
garden wasn’t bloomin’ —just a Wild tangle
of briary stuff along the walls, an an ole fig
—’n’ ole fruit trees runnin’ up the sides, an’
a hint of firs in front. The big stone-
flagged back verandah was one of those that
migliter done for the front, with round pil-
lars with mouldin’s top ’n bottom. Columns
I think you call ’em.
“So I tole her again what I wanted. ‘l’m
dealin in bottles, mum,’ I said, standing with
me ole hat in one hand, and the chaff-bag in
the other.
“She give a short laugh, more like a
cough, without smiling, right in my face.
‘Do 1 look like a woman that has bottles to
sell?’ she said.
“I begged her pardon, mum, an’ said I
was after any old iron, or lead, or brass, or
broken furniture that was thrown aside.
Just then another woman came on the veran-
dah and give me a bran’ new start. She was
something like the other, but smaller, and
withered, ’n’ twenty years older, ’n’ bent —•
and, oh! but her face was hard; and she was
hugging herself in a shawl. I thought she
might be the other one’s sister.
“ ‘Why Ellen!’ she squealed, ‘Whatever
are you doing there? And who’s that;
“ ‘Never mind, Emma,’ says my woman.
‘lt’s only a dustman. Old Betty is doubled
up with her rheumatism again, and Bob’s
out, so I answered the gate. You go inside,
Emma, or you’ll catch cold.’ An’ the other
went inside. Then the Emma one turned
to me agen an’ pointed to a boxed-in place
in the end of the stable. (The stable was
very high to the lofts, with great beams, an’
a stone wall at the back.) ‘Go in there’ she
said, ‘an’ see if there’s anything you want.’
“Blimy! but it was a find. There was
all sorts of odds and ends when I shifted the
curtain of cobwebs an’ dust. There was
chairs that must have had the toothache for
twenty years, an’ lookin’ glasses with only
their shoulders out, an’ broken washstands
—you know, all sorts of things you’d find in
a place like that. An’ they was all good, an’
bm of their sort. There was boxes of nails
an’ old chisels, an’ screws, an’ gimlets, an’
screwdrivers, and brass hooks an’ things.
That was a find for my Old Un! It was a
load, an’ I had a good turn out then, an’ I
was empty, fir it was out at the end of my
beat.
“It got darker of a sudden and I noticed
there was a winder for the first time. I
hadn’t seen it before because of the cobweb
curtains and dust ‘frosting.’ She was stand-
ing in the doorway, and filled it, and, blimy!
She give me another jump. ‘Don’t go poking
round,’ she said. ‘Clear it all out if you
want it.’
“Blimy! I took her at her word ! There
was a heavy, shallow, hardwood box in the
road, lined with thick lead—an’ old sink of
some kind—an’ I dragged that out first, ’n’
then some brass rods, an’ the ironwork of a
bed, for the bottom of the cart. There was
any quantity of bottles stacked in a corner.
There was an old underground cistern in the
yard, with the brickwork about two foot
above the surface and covered with hard-
wood, worn an’ black with age, ’n’ a old
pump in the middle that might a done for
Uord Nelson’s Victory, an’ she sat herself
down on the edge of this. She sat wide, this
woman, with her elbows on her knees an’ her
chin on her palms, and seemed to watch me,
in a way, carrying the things out.
“There was two ole. tall, oblong, shnllcr
clocks on a dusty shelf in the corner: one
hwl the works in, an’ a picture of Edin-
burgh, or some place, on the glass door, but
'he other was empty an’ had no glass—but
T found the works on the end of the shelf.
r, 'hey’d have stooped for the cobwebs ennyhow.
They was twins—and old uns at that. I
thought they’d be just the thing for my Old
Un to tinker with.- Blimy!

bottle of tonic.
“ ‘D’ yer want them clocks?’ I said to
her, more for the sake of something to say.
“ ‘What clocks?’ she said.
“I showed her one, and she give a sort of
jerk an’ looked at it curiously.
“ ‘I told you to take everything,’ she said.
‘But what do you want them for? They’ll
never go again.’ She said that as if she
was remembering the time when they did go.
“So I told her about the Old Un. and I
said that he'd make ’em go; but I didn’t
know about keepin’ time. But she didn’t
seem to be listening, so I carried them out.
She never moved from her position on the
tank top.
“When I come in agen she was singing
to herself, ‘The Irish Emigrant.’ I heard
some of it: —
And the red was in your cheek, Mary,
An’ the love light in your eye.
“She sung it bitter-like, nasty-like—l didn’t
like it at all. Especially as she got up
sudden, with that laugh and stage fling
of hers. ‘Wait a minute,’ she said, and she
went in, an’ presently she comes out with a
big cup o’ tea (breakfast cups I think they
call ’em) an’ a plate of bread and butter
on a tray ; an’ she set it down on the top of
the tank. ‘There you are,’ she said. ‘I
know you don’t drink.’
“Now how did she know that.
“ ‘l’ll keep an eye to your horse,’ she
said; an’ went ’n’ set the gate a little ajar.
“Well, that woman mesmerised me. I
know how to handle cups and saucers, an’
bread an’ butter in company, without bother-
in' about it; but how I know I never could
tell —an’ she watched me all the time. I
told her all about the Old Un—and the Lord
knows what else beside —without her seemin’
to ask me. But she did ask me if I had
an uncle (how’d she know I didn’t have
ten?). So I told her all I knew about
Uncle Alf, that was a lot younger than the
Old Un. He went away through the dig-
gings to West Australia an’ South Africa,
an’ the Lord knows where, an’ dropped
clear out o’ sight.
“Every now an’ agen she’d go to the gate
an’ stand with her hand on the stonework,
looking out. Just like some bloomin’ scene
on the stage. At last she said, sudden,
‘You’ll be more like your uncle than your
father!’
“ ‘Now, howin’ell did she know that?
’n’ why did she say it?’ (Uncle Alf had been
educated, though, so he wasn’t like me in
that.)
“We both seemed to know when it was
time to part, so I got up, an’ she opened the
gate.
“‘Now, what about the price?’ I asked
her.
“ ‘What price?’ she said.
“ ‘For the things, mum,’ I said. ‘I want
to pay yer. I’m a dealer.’
“She laughed that laugh of hers agen.
‘Never mind that,’ she said. ‘I was paid
long ago—’n’ paid the price too. Here, take
this for your Old Un to treat himself with.’
An’ she shoves a sovereign inter me hand.
‘Now go,’ she said. ‘Good-bye! Don’t come
again.’ An’ she shoves me out and shuts
the gate, and I stood staring at the blind
side of it. Then there was a big policeman
at my elbow, like they happen. ‘What doin?’
he said, an’ I told him. ‘Well, I’m blowed!’
he said. ‘You’re the first one I saw go in
there. Did ye see her?’ I said I did, an’
he lifted his helmet ’n’ scratched his head
’n’ said he was blowed agen, an’ marched off
slow.
“I stared at that gate again. There’d
been a slanting shower in the morning, and
wetted it, an’ then the sun had brought some
black lettering out plainer, I suppose, on the
damp wood in the perished grey paint. It
was the name of the house, ‘l’ll Bide.’
“ ‘l’ll Wait,’ yer know—like some old song
that used to run: ‘l’ll bide; I’ll bide my
time.’ But perhaps she only meant she’d
wait —without any revenge in it.
“But,” concluded Benno reflectively,
“howin’ell did she know that the Old Un
took his drop of shicker?”
* * * *
That was Benno’s storv. This is mine.
Benno’s Old Place Back in a Garden
haunted me in spare moments; so, one sad
but sunny afternoon in early winter, and
during a sober period, I (John Lawrence)
took a run out that way. On the ferry I
sat beside a man I took to be an old digger—
and successful at that; and we got into con-
versation. He’d been in Westralia, after
Coolgardie, where I had been —also Papua,
South Africa, Klondyke and South America.
Presently I could see he’d had a few and
wanted somebody to talk to. He was burst-
ing with health and philanthropy and life
and good nature and success and love for
dear old Australia, and champagne last
night, and nips this morning. And he’d
just got back after many years and was
staying at the Metropole.
There was something about him that re-
minded me of someone long ago—or lately,
if you understand; but, when he lifted his
hat to rub his head in growing enthusiasm,
I saw what my memory was striving at. His
eyes were grey with haunting memories
hiding, and a hint of a glint in them; and
his hair, iron grey, stood up round the
front like a well-made brush. Greying hair
makes little change in such faces. It was
Benno, taller and older—or Benno’s roving
Uncle Alf. And Benno’s manner began to
come out strongly. But still there was
something that reminded me of someone,
long ago and lately, who was not like Benno.
And now, I know, it was myself. Not a
physical double —that would be abhorrent to
me: I’d hate him. And a double all round!
He’d give me the horrors; he’d be the
horrors himself. But this was different—a
sort of soul or life double.
“And now I’m going over to look up some
dear old friends of mine on the Shore, that
I haven’t heard of for years,” said the
digger. “Fifteen years—fifteen year”— his
voice seemed to lose a little of its confidence.
“Maybe her letter went wrong at last—or
mebbe mine,” he went on, as to himself.
“Putting off writing; putting off writing.
Waiting for good or better news to send.
New friends —and —newer friends—and so
the years go Putting off writing! Never
you do that, young man!” He spoke to me
then as if I were a young man. It takes us
a long while to forget we’re not —the years
steal by so carefully, no matter how careless
or reckless we are. “You’re quite a young
man yet!” they say.
“Do you know where to find your friends,”
I asked.
We’d landed and got on the same tram
(and I hadn’t thought that strange), and
were passing ltidge-street.
“Know where to find them!” he said,
rather loudly, “Sure —of course I do. They
weren’t of the shifting sort. And my
friend —well she was a woman who could
wait.” He added this in a lower tone.
A few stopping places further on I pre-
pared to get out, where Benno had told me
—near the place where I’d find that old
house back in a garden.
“Well, so long!” I said. “I get out hero.
I hope you’ll find your friends all right,”
and I held out my hand. But he got up.
“I get out here too,” he said. “The lane I
want is somewhere about here.”
He stood on the kerb looking round in a
dazed way.
“I know it’s somewhere abofit here,” he
said. “By the park back there* —but the
place is changed. God! how it is changed.
I don’t know any of these new streets.”
Then his eyes lighted. “I’m on the right
track, anyhow,” he said. “There’s that little
old pub. / remember it.” There was a
little old stone inn with a figtree up the
road on the other side.
“What sort of a place are you looking
for?” I asked.
“I’m looking,” he said, “for an old place
back in a garden.”
I caught my tongue in time.
“Come on,” he said, “and have a nip
before we part. I want one.” He did, by
the look of him. And I wanted one too, on
general principles.
The landlord was one of those little, fat,
round-headed, grey, old boys. “Shall we ask
him?” I asked my digger acquaintance, after
nips. “No!” he said hurriedly. “Come
along! I knew him,” he said, when we got
outside. “But he didn’t recognise me.”
“I’ll see you to the gate,” I said. He
made no objection, and we went on. Up on
the other side of the street was an elbow of
new, mean, two-storeyed brick corner shops,
with an allotment alongside and a couple of
prematurely aged cottages going up.
“It will be somewhere round above those
shops,” he said. We went round and down.
He said there ought to be a lane along
above the rocks down there, but there wasn’t
—only a new, broad sandstone street with a
little gully filled in, and a retaining wall on
the water frontage ; and a row of new brick
double cottages facing it along the back of
the shops’ allotment. And between them
and the shop frontage nothing but bare
ground, and a builder’s litter, a rough iron
contractor’s shed, some cement barrels, and
the stumps of one or two big old ornamental
trees. And the choking breath and lip-
cracking taste of new lime.
“What tricks our memory does play us !”
said the digger. “I never had the bump of
locality, anyhow. Get lost if I went down a
gully. It must be up round the next damned
new block.”
“We’ll ask at the shops first,” I said.
There was a grocer’s shop and a green-
grocer’s, of course —and, of course a mill-
inery. The milliner was a young, married
Juno (and I wondered how she made ends
meet in that locality). I asked her first,
because of her appearance, and we saw her
first at the door —calling a kid. But she
never heard of any old place back in a gar-
den ; she’d only come there lately. So we
went on up the street.
I caught a boy. He took us across the
road to get a full view. “Do yer see them
there noo shops ’n’ cottages on them allot-
ments there?” he said.
We did.
“Well, then, on that piece er land where
them buildin’s stand there was a old place
back in a garden. It was pulled down last
year ’n’ carted away, ’n’ the big ole fig tree’s
cut down ’n’ carted away too—’n’ the land
cut up inter allotments. There was two ole
maids,” he shrilled, “lived there alone for
hever so long. Disserpointment in love,
they said. They called their house ‘l’ll
Bide,’ so they muster been ratty. One
died years ergo, the oldest, Mother says; en
after a year of it alone the other come out of
her hole en went to the dorgs—shickered, or
drownded or poisoned herself, I don’t know
which. The d’s came to a harnt of mine to
help ’dentify her” —
“Clear out!” I said, and I threw the
young devil a shilling. I turned to my
digger acquaintance, and his face wa« dead.
I took him by the arm and steered him to-
wards the tramway stopping place. He
came to life a little. “Wait here for the
trams,” he said, reading it on the post.
“Wait here for the trams.”
“I’ll see you into one,” I said.
“No,” he said. “Leave me—l want to
think. Thank you! Good-bye!”
But I watched him from a little further
up and saw him making over for the pub.
Myself again!
I went on a mile or two farther out. feel-
ing like one who had left himself behind in
a way, to look up another but a much
smaller old place back in a garden, where
someone once dear to me had been born; but
had not died ; and on my Avay back I called
in at that little Figtree Inn again.
My digger was there, drinking and shout-
ing for all hands. (“A hangel dropped out
of a hairio-plane,” said one of the bummers
to me.) The digger was telling wild stories
of Klondyke, and Johannesburg, and Papua,
and South America. He’d renewed ac-
quaintance with the landlord. He grabbed
me and made me drink. I got him aside.
“Best come across home,” I Said.
“I’ve got no home —I’m home!” he said.
“I’ll send for my luggage in the morning.
I’m staying here, and —and, damn you all!
I’ll bide!”
I got him into the parlor and he called in
more drinks there.
“It’s no use,” he said, in a quietened tone,
but with drunken obstinacy. “I must stay.
It was a lie. She was'tt’t one of the sort to
go to the dogs. She wasn’t one of the
drowning or poisoping sort. She’ll wait,
I told you. She’s about here somewhere —
she’ll come back! I’m going to buy that
ground and pull down those (hie!) blasted
shops, and—and (hie!) build again. I’ll
Bide!”
HENRY LAWSON.
